The Definition and Three Divisions of Hukm
Sanusi defines hukm and divides it into three types, then defines shar‘ī as divine discourse involving demand, permission, or stipulation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general definition of hukm as a logical judgment and its three main categories: religious, customary, and rational, as well as the definition of shar‘ī hukm as divine discourse.

Core Idea
Hukm is a logical judgment before it is a legal one.

Sanusi begins his introduction by clarifying what a 'ruling' (ḥukm) means in kalam discourse. It is not merely a legal verdict but a broader logical category: the affirmation or negation of something. He then divides this into three types: religious, customary, and rational. This lesson unpacks that foundational distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of hukm
Read the Arabic text and translation for the definition of hukm. Notice that it is a logical definition (affirmation or negation), not a legal one.
2
Identify the three divisions
Find the three categories: shar‘ī, ‘ādī, and ‘aqlī. Understand that these are broad types of judgments.
The passage only explains shar‘ī in detail.
3
Understand the definition of shar‘ī
Read the definition of shar‘ī as Allah's discourse related to actions of the morally responsible, either by demand, permission, or stipulation.
This sets the foundation for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الأولى : في الأحكام. الْحُكْمُ : إِثْبَاتُ أَمْرٍ أَوْ نَفْيُهُ. وَيَنْقَسِمُ إِلَىٰ ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامٍ : شَرْعِيٌّ ، وَعَادِيٌّ ، وَعَقْلِيٌّ. فَالشَّرْعِيُّ : خِطَابُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى الْمُتَعَلِّقُ بِأَفْعَالِ الْمُكَلَّفِينَ بِالطَّلَبِ أَوِ الإِبَاحَةِ ، أَوِ الْوَضْعِ لَهُمَا. وَيَدْخُلُ فِي الطَّلَبِ أَرْبَعَةٌ : الإِيجَابُ ، وَالنَّدْبُ ، وَالتَّحْرِيمُ ، وَالْكَرَاهَةُ. فَالإِيجَابُ : وَهُوَ طَلَبُ الْفِعْلِ طَلَبًا جَازِمًا ؛ كَالإِيمَانِ بِاللَّهِ وَرُسُلِهِ ، وَكَقَوَاعِدِ الْإِسْلَامِ الْخَمْسِ. وَالنَّدْبُ : وَهُوَ طَلَبُ الْفِعْلِ طَلَبًا غَيْرَ جَازِمٍ ؛ كَصَلَاةِ الْفَجْرِ وَنَحْوِهَا. وَالتَّحْرِيمُ : وَهُوَ طَلَبُ الْكَفِّ عَنِ الْفِعْلِ طَلَبًا جَازِمًا ؛ كَشُرْبِ الْخَمْرِ وَالزِّنَا وَنَحْوِهِمَا. وَالْكَرَاهَةُ : وَهِيَ طَلَبُ الْكَفِّ عَنِ الْفِعْلِ طَلَبًا غَيْرَ
The First Introduction: On Rulings. The judgment (al-ḥukm) is the affirmation or negation of something. And it is divided into three divisions: religious (shar‘ī), customary (‘ādī), and rational (‘aqlī). The religious is the discourse of Allah Most High that pertains to the actions of the legally responsible persons by way of demand (ṭalab) or permission (ibāḥa), or stipulation (waḍ‘) for both. And under demand (al-ṭalab) come four [types]: obligation (al-ījāb), recommendation (al-nadb), prohibition (al-taḥrīm), and disapprobation (al-karāha). So obligation: it is the demand to do an act with decisiveness; like faith in Allah and His messengers, and like the five pillars of Islam. And recommendation: it is the demand to do an act without decisiveness; like the dawn prayer and the like. And prohibition: it is the demand to refrain from an act with decisiveness; like drinking wine and fornication and the like. And disapprobation: it is the demand to refrain from an act without
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage from the first introduction on rulings (al-aḥkām). The author, Sanusi, begins by defining the judgment (al-ḥukm) as the affirmation or negation of something. He then divides judgment into three categories: religious (shar‘ī), customary (‘ādī), and rational (‘aqlī). He proceeds to define the religious judgment as the discourse of Allah that pertains to the actions of the legally responsible persons, and it is either by way of demand (ṭalab), permission (ibāḥa), or stipulation (waḍ‘). He then focuses on the category of demand, which comprises four types: obligation (ījāb), recommendation (nadb), prohibition (taḥrīm), and disapprobation (karāha). Each is defined with examples: obligation is a decisive demand to perform an act, exemplified by faith and the five pillars; recommendation is a non-decisive demand to perform an act, like the dawn prayer; prohibition is a decisive demand to refrain, like drinking wine and fornication; and disapprobation is a non-decisive demand to refrain, though the passage cuts off before completing the example for this last category. This classification is foundational for Islamic legal theory and kalam discussions on divine commands.

Key Terms
حكم
شرعي
عادي
عقلي
طلب
إباحة
وضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hukm always refers to a legal ruling from the Quran or Sunnah.
Correction: In kalam, hukm is first defined logically as affirmation or negation, and only one branch is religious. This broader meaning is foundational.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, how many divisions of hukm are there?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Hukm in kalam is a logical judgment of affirmation or negation, with three branches: religious, customary, and rational. The religious branch is divine discourse involving demand, permission, or stipulation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that 'hukm' is a logical judgment before it is a legal one. How does this reshape your understanding of Islamic rulings? Do you often rush to ask 'is it haram or halal?' without first asking 'is this rationally necessary, possible, or impossible?' What would change if you started with the rational judgment? Commit to doing that for one decision this week.
?
The religious hukm is defined as Allah's discourse involving demand, permission, or stipulation. When you encounter an ayat or hadith, do you truly receive it as a personal demand from Allah to you, or do you treat it as background knowledge? Pick one specific demand that you have been ignoring. Write it down and set a concrete action to fulfill it today.
?
Sanusi distinguishes three types of hukm: religious, customary, and rational. Think about a habit or practice in your life that you follow because 'that's how it's always done' (customary). How can you examine whether it aligns with the religious and rational judgments? What is one custom you need to re-evaluate in light of this lesson?

The Four Types of Demand in Shar‘ī Hukm
Sanusi enumerates the four types of demand within shar‘ī hukm and defines each with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of divine demand (ṭalab) in Islamic law: obligation, recommendation, prohibition, and disapprobation, and the distinction between decisive and non-decisive demands.

Core Idea
Divine commands come in four forms, each defined by decisiveness and direction.

In this passage, Sanusi focuses on one aspect of the shar‘ī judgment: the category of demand (ṭalab). He lists four types of demand: obligation, recommendation, prohibition, and disapprobation. Each is defined by whether the demand is decisive or not, and whether it is to perform or refrain. This lesson examines each type with its examples, noting that the text is incomplete at the end.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types
Read the passage and find the four names: ījāb, nadb, taḥrīm, karāha.
2
Understand the two variables
Notice that each type combines two features: demand to do or to refrain, and decisive or non-decisive. Make a mental grid.
This will help you remember the categories.
3
Study the examples
Read the examples for obligation (faith, five pillars), recommendation (dawn prayer), and prohibition (wine, fornication). Note that the example for karāha is missing due to the cut-off.
4
Note the incomplete text
The passage cuts off during the definition of karāha. From the pattern, it is a non-decisive demand to refrain.
This is a minor gap; the concept is clear from the established pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الأولى : في الأحكام. الْحُكْمُ : إِثْبَاتُ أَمْرٍ أَوْ نَفْيُهُ. وَيَنْقَسِمُ إِلَىٰ ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامٍ : شَرْعِيٌّ ، وَعَادِيٌّ ، وَعَقْلِيٌّ. فَالشَّرْعِيُّ : خِطَابُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى الْمُتَعَلِّقُ بِأَفْعَالِ الْمُكَلَّفِينَ بِالطَّلَبِ أَوِ الإِبَاحَةِ ، أَوِ الْوَضْعِ لَهُمَا. وَيَدْخُلُ فِي الطَّلَبِ أَرْبَعَةٌ : الإِيجَابُ ، وَالنَّدْبُ ، وَالتَّحْرِيمُ ، وَالْكَرَاهَةُ. فَالإِيجَابُ : وَهُوَ طَلَبُ الْفِعْلِ طَلَبًا جَازِمًا ؛ كَالإِيمَانِ بِاللَّهِ وَرُسُلِهِ ، وَكَقَوَاعِدِ الْإِسْلَامِ الْخَمْسِ. وَالنَّدْبُ : وَهُوَ طَلَبُ الْفِعْلِ طَلَبًا غَيْرَ جَازِمٍ ؛ كَصَلَاةِ الْفَجْرِ وَنَحْوِهَا. وَالتَّحْرِيمُ : وَهُوَ طَلَبُ الْكَفِّ عَنِ الْفِعْلِ طَلَبًا جَازِمًا ؛ كَشُرْبِ الْخَمْرِ وَالزِّنَا وَنَحْوِهِمَا. وَالْكَرَاهَةُ : وَهِيَ طَلَبُ الْكَفِّ عَنِ الْفِعْلِ طَلَبًا غَيْرَ
The First Introduction: On Rulings. The judgment (al-ḥukm) is the affirmation or negation of something. And it is divided into three divisions: religious (shar‘ī), customary (‘ādī), and rational (‘aqlī). The religious is the discourse of Allah Most High that pertains to the actions of the legally responsible persons by way of demand (ṭalab) or permission (ibāḥa), or stipulation (waḍ‘) for both. And under demand (al-ṭalab) come four [types]: obligation (al-ījāb), recommendation (al-nadb), prohibition (al-taḥrīm), and disapprobation (al-karāha). So obligation: it is the demand to do an act with decisiveness; like faith in Allah and His messengers, and like the five pillars of Islam. And recommendation: it is the demand to do an act without decisiveness; like the dawn prayer and the like. And prohibition: it is the demand to refrain from an act with decisiveness; like drinking wine and fornication and the like. And disapprobation: it is the demand to refrain from an act without
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage from the first introduction on rulings (al-aḥkām). The author, Sanusi, begins by defining the judgment (al-ḥukm) as the affirmation or negation of something. He then divides judgment into three categories: religious (shar‘ī), customary (‘ādī), and rational (‘aqlī). This classification is foundational for Islamic legal theory and kalam discussions on divine commands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine demands are equally binding and decisive.
Correction: Sanusi distinguishes between decisive (jāzim) and non-decisive (ghayr jāzim) demands, leading to four categories with different levels of obligation.

Checkpoint
How many types of demand (ṭalab) are there in Sanusi's classification?
A) Two
B) Three

D) Five

Key Takeaway
Divine demand is divided into four categories based on whether it commands an act or a refrain, and whether it is decisive or non-decisive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a sunnah act, do you genuinely feel that Allah is asking you to do it, even though it is not obligatory? If not, how can you cultivate that awareness starting today?
?
Is there a disliked action in your daily life that you continue doing despite knowing it is a non-decisive demand to refrain? What would change if you took that demand seriously as a command from your Lord?
?
If you truly believed that every demand—whether decisive or not—is a call from Allah, would your attitude toward religious obligations and recommendations shift? What is one step you can take today to honor that belief?

Definition of Ibahah (Permissibility) as a Shari' Ruling
Sanusi defines ibahah as one of the five shari' rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of ibahah (permissibility) as one of the five shari' rulings, including its key components and examples.

Core Idea
Ibahah means both action and omission are equally permitted without preference.

Sanusi now turns to the fifth of the five shari‘ rulings: ibahah (permissibility). This ruling is distinct because it grants equal permission for both performing and leaving an act, with no preference for either. The author provides a clear definition and examples from everyday life like marriage and sale.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the key phrase 'without preference' (min ghayri tarjih).
2
Identify the key components
The definition consists of three parts: permission (idhn), from the Lawgiver (al-shar‘), for both action and omission (fi al-fi‘l wa al-tark), and the absence of preference.
This distinguishes ibahah from other rulings.
3
Consider the examples
Sanusi gives marriage and sale as examples. Why are these considered permissible? Reflect on how they fit the definition.
These acts are neutral in themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْإِبَاحَةُ : فَهِيَ إِذْنُ الشَّرْعِ فِي الْفِعْلِ وَالتَّرْكِ مَعاً مِنْ غَيْرِ تَرْجِيحٍ لِأَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى الْآخَرِ ؛ كَالنِّكَاحِ وَالْبَيْعِ وَنَحْوِهِما .
As for permissibility (al-ibahah): it is the permission of the Law (al-shar‘) for both the action and the leaving together, without preference of one over the other; like marriage (al-nikah) and sale (al-bay‘) and similar things.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth of the five shari' rulings: ibahah (permissibility). The author states that ibahah is the permission (idhn) from the Lawgiver (al-shar‘) for a person to either perform an action or refrain from it, with neither option being given preference (tarjih) over the other. It is a neutral choice. The examples given are marriage and sale, which are permissible acts in themselves, though conditions may apply. This definition distinguishes ibahah from other rulings, such as obligation (wujub) where action is preferred, or prohibition (tahrim) where refraining is preferred.

Key Terms
إِبَاحَة
إِذْن
الشَّرْع
فِعْل
تَرْك
تَرْجِيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think ibahah means the act is always better to do.
Correction: Ibahah implies equal permissibility of action and omission, with no preference for either.

Checkpoint
What is the defining characteristic of ibahah (permissibility) according to the passage?
A) The action is recommended but not required.
B) Both performing and omitting the action are equally permitted without preference.
C) The action is forbidden but with a dispensation.
D) The action is required for some people but not others.

Key Takeaway
Ibahah is the neutral permission from the Lawgiver to do or not do an act, with no preference for either.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a permissible action in your daily life—like eating a particular food or choosing a leisure activity. How does knowing that this action is not just 'allowed' but a specific permission from Allah (ibahah) change your awareness of His authority over every detail of your life? Will you now pause to thank Him for such permissions or take them for granted?
?
The lesson defines ibahah as a neutral choice with no preference. Are there permissible things you do excessively or neglect entirely? What would it mean for you to intentionally exercise both action and omission in such matters purely out of obedience to the ruling of ibahah? Commit to one specific change.
?
If ibahah is a divine permission, then even your neutral choices are acts of submission when done with the right intention. Which of your 'neutral' habits will you consciously attach to the intention of following Allah’s permission starting today?

The Three Wad'i Rulings: Cause, Condition, Preventer
Sanusi classifies wad'i rulings into cause, condition, and preventer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of wad'i rulings — cause, condition, and preventer — and how each relates to the existence or absence of a ruling.

Core Idea
Wad'i rulings are signs set by Allah indicating how the five rulings apply.

Before we study the five rulings themselves, we need to understand the wad'i ('positional') rulings — the indicators that the Lawgiver has placed to connect us to those rulings. In this passage, Imam Sanusi defines the three types of wad'i rulings: cause, condition, and preventer. Understanding these will help you see how external factors either necessitate, condition, or block a ruling without being the ruling itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three terms: sabab, shart, mani'. They are the three types of wad'i rulings.
The passage begins by defining wad'i rulings as indications set by the Lawgiver.
2
Understand each definition
Break down the definition for each: cause (exists→ruling exists; absent→ruling absent), condition (absent→ruling absent; exists→nothing), preventer (exists→ruling absent; absent→nothing). Pay attention to the phrase 'li-dhatihi' (by itself) which emphasizes the intrinsic relationship.
Cause and condition are often confused; note the asymmetry: cause entails both sides, condition only one.
3
Apply the examples
Review the examples given: sun's zenith for cause, completion of year for condition, menstruation for preventer. See how they fit the definitions.
These examples are from fiqh; they illustrate the logical relationships.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْوَضْعُ لَهُمَا : فَهُوَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ نَصْبِ الشَّارِعِ أَمَارَةٌ عَلَى حُكْمِ مِنْ تِلْكَ الْأَحْكَامِ الْخَمْسَةِ وَهِيَ : السَّبَبُ ، وَالشَّرْطُ ، وَالْمَانِعُ . فَالسَّبَبُ : مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ الْوُجُودُ ، وَمِنْ عَدَمِهِ الْعَدَمُ لِذَاتِهِ : كَزَوَالِ الشَّمْسِ لِوُجُوبِ الظُّهْرِ مَثَلاً . وَالشَّرْطُ : مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ عَدَمِهِ الْعَدَمُ ، وَلا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ وُجُودٌ وَلا عَدَمٌ لِذَاتِهِ ؛ كَتَمَامِ الْحَوْلِ لِوُجُوبِ الزَّكَاة . وَالْمَانِعُ : مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ الْعَدَمُ ، وَلا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ عَدَمِهِ وُجُودٌ وَلا عَدَمُ لِذَاتِهِ : كَالْحَيْضِ لِوُجُوبِ الصَّلاةِ
As for the positional (al-wad‘) for them: it is an expression of the Lawgiver's setting up an indication (amarah) of one of those five rulings. They are: the cause (al-sabab), the condition (al-shart), and the preventer (al-mani‘). The cause: that from whose existence the existence (of the ruling) necessarily follows, and from whose absence the absence (of the ruling) follows by itself (li-dhatihi); like the setting of the sun for the obligation of noon prayer. The condition: that from whose absence the absence (of the ruling) necessarily follows, but from whose existence neither existence nor absence follows by itself; like the completion of the year for the obligation of zakat. The preventer: that from whose existence the absence (of the ruling) necessarily follows, but from whose absence neither existence nor absence follows by itself; like menstruation for the obligation of prayer.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines the positional rulings (al-wad‘), which are signs set by the Lawgiver that indicate one of the five shari' rulings. It then enumerates three subcategories. (1) Cause (sabab): something whose existence directly entails the existence of the ruling, and whose absence directly entails the absence of the ruling, by its own essence. Example: the sun's zenith (zawal al-shams) is the cause for the obligation of the noon prayer. (2) Condition (shart): something whose absence entails the absence of the ruling, but whose existence does not necessarily entail the existence or absence of the ruling. Example: the completion of one lunar year (hawl) is a condition for the obligation of zakat; if the year is incomplete, no zakat, but if it is complete, zakat may still not be obligatory if other conditions are not met. (3) Preventer (mani‘): something whose existence entails the absence of the ruling, but whose absence does not entail the existence or absence of the ruling. Example: menstruation (hayd) prevents the obligation of prayer; if menstruation is present, prayer is not obligatory, but if it is absent, prayer may still not be obligatory for other reasons. This classification helps distinguish different ways in which external factors relate to the rulings.

Key Terms
وَضْع
سَبَب
شَرْط
مَانِع
لِذَاتِهِ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that cause and condition are the same, but they differ in that a cause's presence necessarily brings the ruling, while a condition's presence does not.
Correction: A cause (sabab) directly entails both existence and absence of the ruling, whereas a condition (shart) only entails absence when absent. The presence of a condition does not guarantee the ruling.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is correctly described as a 'cause' (sabab)?
A) Menstruation prevents prayer; when absent, prayer is not necessarily obligatory.
B) The setting of the sun makes the noon prayer obligatory; without it, the prayer is not due.
C) Completion of the year is necessary for zakat, but does not make it obligatory.
D) Both A and C are correct.

Key Takeaway
The three wad'i rulings — cause, condition, and preventer — are distinct ways in which external factors indicate the presence or absence of a ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a religious obligation you find difficult to fulfill. What is its cause according to the shari'ah? Is that cause present in your life right now? If so, what conditions might you be neglecting, and what preventers are you using as excuses? Be honest — are those preventers genuine according to this classification, or are you simply avoiding the duty? Commit to removing one false preventer today.
?
Consider a condition you frequently ignore, like completing a full year before paying zakat or being in a state of purity for prayer. How does knowing that a condition's absence necessarily removes the ruling, but its presence does not guarantee it, change your view of your own accountability? Will you start treating conditions as serious limits set by Allah, not optional suggestions?
?
The preventer (mani') like menstruation excuses a woman from prayer, but its removal does not automatically obligate her. Where in your life do you use the removal of a preventer as an excuse to assume you are now fully obligated, when perhaps other conditions are missing? What will you do to check all three factors before concluding your duty?

The Customary Ruling: Definition and Four Divisions
Sanusi defines the customary ruling and lists its four divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the customary ruling and its four logical divisions based on existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Customary rulings describe habitual correlations, not necessary causes.

Sanusi now turns from rational judgments to customary ones—the second major category of ruling. Unlike rational judgments, customary rulings are based on repeated observation and allow for exceptions. This passage defines the customary ruling and lays out its four types, which cover all possible connections between existence and non-existence of two things.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic definition of the customary ruling. Notice the key elements: it affirms a connection, based on repetition, with possibility of exception and no actual causal influence.
The definition is compact; take your time to parse each condition.
2
Understand the four types
The passage then lists four types of connections. Identify each type: existence to existence, non-existence to non-existence, existence to non-existence, non-existence to existence. The example of eating and satiety/hunger illustrates each.
3
Reflect on why these are not causal
Consider why Sanusi emphasizes that there is no actual effect of one upon the other. This distinguishes customary from rational judgments, where causality is necessary. Customary relations are merely habitual correlations observed by repetition.
This distinction is crucial for understanding divine causality in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْحُكْمُ الْعَادِيُّ : فَهُوَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ إِثْبَاتِ الرَّبْطِ بَيْنَ أَمْرِ وَأَمْرٍ وُجُوداً أَوْ عَدَماً بِوَاسِطَةِ التَّكَرُرِ ، مَعَ صِحَّةِ التَّخَلُّفِ وَعَدَمِ تَأْثِيرِ أَحَدِهِمَا فِي الْآخَرِ الْبَتَّةَ . وَأَقْسَامُهُ أَرْبَعَةٌ : رَبْطُ وُجُودٍ بِوُجُودٍ : كَرَبْطِ وُجُودِ الشَّبَعِ بِوُجُودِ الْأَكْلِ · - وَرَبْطُ عَدَمٍ بِعَدَمٍ : كَرَبْطِ عَدَمِ الشَّبَعِ بِعَدَمِ الْأَكْلِ وَرَبْطُ وُجُودٍ بِعَدَمٍ : كَرَبْطِ وُجُودِ الْجُوعِ بِعَدَمِ الْأَكْلِ - وَرَبْطُ عَدَمٍ بِوُجُودٍ : كَرَبْطِ عَدَمِ الْجُوعِ بِوُجُودِ الْأَكْلِ
As for the customary ruling (al-hukm al-‘adi): it is an expression of affirming a connection (al-rabṭ) between one thing and another, in existence or non-existence, by means of repetition, while the possibility of exception remains and with no effect of one upon the other at all. Its divisions are four: connection of existence to existence: like the connection of the existence of satiety to the existence of eating. And connection of non-existence to non-existence: like the connection of the non-existence of satiety to the non-existence of eating. And connection of existence to non-existence: like the connection of the existence of hunger to the non-existence of eating. And connection of non-existence to existence: like the connection of the non-existence of hunger to the existence of eating.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines the customary ruling (al-hukm al-‘adi), which is a judgment about habitual correlations between events based on repeated observation, not on any real causal influence. The author specifies that such correlations are established by repetition (takarur), they allow for exceptions (sihhat al-takhalluf), and there is no actual effect (ta'thir) of one thing upon the other. Then the author enumerates four possible types of such connections: (1) existence linked to existence (e.g., satiety follows eating); (2) non-existence linked to non-existence (e.g., lack of satiety follows lack of eating); (3) existence linked to non-existence (e.g., hunger occurs when eating is absent); (4) non-existence linked to existence (e.g., absence of hunger when eating occurs). These categories cover all logical possibilities and are used to explain natural regularities without implying necessary causation, which is reserved for God's action.

Key Terms
حُكْم عَادِيّ
رَبْط
وُجُود
عَدَم
تَكَرُر
تَخَلُّف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that customary rulings imply necessary causation, like rational judgments.
Correction: Customary rulings are based on habit and allow exceptions; they do not imply that one thing must cause another. True causation is only from Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for establishing a customary ruling?
A) Logical necessity.
B) Divine command.
C) Repeated observation while allowing exceptions.
D) The intrinsic nature of things.

Key Takeaway
Customary rulings are empirical generalizations that describe regularities without implying causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a regular outcome (like your phone working when you press a button), do you automatically assume it must happen every time? How does this lesson challenge your reliance on such habits?
?
Can you identify a 'customary ruling' in your daily life that you have mistaken for a necessary cause? What would it mean to hold that connection loosely, knowing that only God is the true agent?
?
If you truly believed that the regularities of nature are mere habits established by God and can be broken at any moment, would your sense of security change? How could this belief deepen your reliance on Allah alone?

The First Origin of Disbelief: Essential Attribution
Sanusi defines the first of seven origins of kufr and bid'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what Sanusi identifies as the first origin of kufr and bid'ah and why it is considered a dangerous belief.

Core Idea
Essential attribution—the belief that all beings are directly attributed to God—is the first origin of disbelief.

Sanusi begins his enumeration of the seven origins of disbelief and innovation by naming the first: essential necessity or essential attribution. This idea, while seemingly pious, can lead to denying God's free will and willful creation. In this lesson, you will learn its definition and why it is considered a dangerous belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Understand the definition
Focus on the definition of 'al-ijab al-dhati' given: 'it is the attribution of all beings to Allah Almighty.' Ask yourself what this means in the context of kalam.
3
Consider why it is problematic
Reflect on why attributing all beings directly to God might be considered an origin of disbelief. The commentary will help you connect it to the issue of God's free will.
This connects to the broader theme of usul al-kufr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الرابعة : في أصول الكفر والبدع . وَأُصُولُ الْكُفْرِ وَالْبِدَعِ سَبْعَةٌ : الإِيجَابُ الذَّاتِيُّ : وَهُوَ إِسْنَادُ الْكَائِنَاتِ إِلَى اللهِ تَعَالَى
The Fourth Introduction: Concerning the Origins of Disbelief and Innovations. And the origins of disbelief and innovations are seven: Essential Necessity (al-ijab al-dhati): it is the attribution of all beings to Allah Almighty
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of an enumeration. Sanusi introduces the Fourth Introduction on the origins (usul) of kufr (disbelief) and bid'a (innovation). He states that there are seven such origins. He then names the first one: 'al-ijab al-dhati' (essential necessity or essential attribution). He defines it as attributing all beings (al-ka'inat, i.e., all created things and events) directly to God Almighty. The text cuts off here on page 99. This origin likely refers to the view that God is compelled by His essence to create, or that all things emanate from Him necessarily, which could lead to denying God's free will and willful creation. The passage sets up the list but only gives the first item.

Key Terms
أصول الكفر والبدع
الإيجاب الذاتي
إسناد الكائنات إلى الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that attributing all beings to God is a correct expression of tawhid and shows reliance on God.
Correction: Sanusi warns that this belief implies that God is compelled by His essence, denying His free will and making creation necessary rather than voluntary. True tawhid acknowledges God's absolute freedom and the contingent nature of creation.

Checkpoint

A) Denying God's existence.
B) Attributing all beings to God Almighty.
C) Worshipping idols.
D) Denying prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The first origin of disbelief and innovation according to Sanusi is the belief that all beings are necessarily attributed to God, which denies God's free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does the belief that your existence is purely from His will and not a necessity change your gratitude? What would it look like for you to live with that awareness today?
?
When something goes wrong in your life, do you ever feel entitled to ask 'Why did God have to do this to me?' as if He was compelled? This lesson teaches that even thinking God is compelled by His essence is an origin of disbelief. Can you identify a specific situation where you might be slipping into that mindset? What will you remember from this lesson to correct it?
?
The first origin of disbelief is attributing all beings directly to God as a necessity. But we are constantly told that everything happens by God's will. What is the subtle difference in your heart between trusting God's will and expecting God's will? How can you ensure your trust does not turn into a demand that God act in a certain way, which would imply necessity?

Six Kinds of Possible Opposites
Sanusi enumerates six types of opposites among possible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six categories of opposites that can apply to possible (contingent) things in kalam.

Core Idea
Opposites among possible things come in six distinct categories, not just one.

In this passage, Sanusi introduces a classification that is essential for arguments about God's attributes and the contingency of the world. Before we can reason about what God must be, we need to understand the kinds of opposites that creation can have. These six categories cover the fundamental ways things can be contrasted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own wording introduce the six categories.
Notice that Sanusi begins by stating the topic and then enumerates.
2
Identify each category
Go through each of the six categories in the list: existence/non-existence, quantities, qualities, times, places, directions. Reflect on what each means.
Think of concrete examples for each: large/small for quantities, knowledge/ignorance for qualities, etc.
3
Reflect on the importance
Ask yourself: Why does Sanusi list these six? How do they help in theological arguments? The commentary will clarify.
This classification is used later to argue that God cannot have opposites or be subject to these categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقدمة السادسة : في الممكنات المتقابلات ]
وَالْمُمْكِنَاتُ الْمُتَقَابِلاتُ سِتَّةُ : الْوُجُودُ وَالْعَدَمُ ، وَالْمَقَادِيرُ ، وَالصَّفَاتُ ، وَالأَزْمِنَةُ ، وَالْأَمْكِنَةُ ، وَالْجِهَاتُ .
[The Sixth Preface: On Possible Opposites] And the possible opposites are six: existence and non-existence, quantities, qualities, times, places, and directions.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the six categories of opposites that can apply to possible (contingent) things. In kalam, 'possible' (ممكن) is that which can exist or not exist. The opposites are:

1. Existence and non-existence (الوجود والعدم) — the fundamental pair.
2. Quantities (المقادير) — e.g., large and small.
3. Qualities (الصفات) — e.g., knowledge and ignorance.
4. Times (الأزمنة) — e.g., past and future.
5. Places (الأمكنة) — e.g., inside and outside.
6. Directions (الجهات) — e.g., above and below.

These are used in arguments about God’s attributes and the contingency of the world.

Key Terms

الأزمنة
الأمكنة
الجهات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'opposites' only means contradiction (existence/non-existence), but Sanusi includes other relational opposites like quantity and quality.
Correction: Sanusi lists six categories, showing that possible things can be opposite in many ways.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The Six Mutually Opposed Possible Things
Sanusi lists the six categories of mutually opposed possible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six categories of mutually opposed possible things and their role in theological discussions about contingent beings.

Core Idea
All contingent things fall into six pairs of opposites.

Sanusi introduces the sixth foundational preface, a classification of the basic categories of opposites that apply to all possible (contingent) things. Understanding this classification is essential for discussing the attributes of Allah and the nature of creation, as it provides the vocabulary for theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage

The passage is concise; each item is a category of opposites.
2
Identify the six categories
Translate and list each category. Note that the first category is a pair (existence and non-existence), while the others are singular nouns but imply opposites (e.g., measures imply large/small).
This classification is foundational in kalam.
3

Ask: Why does Sanusi list these? The categories help analyze any contingent being in terms of its essential properties. They are used to discuss what is possible, necessary, or impossible for Allah and creation.

Source Text

Division of Kalam into Khabar and Insha'
Sanusi divides kalam into khabar and insha' and defines each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main divisions of kalam (speech) according to Sanusi: informative (khabar) and performative (insha'), and why only informative speech can be true or false.

Core Idea
Speech is either about reality or about action; only the first can be true or false.

Sanusi begins by categorizing all meaningful speech into two fundamental types. This division is crucial because it sets the stage for understanding what kinds of statements can be evaluated as true or false. The passage introduces the key terms that will underlie subsequent discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the clear contrast between the two types of speech.
Pay attention to the phrase 'li-dhatihi' (in its essence) which emphasizes the intrinsic property.
2
Identify the two divisions
Find the two categories: khabar and insha'. Note their definitions as given by Sanusi.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why only khabar can be true or false. Think of examples of insha' (commands, questions) that have no truth value.
This division is foundational for all subsequent logical and theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْكَلَامُ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى خَبَرٍ وَإِنْشَاءِ . فَالْخَبَرُ : مَا يَحْتَمِلُ الصِّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ لِذَاتِهِ . وَالإِنْشَاءُ : مَا لا يَحْتَمِلُ صِدْقاً وَلا كَذِباً لِذَاتِهِ . وَالصِّدْقُ : عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ مُطَابَقَةِ الْخَبَرِ لِمَا فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ خَالَفَ اللِاعْتِقَادَ أَمْ لا . وَالْكَذِبُ : عَدَمُ مُطَابَقَةِ الْخَبَرِ لِمَا فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ ، وَافَقَ الاعْتِقَادَ أَمْ لا .
And kalam is divided into informative (khabar) and performative (insha'). Informative speech: that which admits truth and falsehood in its essence. Performative speech: that which does not admit truth nor falsehood in its essence. Truth: is an expression for the correspondence of the informative speech to what is in the actual state of affairs, whether it agrees with belief or not. Falsehood: is non-correspondence of the informative speech to what is in the actual state of affairs, whether it agrees with belief or not.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage first divides kalam (speech) into two types: informative (khabar) and performative (insha'). Informative speech is that which can be true or false in its essence, because it makes a claim about reality. Performative speech, such as commands or questions, does not have truth value in itself. Then the author defines truth (sidq) as the correspondence of the informative statement to the actual state of affairs (ma fi nafs al-amr), regardless of whether the speaker believes it or not. Similarly, falsehood (kadhb) is the non-correspondence of the informative statement to reality, again regardless of the speaker's belief. This is a realist definition of truth and falsehood, grounding truth in objective reality independent of the speaker's mental state.

Key Terms
كلام
خبر
إنشاء
صدق
كذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All speech can be true or false.
Correction: Only informative speech (khabar) can be true or false; performative speech (insha') like commands and questions has no truth value.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which type of speech can be true or false?
A) Both khabar and insha'
B) Only khabar
C) Only insha'
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Kalam is divided into khabar (informative) and insha' (performative); only khabar can be evaluated as true or false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak, do you always ensure your khabar (informative statements) are true? Think of the last conversation you had—was every statement you made accurate? How can you hold yourself more accountable to truthfulness in daily speech?
?
Consider the insha' (performative speech) in your prayer, such as 'Allahu Akbar' or the Fatiha. Since insha' cannot be true or false in itself, what does that tell you about the nature of your worship? Does it change how you approach sincerity and presence of heart?
?
Reflect on a time when you treated a divine command (insha') as merely informative, thinking you could evaluate its truth rather than obey it. How does understanding the distinction between khabar and insha' reshape your relationship with revelation and your responsibility to act?

Definitions of Truth and Falsehood
Sanusi defines truth and falsehood in terms of correspondence to reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's realist definitions of truth and falsehood, based on correspondence to the actual state of affairs independent of the speaker's belief.

Core Idea
Truth is correspondence to reality, not to belief.

Having classified speech into informative and performative, Sanusi now defines the terms 'truth' and 'falsehood' specifically for informative speech. He grounds them in objective reality, rejecting any subjective measure based on the speaker's conviction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the repeated phrase 'whether it agrees with belief or not'.
This phrase is the key to understanding Sanusi's objective definition.
2
Analyze the definition of truth
Truth is defined as correspondence (mutabaqah) of the informative statement to what is in 'nafs al-amr' (the actual state of affairs). Note that belief is explicitly excluded from the definition.
3
Analyze the definition of falsehood
Falsehood is the lack of that correspondence. Again, belief is irrelevant.
This guards against the common misconception that a lie is defined by the speaker's belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And kalam is divided into informative (khabar) and performative (insha'). Informative speech: that which admits truth and falsehood in its essence. Performative speech: that which does not admit truth nor falsehood in its essence. Truth: is an expression for the correspondence of the informative speech to what is in the actual state of affairs, whether it agrees with belief or not. Falsehood: is non-correspondence of the informative speech to what is in the actual state of affairs, whether it agrees with belief or not.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage first divides kalam (speech) into two types: informative (khabar) and performative (insha'). Informative speech is that which can be true or false in its essence, because it makes a claim about reality. Performative speech, such as commands or questions, does not have truth value in itself. Then the author defines truth (sidq) as the correspondence of the informative statement to the actual state of affairs (ma fi nafs al-amr), regardless of whether the speaker believes it or not. Similarly, falsehood (kadhb) is the non-correspondence of the informative statement to reality, again regardless of the speaker's belief. This is a realist definition of truth and falsehood, grounding truth in objective reality independent of the speaker's mental state.

Key Terms
صدق
كذب
مطابقة
نفس الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Truth and falsehood depend on the speaker's belief.
Correction: Sanusi defines truth and falsehood solely by correspondence to reality, not by belief.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, when is a statement considered false?
A) When the speaker believes it is false
B) When it does not correspond to the actual state of affairs
C) When it is disagreed upon
D) When it is performative

Key Takeaway
Truth is correspondence of informative speech to reality; falsehood is lack of that correspondence, and neither depends on the speaker's belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation where you believed you were speaking truthfully, but your words did not match reality. Did you feel justified because of your belief? How does Sanusi's definition expose the weight of responsibility on your words? Will you now commit to verifying your statements before speaking, even when you 'feel' they are true?
?
If truth is correspondence to reality, not to your belief, then every false statement you make—even by mistake—is actually false. Does this humble you? How will you change your habits of speech to align with reality, especially in matters of deen and worldly affairs?
?
Allah's attribute of as-Sidq means His speech is perfectly true. Since your speech is not infallible, what safeguards will you put in place to ensure your words are as true as possible? Are you willing to admit when you have spoken falsehood, even if you believed it?

Division of Speech and Objective Truth
Sanusi defines the two types of speech and the criteria for truth and falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the division of speech into report and construction, and the objective definitions of truth and falsehood based on correspondence to reality.

Core Idea
Truth is correspondence to reality, not to belief.

In this short but foundational passage, Sanusi establishes the basic classification of speech for Islamic logic and theology. He distinguishes between statements that can be true or false (reports) and those that cannot (constructions). He then defines truth and falsehood in terms of objective reality, separating them from the speaker's belief. This objective standard is crucial for sound reasoning about theological claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the parallelism between the definitions of khabar and insha', and between sidq and kidhb.
2
Identify the two types of speech
The passage first divides 'kalam' into 'khabar' and 'insha''. Find their defining characteristics: what makes something a report vs. a construction?
A report can be true or false; a construction cannot.
3
Understand the definitions of truth and falsehood
Truth and falsehood apply only to reports. Note that the passage emphasizes 'correspondence to reality' and explicitly separates it from whether the speaker believes it or not.
This objective definition is a key point of Islamic epistemology.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why does Sanusi bother to define truth independently of belief? How does this help in arguments about Allah and revelation?
It grounds theological debate in objective reality, not personal conviction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْكَلَامُ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى خَبَرٍ وَإِنْشَاءٍ
فَالْخَبَرُ : مَا يَحْتَمِلُ الصِّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ لِذَاتِهِ .
وَالإِنْشَاءُ : مَا لا يَحْتَمِلُ صِدْقاً وَلا كَذِباً لِذَاتِهِ .
وَالصِّدْقُ : عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ مُطَابَقَةِ الْخَبَرِ لِمَا فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ خَالَفَ الاعْتِقَادَ أَمْ لا .
وَالْكَذِبُ : عَدَمُ مُطَابَقَةِ الْخَبَرِ لِمَا فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ ، وَافَقَ الاعْتِقَادَ أَمْ لا
And speech (kalam) divides into report (khabar) and construction (insha'). So the report: that which admits truth and falsehood in itself. And the construction: that which does not admit truth nor falsehood in itself. And truth (sidq): expression for the report's correspondence to what is in reality, whether it conforms to belief or not. And falsehood (kidhb): non-correspondence of the report to what is in reality, whether it conforms to belief or not.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of types of speech and their truth-values. (1) Speech (al-kalam) divides into two categories: khabar (report/statement) and insha' (construction/performative). (2) A report is defined as something that by its nature can be true or false; examples are statements like 'The sky is blue.' (3) An insha' is something that by its nature cannot be true or false; examples include commands, questions, and exclamations. (4) Truth is then defined as correspondence of the report to reality, regardless of whether the speaker believes it or not. (5) Falsehood is defined as lack of correspondence, regardless of belief. This emphasizes objective correspondence rather than subjective intention.

Key Takeaway

Conception and Assent: The Two Types of Knowledge
Sanusi defines ḥukm and classifies knowledge into taṣawwur and taṣdīq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defines a judgment (ḥukm) and how he classifies knowledge into conception (taṣawwur) and assent (taṣdīq).

Core Idea
Knowledge is either conception without judgment or assent with judgment.

Before discussing theological proofs, Sanusi establishes the basic terminology of logic and epistemology. He defines what a judgment is (affirmation or denial) and then distinguishes two types of perception: one where we merely grasp a meaning, and another where we affirm or deny that meaning of something. This distinction, known as taṣawwur and taṣdīq, is the foundation of all subsequent reasoning in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the definitions and examples.
2
Identify the definition of ḥukm
Sanusi begins by defining ḥukm. Find that definition and note that it is either affirmation or denial.
3
Distinguish taṣawwur and taṣdīq
Trace how Sanusi differentiates between mere conception (taṣawwur) and conception with a judgment (taṣdīq). The example of temporality is key.
4
Reflect on why this matters
The commentary notes that this introduction is placed here because all later topics depend on this classification. Ask yourself why understanding these two types of knowledge is essential for kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْحُكْمُ : إِثْبَاتُ أَمْرٍ أَوْ نَفْيُهُ يعني : أَنَّ مَنْ أدرك أمراً مِنَ الأمورِ : فإما أن يتصوَّرَ معناه فقط ولم يحكم بثبوته ولا نفيه ، فهذا الإدراكُ يُسمَّى في الاصطلاحِ : تصوراً ؛ كإدراكنا مثلاً أنَّ معنى الحدوثِ : الوجود بعدَ عدمِ ، ولم نثبته لأمرٍ ولا نفيناهُ عنهُ وإِمَّا أن يتصوَّرَ معَ ذلكَ ثبوت ذلك المعنى لأمر أو نفيه عنه ، فهذا الإدراكُ يُسمَّى في الاصطلاح : تصديقاً ، ويُسمى أيضاً : حُكْماً ؛ كإثباتنا الحدوث مثلاً - بعد تصورِنا لمعناه ـ للعوالم ؛ وهي ما سوى المولى تبارك وتعالى ، فنقولُ : العوالم حادثةٌ ، أو نفيناهُ عمَّنَ وجَبَ قدمه ؛ وهو مولانا تبارك وتعالى ، فنقولُ : مولانا جل وعزَّ ليسَ بحادث (۱) إنما قدَّم مقدمة الأحكام على غيرها ؛ لأن بها يعرف ما عداها . « بناني » ( ص ) .
The judgment (al-ḥukm) is affirming a thing or denying it. That is: whoever perceives a thing from among things: either he conceives (yataṣawwaru) its meaning only and does not judge its affirmation nor its denial – this perception is called in terminology conception (taṣawwur), like our perception, for example, that the meaning of temporality (al-ḥudūth) is existence after non-existence, and we have not affirmed it for a thing nor denied it from it. Or he conceives, along with that, the affirmation of that meaning for a thing or its denial from it – this perception is called in terminology assent (taṣdīq), and is also called judgment (ḥukm); like our affirmation of temporality, for example, after our conception of its meaning, to the worlds (al-ʿawālim) – which are other than the Lord (al-mawlā) blessed and exalted – so we say: the worlds are temporal. Or we deny it from Him who is necessarily eternal – and He is our Lord blessed and exalted – so we say: our Lord, majestic and mighty, is not temporal. (1) He only presented the introduction on judgments before others because through it what is other than it is known. [al-Banānī] (p.)
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'ḥukm' (judgment) and provides the foundational classification of knowledge into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). The author begins by stating that a judgment (ḥukm) is either an affirmation or a denial of something. He then explains that when someone perceives a matter, there are two possibilities. First, one may simply grasp the meaning of the thing without affirming or denying it of anything. This type of perception is called 'taṣawwur' (conception) in technical terminology. The example given is understanding the meaning of 'ḥudūth' (temporality) as existence after non-existence, without attributing it to any specific entity. Second, along with understanding the meaning, one may also affirm that meaning for something or deny it from something. This is called 'taṣdīq' (assent) and also called 'ḥukm' (judgment). The example is affirming temporality of the worlds (everything other than God), leading to the statement 'The worlds are temporal,' or denying temporality of God, leading to 'Our Lord is not temporal.' The passage closes with a gloss explaining why this introduction on judgments was placed before other topics: because through it, what comes after is understood.

Key Terms

التصور
التصديق
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Tasawwur is a technical term for conception of a meaning without any judgment, not mere sensation.

Checkpoint

B) Hukm
C) Tasawwur
D) Hadith

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is divided into conception (mere understanding) and assent (understanding with affirmation or denial), and this classification is foundational for all kalām reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You have just learned that taṣdīq depends on taṣawwur. Reflect: do you truly conceive the meaning of ‘ḥudūth’ (temporality) as deeply as needed before you can affirm that everything other than Allah is temporal? Have you really internalized that only Allah is eternal? How does that change your priorities today?

Grammatical Note on 'bil-talab' in the Definition
Sanusi clarifies the grammatical dependency of the preposition 'bil-talab'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical relationship between the genitive 'bil-ṭalab' and the word 'khiṭāb' in the definition of the revealer's speech, and why this grammatical analysis is acceptable despite the description of the verbal noun.

Core Idea
Even a described verbal noun can govern genitives in Arabic grammar.

This lesson examines a detailed grammatical note by Sanusi on the definition discussed earlier. The phrase 'bil-ṭalab' appears as a genitive, and its attachment to the word 'khiṭāb' raises a subtle issue: the verbal noun 'khiṭāb' is described by an adjective before it is fully operative. Sanusi offers a justification for this construction, showing that Arabic grammar allows both strong and weak operators to govern genitives. This analysis demonstrates the precision required in defining uṣūl al-fiqh terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Note the key terms and the grammatical structure being discussed.
The passage ends abruptly; the full argument may continue beyond the provided text.
2
Identify the grammatical problem
Notice that the genitive 'bil-ṭalab' is best attached to 'khiṭāb', but 'khiṭāb' is a verbal noun that has been described by an adjective, which normally would weaken its verbal force and ability to govern.
This is the central issue Sanusi addresses.
3
Understand the justification
Sanusi provides two justifications: first, both weak and strong operators can govern genitives; second, the verbal noun here has not remained purely verbal, allowing leniency.
This shows that grammatical flexibility is part of the language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله لَهُ : ) بالطلب ، أو الإباحة ، أو الوضع لهما ) المجرورُ الذي ( بالطلب ( أحسنُ ما فيهِ أنْ يتعلَّق بقوله : ( خطاب ) ، وفيهِ وصفُ المصدر قبل إعمالِهِ ، إلا أنَّهُ يُسهَلُهُ أَنَّ المجرور يعملُ فيهِ العاملُ الضعيفُ والقويُّ ، وأيضاً : فالمصدر هنا لم يبق على
His statement 'to him' (lahu) with 'by request, or permission, or the setting [of something] for them'—the genitive which is 'by request'—the best about it is that it is attached to His statement 'khiṭāb' (address), and in it there is description of the verbal noun before its operation; however, it is facilitated by the fact that both the weak and strong operator can operate on the genitive, and also: the verbal noun here has not remained upon...
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This short passage is a grammatical note on the definition being discussed. The author examines the phrase 'bil-ṭalab' (by request) which is a genitive (majrūr) in the original definition. He states that the best analysis is to attach this genitive to the word 'khiṭāb' (address), even though the verbal noun (maskdar) 'khiṭāb' is described by an adjective (the phrase 'al-mutaʿalliq bi-afʿāl al-mukallafīn') before it is fully operative grammatically. This is usually problematic because describing a verbal noun weakens its verbal force. However, the author excuses this by noting that both strong and weak operators can govern genitives in Arabic. He also adds that the verbal noun here has not remained purely verbal—the text cuts off, but the implication is that this leniency is acceptable. The passage demonstrates the high level of grammatical precision in defining technical terms in uṣūl al-fiqh.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Grammatical details in definitions are trivial and do not affect the meaning or validity of legal terms.
Correction: This passage shows that precise grammatical analysis is essential in uṣūl al-fiqh because the correct interpretation of definitions depends on understanding the syntactic relationships between parts of the definition.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Definitions of the Five Legal Rulings
Sanusi defines the five ahkam taklifiyya with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the five legal rulings (ahkam taklifiyya) and the key difference between the four that involve a demand (talab) and permission (ibaha).

Core Idea
Five rulings, four derive from demand: obligation, recommendation, prohibition, and disapprobation.

Sanusi begins by defining the five categories of rulings that apply to human actions. Each definition comes with concrete examples, making abstract concepts accessible. Notice that the first four share the common element of 'demand' (talab), while the fifth, permission, is defined as the absence of demand.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read each definition in Arabic and English, paying attention to the pattern: each starts with the ruling name and then a description involving 'talab' or 'idhn'.
Notice that ibaha does not use the word 'talab'.
2
Identify the demand element
For the first four rulings, identify the phrase 'talab' followed by either 'jazim' or 'ghayr jazim'. Observe how the demand is either for an act or for refraining.
3
Compare ibaha
Permission is fundamentally different: it does not involve demand but rather equal permission to do or leave. Contrast its definition with the others.
This distinction is crucial for the next lesson.
4
Review the examples
The examples (faith, prayer, wine, etc.) help cement the meanings and show how rulings apply to real actions.
Examples are from core Islamic practices.
5
Reflect on coverage
These five rulings cover every possible human action from a legal perspective: required, recommended, forbidden, disliked, or permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالإيجاب : وهو طلب الفعل طلبا جازما ; كالإيمان بالله ورسله , وكقواعد الإسلام الخمس . والنّدب : وهو طلب الفعل طلبا غير جازم ; كصلاة الفجر ونحوها . والتّحريم : وهو طلب الكف عن الفعل طلبا جازما ; كشرب الخمر والزنا ونحوهما . والكراهة : وهي طلب الكف عن الفعل طلبا غير جازم ; كقراءة القرآن في الركوع والسجود مثلا . وأما الإباحة : فهي إذن الشرع في الفعل والترك معا من غير ترجيح لأحدهما على الآخر ; كالنكاح والبيع ونحوهما . لا إشكال في دخول الأربعة الأحكام في الطلب ; لأن الطلب : إما طلب فعل أو طلب ترك , وكل واحد منهما : إما جازم أو غير جازم , فالمجموع أربعة ; من ضرب اثنين في اثنين .
So obligation (al-ijab): it is the demand (talab) of an act with decisive demand; like faith in Allah and His messengers, and like the five pillars of Islam. And recommendation (al-nadb): it is the demand of an act with non-decisive demand; like the prayer of Fajr and similar. And prohibition (al-tahrim): it is the demand of refraining from an act with decisive demand; like drinking wine and fornication and similar. And disapprobation (al-karaha): it is the demand of refraining from an act with non-decisive demand; like reciting the Quran in bowing and prostration for example. As for permission (al-ibaha): it is the permission of the Law (shar‘) for both performing and abandoning an action equally, without preference of one over the other; like marriage and sale and similar. There is no problem in the inclusion of the four rulings in demand (talab); because demand is either a demand of an act or a demand of refraining, and each of them is either decisive or non-decisive, so the total is four: from multiplying two by two.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage defines the five legal rulings (ahkam taklifiyya) and then derives the four that involve demand (talab) through a simple logical combination. Sanusi begins by defining each ruling with examples: obligation (ijab) is a decisive demand to perform an act, like faith and the pillars of Islam; recommendation (nadb) is a non-decisive demand to perform an act, like the Fajr sunna prayer; prohibition (tahrim) is a decisive demand to refrain from an act, like wine and fornication; disapprobation (karaha) is a non-decisive demand to refrain from an act, like reciting Quran in bowing and prostration; permission (ibaha) is the Law's equal permission to do or leave an action, like marriage and sale. Then he explains logically that the four rulings (excluding ibaha) all fall under the concept of demand (talab). Demand is either for an act or for refraining (tark), and each of these demands is either decisive (jazim) or non-decisive (ghayr jazim). Combining these two bifurcations yields exactly four categories: decisive demand to act (ijab), non-decisive demand to act (nadb), decisive demand to refrain (tahrim), non-decisive demand to refrain (karaha). This is a concise demonstration that these four rulings are exhaustive and logically derived.

Key Terms
الإيجاب
الندب
التحريم
الكراهة
الإباحة
طلب
جازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The five rulings are arbitrary and have no logical basis.
Correction: Sanusi shows that four of them (obligation, recommendation, prohibition, disapprobation) are logically derived from the concept of demand (talab) using binary distinctions.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT defined using the term 'talab' (demand)?
A) Obligation (al-ijab)
B) Recommendation (al-nadb)
C) Permission (al-ibaha)
D) Prohibition (al-tahrim)

Key Takeaway
The five legal rulings (ahkam taklifiyya) are obligation, recommendation, prohibition, disapprobation, and permission; the first four involve a demand, while permission does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear 'obligation' (ijab), do you feel it as a decisive demand from Allah, or as a burden that weighs on you? What does your attitude toward the five pillars reveal about your faith? Commit today to performing them with the urgency they deserve.

?
Prohibition (tahrim) is a decisive demand to refrain. Think of a sin you find difficult to leave. Are you convinced that Allah's prohibition is absolute, or do you allow yourself excuses? Reflect on the weight of 'decisive demand' and ask yourself: will I treat this sin with the seriousness it demands starting today?

The Logical Derivation of Four Rulings from Demand
Sanusi derives the four demand-based rulings from two binary oppositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi logically derives the four rulings that involve demand (talab) from the combination of two binary distinctions: act vs. refrain and decisive vs. non-decisive.

Core Idea
Two times two gives four: the logical basis for obligation, recommendation, prohibition, and disapproval.

After defining the five rulings, Sanusi provides a concise logical demonstration that the first four are exhaustive and derived from the concept of demand. This small proof shows the systematic nature of Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the short Arabic passage and its translation. Note that it states there is no problem in the four rulings being included in demand.
2
Identify the two distinctions
The passage splits demand into two pairs: either for an act or for refraining; and each of those is either decisive or non-decisive.
These are binary oppositions.
3
Apply the multiplication
Multiplying the two possibilities of the first distinction (act/refrain) by the two of the second (decisive/non-decisive) yields four categories.
Sanusi explicitly says 'from multiplying two by two'.
4
Map the categories to rulings
Match each combination: decisive+act = obligation, non-decisive+act = recommendation, decisive+refrain = prohibition, non-decisive+refrain = disapprobation.
This mapping is not in the passage but follows from the earlier definitions.
5
Note the exclusion of ibaha
Permission does not involve demand, so it falls outside this derivation. This highlights the unique nature of ibaha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالإيجاب : وهو طلب الفعل طلبا جازما ; كالإيمان بالله ورسله , وكقواعد الإسلام الخمس . والنّدب : وهو طلب الفعل طلبا غير جازم ; كصلاة الفجر ونحوها . والتّحريم : وهو طلب الكف عن الفعل طلبا جازما ; كشرب الخمر والزنا ونحوهما . والكراهة : وهي طلب الكف عن الفعل طلبا غير جازم ; كقراءة القرآن في الركوع والسجود مثلا . وأما الإباحة : فهي إذن الشرع في الفعل والترك معا من غير ترجيح لأحدهما على الآخر ; كالنكاح والبيع ونحوهما . لا إشكال في دخول الأربعة الأحكام في الطلب ; لأن الطلب : إما طلب فعل أو طلب ترك , وكل واحد منهما : إما جازم أو غير جازم , فالمجموع أربعة ; من ضرب اثنين في اثنين .
So obligation (al-ijab): it is the demand (talab) of an act with decisive demand; like faith in Allah and His messengers, and like the five pillars of Islam. And recommendation (al-nadb): it is the demand of an act with non-decisive demand; like the prayer of Fajr and similar. And prohibition (al-tahrim): it is the demand of refraining from an act with decisive demand; like drinking wine and fornication and similar. And disapprobation (al-karaha): it is the demand of refraining from an act with non-decisive demand; like reciting the Quran in bowing and prostration for example. As for permission (al-ibaha): it is the permission of the Law (shar‘) for both performing and abandoning an action equally, without preference of one over the other; like marriage and sale and similar. There is no problem in the inclusion of the four rulings in demand (talab); because demand is either a demand of an act or a demand of refraining, and each of them is either decisive or non-decisive, so the total is four: from multiplying two by two.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage defines the five legal rulings (ahkam taklifiyya) and then derives the four that involve demand (talab) through a simple logical combination. Sanusi begins by defining each ruling with examples: obligation (ijab) is a decisive demand to perform an act, like faith and the pillars of Islam; recommendation (nadb) is a non-decisive demand to perform an act, like the Fajr sunna prayer; prohibition (tahrim) is a decisive demand to refrain from an act, like wine and fornication; disapprobation (karaha) is a non-decisive demand to refrain from an act, like reciting Quran in bowing and prostration; permission (ibaha) is the Law's equal permission to do or leave an action, like marriage and sale. Then he explains logically that the four rulings (excluding ibaha) all fall under the concept of demand (talab). Demand is either for an act or for refraining (tark), and each of these demands is either decisive (jazim) or non-decisive (ghayr jazim). Combining these two bifurcations yields exactly four categories: decisive demand to act (ijab), non-decisive demand to act (nadb), decisive demand to refrain (tahrim), non-decisive demand to refrain (karaha). This is a concise demonstration that these four rulings are exhaustive and logically derived.

Key Terms
طلب
جازم
غير جازم
فعل
ترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many rulings does Sanusi derive from the combination of act/refrain and decisive/non-decisive?
A) One
B) Two
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
By combining the two distinctions (act vs. refrain and decisive vs. non-decisive), four rulings—obligation, recommendation, prohibition, and disapprobation—are logically derived from the concept of demand.

?
Consider your daily actions: can you sort them into the four rulings? Where does ibaha (permissibility) fit in your life? Are you using it as a loophole to avoid decisive demands?
?
The logical neatness of these four rulings shows that Allah's guidance is precise and ordered. Does this give you more confidence in the Sharīʿah, or does it make you realize how careless you have been in distinguishing between categories? How will you change your approach to divine demands starting today?

Logical Components of the Definition of Obligation
Sanusi breaks down the definition of obligation into its logical parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of obligation (ijab) is constructed using genus (jins) and differentia (fasl), and how each restriction excludes other rulings.

Core Idea
A precise definition uses genus and differentia to separate obligation from other demands.

Sanusi dissects the definition of obligation using logical tools. He shows how 'demand' is the genus, 'act' excludes prohibitions, and 'decisive' excludes recommendations. This reveals the precision required in kalam definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
First, read the Arabic definition of obligation and its translation. Notice the structure: it has three parts (demand, act, decisive).
The definition is: 'Demand of an act with decisive demand.'
2
Identify the genus and differentia
Identify 'demand' as the genus and 'act' as the differentia. Ask: What does 'act' exclude? (It excludes prohibition and disapproval because they are demands to refrain, not to act.)
3
Understand the third restriction
The phrase 'decisive demand' excludes recommendation (nadb). Why? Because recommendation lacks decisiveness—the person is allowed to abandon it. Reflect on how this exclusion works.
Sanusi notes that in nadb, permission to abandon is given, so the demand is not decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولنا في حد الإيجاب : ( طلب ) جنس في الحد , وقولنا : ( الفعل ) فصل يخرج التحريم والكراهة ; لأنهما طلب كف عن فعل , لا طلب فعل . وقولنا : ( طلبا جازما ) يخرج الندب ; لأنه طلب للفعل من غير جزم في الطلب ; بألا يؤذن في الترك , بل هو قد سمح له في الترك . ولا يخفى عليك معرفة ما يحترز بالقيود عنه في سائر الحدود . واعلم : أن مذهب جمهور الأصوليين : أن الأحكام التكليفية وهي التي يخاطب بها المكلفون - خمسة : الإباحة والأربعة الداخلة في الطلب , وزاد السبكي سادسا ; وهو خلاف الأولى ; لأن النهي غير الجازم عنده إن تعلق بالكف عن فعل بدلالة المطابقة ; كالنهي المتعلق بالقراءة في الركوع مثلا .. فهو الكراهة , وإن تعلق بالكف عن الفعل بدلالة الالتزام ; كدلالة طلب المندوب بدلالة الالتزام على ضده .. فهو خلاف الأولى ; كطلب قيام الليل ; فإنه يدل على النهي بالالتزام على النهي عن ضده ; كنوم الليل كله , فيطلق على النوم أنه خلاف الأولى , ولا يطلق عليه أنه مكروه .
And our statement in the definition of obligation (al-ijab): 'demand (talab)' is the genus (jins) in the definition, and our statement: 'the act (al-fi’l)' is a differentia (fasl) that excludes prohibition (tahrim) and disapprobation (karaha) because they are a demand to refrain from an act, not a demand of an act. And our statement: 'with decisive demand (talaban jaziman)' excludes recommendation (nadb) because it is a demand for the act without decisiveness in the demand; in that permission to abandon is not given, rather he is permitted to abandon. And it is not hidden from you the knowledge of what is guarded against by the restrictions in the other definitions. And know: that the position of the majority of uṣūliyyūn (legal theorists) is that the rulings of obligation (al-aḥkām al-taklīfiyya), which are those by which the legally responsible are addressed, are five: permission (ibāḥa) and the four that fall under demand (ṭalab). And al-Subkī added a sixth: it is contrary to the better (khilāf al-awlā). Because the non-decisive prohibition (al-nahy ghayr al-jāzim) according to him, if it pertains to refraining from an act by direct indication (dalālat al-muṭābaqa) — like the prohibition related to recitation in bowing for example — then it is disapprobation (karāha). And if it pertains to refraining from an act by implication (dalālat al-iltizām) — like the indication of the demand of the recommended (al-mandūb) by implication of its opposite — then it is contrary to the better (khilāf al-awlā). Like the demand to stand at night (qiyām al-layl), for it indicates by implication the prohibition of its opposite, like sleeping the entire night. So it is said of sleep that it is contrary to the better, and it is not said of it that it is disliked (makrūh).
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage serves a dual purpose: first, it provides a detailed logical breakdown of the definition of obligation (ijab) using the classical method of genus (jins) and differentia (fasl), and second, it discusses the classification of legal rulings and introduces al-Subki's addition of a sixth type. Sanusi explains that in the definition of ijab, 'demand' (talab) is the genus common to all rulings that involve demand. Then 'the act' (al-fi'l) is a differentia that excludes prohibition and disapprobation because those are demands to refrain, not to act. Then 'decisive demand' (talaban jaziman) excludes recommendation (nadb) because the demand in nadb is not decisive—the person is allowed to abandon the act. This analysis applies similarly to the other definitions. Next, Sanusi states that the majority of usul scholars hold that the rulings of obligation (ahkam taklifiyya) are five: ibaha plus the four that fall under demand. However, al-Subki added a sixth: khilaf al-awla (contrary to the better). The reasoning is that a non-decisive prohibition (nahy ghayr jazim) can be understood in two ways: if it directly denotes refraining from an act (by dalalat al-mutabaqa), it is called karaha; if it implies refraining from an act by implication (dalalat al-iltizam), such as the demand for a recommended act implying the prohibition of its opposite, then it is called khilaf al-awla, not makruh. For example, the recommended act of standing at night in prayer (qiyam al-layl) implies that sleeping the whole night is contrary to the better, but it is not deemed disliked (makruh).

طلب
جنس
فصل
الندب
التحريم
الكراهة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the definition of obligation includes any demand to act, even if not decisive.
Correction: Sanusi shows that 'decisive demand' is necessary to exclude recommendation, which is a non-decisive demand to act.

Checkpoint
Why does the phrase 'decisive demand' (talaban jaziman) exclude recommendation (nadb)?
A) Because recommendation is not a demand at all.
B) Because recommendation is a demand to refrain, not to act.
C) Because recommendation lacks decisiveness; the person is permitted to abandon.
D) Because recommendation is a demand for an act but the demand is not from God.

Key Takeaway
The definition of obligation ('demand of an act with decisive demand') uses genus and differentia to precisely exclude other rulings like prohibition and recommendation.

?
Sanusi’s analysis shows that each part of the definition has a specific purpose in excluding error. Commit to looking up the precise definition of one Islamic term this week.
?

Is your understanding based on careful study or on vague habit? If your definition is imprecise, you might be burdening yourself with unnecessary guilt or neglecting real duties. Subki's Addition: The Sixth Ruling (Khilaf al-Awla)
Sanusi presents Subki's view on a sixth ruling and its difference from disapprobation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Subki's addition of 'contrary to the better' (khilaf al-awla) as a sixth ruling and how it differs from disapproval (karaha) based on the mode of indication.

Core Idea
Khilaf al-awla is implied by a non-decisive demand for an act, unlike karaha which is directly expressed.

Sanusi explains that the majority of scholars recognize five rulings, but al-Subki added a sixth: khilaf al-awla. This distinction rests on whether the non-decisive prohibition is direct (dalalat al-mutabaqa) or implied (dalalat al-iltizam). The example of qiyam al-layl clarifies the difference.

Guided Path
1
Understand the five rulings of the majority
Sanusi states the majority's position: five rulings (ibaha, obligation, recommendation, prohibition, disapproval). Note that these form the standard classification.
2
Learn Subki's sixth ruling
Subki adds 'contrary to the better' (khilaf al-awla). This is based on a non-decisive prohibition that is implied, not directly expressed.
The key is the mode of indication: direct (dalalat al-mutabaqa) vs. implied (dalalat al-iltizam).
3
Study the example of qiyam al-layl
The recommendation to pray at night implies that sleeping the whole night is contrary to the better. This is not karaha because the prohibition is not direct.
This distinguishes khilaf al-awla from karaha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
