Deriving the Four Types of Command
Sanusi derives four types from act/abstention and decisiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four types of shar'i command (obligation, recommendation, prohibition, dislike) are logically derived from the two binary distinctions of act/refrain and decisive/non-decisive.

Core Idea
The four rulings come from multiplying act/refrain by decisive/non-decisive.

Sanusi begins by explaining a simple logical method: there are two kinds of command (command to act, command to refrain), and each can be either decisive or non-decisive. By multiplying the two possibilities, we get exactly four categories. This combinatorial approach is the foundation for defining each ruling precisely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on derivation
Read the Arabic passage about the inclusion of four rulings in command. Focus on the first part up to 'four'. Notice how the author states there is no problem with including them.
2
Identify the two distinctions
Find the two binary distinctions: act vs. refrain, and decisive vs. non-decisive. Trace how each command (طلب) is first divided by act/refrain, then each of those by decisiveness.
The author uses 'إمَّا ... أو ...' to indicate alternatives.
3
Verify the four combinations
Multiply the two categories from the first distinction by the two from the second. Confirm that the result is four. Relate each combination to a known ruling: decisive act=obligation, non-decisive act=recommendation, decisive refrain=prohibition, non-decisive refrain=dislike.
The text says 'فالمجموع أربعة' and 'من ضرب اثنين في اثنين'.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider why this logical derivation is important. It shows that the four rulings are exhaustive and mutually exclusive. Any command must fall into one of these categories.
This method prevents confusion between similar rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا إشكال في دخول الأربعةِ الأحكام في الطلب ؛ لأنَّ الطلب : إمَّا طلب فعل أو طلب ترك ، وكلُّ واحدٍ منهما : إمَّا جازمٌ أو غير جازم ، فالمجموع أربعة ؛ مِنْ ضَرْبِ اثنين في اثنينِ . وقولنا في حد الإيجاب : ( طلب ) جنس في الحد وقولنا : ( الفعل ) فصل يُخرجُ التحريم والكراهة ؛ لأنهما طلب كف عن فعل ، لا طلب فعل . وقولنا : ( طلباً جازماً ) يُخرجُ الندبَ ؛ لأنَّهُ طلبٌ للفعل مِنْ غيرِ جزم في الطلب ؛ بألا يؤذن في التركِ ، بل هو قد سمح له في الترك . ولا يخفى عليك معرفة ما يُحترز بالقيود عنه في سائر الحدود .
There is no problem in the inclusion of the four rulings in command; because command: either it is a command to do an act or a command to refrain, and each one of them is either decisive or non-decisive, so the total is four, by multiplying two by two. And our statement in the definition of obligation: (command) is the genus in the definition, and our statement: (act) is the differentia that excludes prohibition and dislike, because they are command to refrain from an act, not command to do an act. And our statement: (decisive command) excludes recommendation, because it is a command to do an act without decisiveness in the command, so that he is not permitted to abandon it, rather he is allowed to abandon it. And it is not hidden from you knowledge of what is guarded against by the restrictions in the rest of the definitions.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage explains how the four types of shar'i rulings that involve a command (طلب) are derived from a logical combination. The author begins by noting that there is no difficulty in including the four rulings (obligation, recommendation, prohibition, dislike) under the category of command, because command (طلب) is either a command to perform an act (طلب فعل) or a command to refrain (طلب ترك). Each of these can be either decisive (جازم) or non-decisive (غير جازم). Multiplying the two divisions yields four: decisive command to act (obligation, وجوب), non-decisive command to act (recommendation, ندب), decisive command to refrain (prohibition, تحريم), non-decisive command to refrain (dislike, كراهة). The author then applies this to the definition of obligation (إيجاب): 'command' is the genus, 'act' is the differentia that excludes prohibition and dislike (which are commands to refrain, not to act), and 'decisive command' excludes recommendation (which is a command to act but not decisive, because the person is allowed to abandon it). He ends by noting that the same method of using restrictive qualifiers should be understood for other definitions.

Key Terms

جازم
غير جازم
إيجاب
ندب
تحريم
كراهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that there are only two types of command: command to act and command to refrain.
Correction: The text shows that each of those is further divided into decisive and non-decisive, yielding four distinct types.

Checkpoint
How many types of command are derived by combining the two divisions?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The four rulings are a complete logical set from two binary distinctions: act/refrain and decisive/non-decisive.

?
This passage shows that even non-decisive commands are still commands from Allah. Do you value the recommended acts as much as the obligatory ones, or do you neglect them because they are 'optional'? What one recommended act can you start doing consistently starting tomorrow as a way to increase your love for Allah's commands?

Applying the Derivation to the Definition of Obligation
Sanusi applies the derivation to the definition of obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of obligation uses the terms 'command', 'act', and 'decisive' to exclude the other three types of command.

Core Idea
Obligation is a decisive command to act, which excludes recommendation, prohibition, and dislike.

After establishing the four categories, Sanusi now shows how they relate to the formal definition of obligation. He takes the definition 'a decisive command to do an act' and explains how each component excludes other rulings. This illustrates the precision required in usul definitions and how the derivation previously discussed is applied.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on definition
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وقولنا في حد الإيجاب'. Identify the definition of obligation as presented: 'طلب الفعل طلباً جازماً' (a decisive command to do an act).
2
Understand genus and differentiae
Recognize that 'command' (طلب) is the genus, 'act' (الفعل) is a differentia, and 'decisive' (جازم) is another differentia. The genus places obligation under the category of command; the differentiae narrow it down.
In definition, genus is the broader category, differentia distinguishes the species.
3
See how each part excludes other rulings
Follow the author's explanation: 'act' excludes prohibition and dislike (since they command refrain, not act); 'decisive' excludes recommendation (since it is non-decisive). Understand why each exclusion works.
4
Apply to other definitions
Reflect on how the same method would apply to defining prohibition, recommendation, and dislike. Each would have a similar structure with different differentiae.
The author hints at this with 'ولا يخفى عليك معرفة ما يُحترز بالقيود عنه في سائر الحدود'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا إشكال في دخول الأربعةِ الأحكام في الطلب ؛ لأنَّ الطلب : إمَّا طلب فعل أو طلب ترك ، وكلُّ واحدٍ منهما : إمَّا جازمٌ أو غير جازم ، فالمجموع أربعة ؛ مِنْ ضَرْبِ اثنين في اثنينِ . وقولنا في حد الإيجاب : ( طلب ) جنس في الحد وقولنا : ( الفعل ) فصل يُخرجُ التحريم والكراهة ؛ لأنهما طلب كف عن فعل ، لا طلب فعل . وقولنا : ( طلباً جازماً ) يُخرجُ الندبَ ؛ لأنَّهُ طلبٌ للفعل مِنْ غيرِ جزم في الطلب ؛ بألا يؤذن في التركِ ، بل هو قد سمح له في الترك . ولا يخفى عليك معرفة ما يُحترز بالقيود عنه في سائر الحدود .
There is no problem in the inclusion of the four rulings in command; because command: either it is a command to do an act or a command to refrain, and each one of them is either decisive or non-decisive, so the total is four, by multiplying two by two. And our statement in the definition of obligation: (command) is the genus in the definition, and our statement: (act) is the differentia that excludes prohibition and dislike, because they are command to refrain from an act, not command to do an act. And our statement: (decisive command) excludes recommendation, because it is a command to do an act without decisiveness in the command, so that he is not permitted to abandon it, rather he is allowed to abandon it. And it is not hidden from you knowledge of what is guarded against by the restrictions in the rest of the definitions.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage explains how the four types of shar'i rulings that involve a command (طلب) are derived from a logical combination. The author begins by noting that there is no difficulty in including the four rulings (obligation, recommendation, prohibition, dislike) under the category of command, because command (طلب) is either a command to perform an act (طلب فعل) or a command to refrain (طلب ترك). Each of these can be either decisive (جازم) or non-decisive (غير جازم). Multiplying the two divisions yields four: decisive command to act (obligation, وجوب), non-decisive command to act (recommendation, ندب), decisive command to refrain (prohibition, تحريم), non-decisive command to refrain (dislike, كراهة). The author then applies this to the definition of obligation (إيجاب): 'command' is the genus, 'act' is the differentia that excludes prohibition and dislike (which are commands to refrain, not to act), and 'decisive command' excludes recommendation (which is a command to act but not decisive, because the person is allowed to abandon it). He ends by noting that the same method of using restrictive qualifiers should be understood for other definitions.

Key Terms
حد
جنس
فصل
إيجاب
ندب
تحريم
كراهة
طلب جازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that recommendation is not a command at all, since the person is allowed to abandon it.
Correction: Recommendation is still a command (طلب), but a non-decisive one, as the text says: 'طلب للفعل من غير جزم'.

Checkpoint
Which part of the definition of obligation excludes recommendation?
A) 'command'
B) 'act'
C) 'decisive'
D) 'obligation'

Key Takeaway
Obligation's definition uses three components (command, act, decisive) to precisely distinguish it from recommendation, prohibition, and dislike.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a command from Allah, do you immediately look for a way out, or do you receive it as decisive? How does the definition of obligation as 'decisive command to act' confront your tendency to treat some obligations as optional?
?
Think of an obligation you habitually neglect. Are you treating it as a recommendation? What would change if you truly believed it is a decisive command with no permission to abandon?
?
In your daily life, how often do you confuse prohibition (leaving something) with obligation (doing something)? This passage distinguishes them by act vs refraining. Where in your practice have you conflated the two?

The Five Taklifi Rulings and Al-Subki's Addition of Khilaf al-Awla
Sanusi enumerates the five taklifi rulings and explains al-Subki's sixth category with its logical basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five taklifi rulings according to the majority of usul scholars and the rationale behind al-Subki's addition of khilaf al-awla as a sixth category.

Core Idea
Taklifi rulings are five, but al-Subki added a sixth: contrary to the better, based on implied indication.

Sanusi begins by stating the consensus of usul scholars on the five categories of rulings that apply to the legally responsible person. He then introduces al-Subki's proposed sixth category, khilaf al-awla, and explains how it differs from dislike. This passage clarifies a subtle distinction in how prohibitions are indicated—directly or by implication—and shows how later scholars debated its origin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five taklifi rulings
Read the passage and note that the majority of usul scholars hold that there are five taklifi rulings: permissibility (ibaha) plus the four that fall under command (obligation, recommendation, prohibition, dislike).
The four under command are often listed as wujub, nadb, tahrim, and karaha.
2
Understand al-Subki's addition
Al-Subki added a sixth ruling: khilaf al-awla (contrary to the better). Focus on how he distinguishes it from karaha (dislike) based on the type of indication—direct (dalalat al-mutabaqa) vs implied (dalalat al-iltizam).
The example of night prayer helps illustrate the concept.
3
Note the historical attribution
The passage mentions that Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) was the first to mention this sixth division, though al-Iraqi says it was transmitted from earlier scholars who called it a later innovation.
This shows the development of usul terminology over time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ مذهب جمهورِ الأصوليين : أنَّ الأحكام التكليفية وهي التي يُخاطَبُ بها المكلَّفونَ - خمسة : الإباحة والأربعةُ الداخلة في الطلب ، وزادَ السبكي سادساً ؛ وهو خلافُ الأولى ؛ لأنَّ النهْيَ غير الجازمِ عندَهُ إِنْ تعلَّق بالكفّ عن فعل بدلالة المطابقة ؛ كالنهي المتعلق بالقراءة في الركوع مثلاً .. فهو الكراهة ، وإن تعلَّق بالكف عنِ الفعلِ بدلالة الالتزام ؛ كدلالة طلب المندوب بدلالة الالتزام على ضدِّهِ .. فهو خلافُ الأولى ؛ كطلب قيام الليل ؛ فإنَّهُ يدلُّ عن النهي بالالتزامِ على النهي عن ضدَّهِ ؛ كنومِ الليل كلّهِ ، فيطلقُ على النوم أَنَّهُ خلافُ الأولى ، ولا يطلق عليهِ أنَّه مكروه . وتبع السبكي في زيادة هذا القسم السادس إمام الحرمين ، قالَ : ( والإمامُ أوَّلُ مَنْ علمناه ذكرَهُ ) ، قال العراقي : ( بل نقله الإمام عن غيره فقال : إنَّهُ ممَّا أحدثه المتأخُرُونَ ).
And know that the school of the majority of usul scholars is that the taklifi rulings, by which the legally responsible are addressed, are five: permissibility and the four included in command. And al-Subki added a sixth, which is contrary to the better (khilaf al-awla), because according to him, if a non-decisive prohibition attaches to refraining from an act by direct indication (dalalat al-mutabaqa), like the prohibition related to recitation in bowing for example, then it is dislike (karaha). And if it attaches to refraining from an act by implied indication (dalalat al-iltizam), like the indication of a recommended command by implied indication to its opposite, then it is contrary to the better, like the command to perform night prayer; for it indicates by implied indication the prohibition of its opposite, like sleeping the entire night, so sleeping is called 'contrary to the better' and it is not called disliked. And al-Subki was followed in adding this sixth division by the Imam al-Haramayn. He said: 'The Imam is the first whom we know to have mentioned it.' Al-'Iraqi said: 'Rather, the Imam transmitted it from someone else and said: It is something that the later scholars introduced.'
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents the standard classification of taklifi rulings and discusses an additional sixth ruling proposed by al-Subki. The author states that the majority of usul scholars hold that the rulings addressed to the mukallaf are five: permissibility (ibaha) plus the four that fall under command (obligation, recommendation, prohibition, dislike). Al-Subki added a sixth: khilaf al-awla (contrary to the better). The justification: a non-decisive prohibition (nahy ghayr jazim) can be attached to refraining from an act in two ways. If it is by direct indication (dalalat al-mutabaqa), it is considered karaha (dislike). But if it is by implied indication (dalalat al-iltizam), as in the case of a recommended act (like night prayer) implying the prohibition of its opposite (like sleeping all night), then that opposite is called khilaf al-awla, not makruh. The author then notes that al-Subki was followed in this by Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), who was reportedly the first to mention it, though al-'Iraqi claims Imam al-Haramayn transmitted it from earlier scholars who said it was introduced by later ones.

Key Terms
الأحكام التكليفية
الإباحة
خلاف الأولى
دلالة المطابقة
دلالة الالتزام
السبكي
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that khilaf al-awla is the same as makruh (disliked).
Correction: The passage clarifies that khilaf al-awla applies when the prohibition is by implied indication (dalalat al-iltizam), while karaha applies when it is by direct indication (dalalat al-mutabaqa). They are distinct categories.

Checkpoint
According to al-Subki, when does a non-decisive prohibition become 'khilaf al-awla' rather than 'karaha'?
A) When it is by direct indication
B) When it is by implied indication
C) When it is a command to act
D) When it is decisive

Key Takeaway
The taklifi rulings are five, but al-Subki added a sixth—contrary to the better—which applies when a prohibition is indicated indirectly by a recommended act's opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a recommended act like night prayer, do you ever consider that neglecting it (e.g., sleeping all night) might be 'contrary to the better'? How does this awareness shape your nightly choices?
?
Al-Subki added a sixth ruling based on implied meanings. In your own life, do you only follow the direct commands of the Quran and Sunnah, or do you also pay attention to the implied lessons and deeper obligations? What might you be missing?
?
Scholars debated whether khilaf al-awla is a valid category. Are you open to revisiting classifications you take for granted when new evidence or reasoning is presented, or do you resist such changes? What does that reveal about your attachment to your own understanding?

Definition of al-Hukm al-Wad'i (Prescribed Indicative Ruling)
Sanusi defines al-hukm al-wad'i as a prescribed indicator for the five rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what al-hukm al-wad'i is and how it differs from direct commands and prohibitions.

Core Idea
Al-hukm al-wad'i is a signpost pointing to a ruling, not the ruling itself.

In this lesson, we explore the second main category of shar'i rulings: al-hukm al-wad'i, or the prescribed indicative ruling. While the first category (al-taklifi) directly commands or prohibits actions, the wad'i ruling is more subtle—it sets up conditions, causes, or indicators that lead us to know the taklifi ruling. Sanusi defines it plainly so we can distinguish it from what we already learned.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key term 'نصب الشارع أمارة' (the Shari' setting up an indicator). This is the core of the definition.
The passage is incomplete due to page break, but the definition itself is clear.
2
Identify the two equivalent formulations
Sanusi gives two ways to say the same thing: 'عبارة عن نصب الشارع أمارة' and 'عبارة عن جعل الشارع أمراً من الأمور أمارة'. The second clarifies that the indicator can be any thing that the Shari' designates.
3
Contrast with al-hukm al-taklifi
Think back to the previous lesson on al-hukm al-taklifi. Al-hukm al-wad'i does not directly oblige, prohibit, or permit; rather, it provides a sign that leads to one of those five rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْوَضْعُ لَهُمَا : فَهُوَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ نَصْبِ الشَّارِعِ أَمَارَةٌ عَلَى حُكْمٍ مِنْ تِلْكَ الْأَحْكَامِ الْخَمْسَةِ يعني : أنَّ الحكم الوضعيّ : عبارةٌ عن جعل الشارع أمراً مِنَ الأمورِ أمارة على حكمٍ مِنْ تلك الأحكام الخمسة ، سواء كان ذلك الأمر
And as for the prescriptive (al-wad') for them, it is an expression for the Shari' setting up an indicator (amara) for one of those five rulings, meaning: al-hukm al-wad'i is an expression for the Shari' making something from among things an indicator for one of those five rulings, whether that thing is ...
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines the second category of shar'i rulings: al-hukm al-wad'i (the prescriptive or indicative ruling). The definition is given in two equivalent formulations. First: it is the Shari' setting up (nasb) an indicator (amara) for one of the five taklifi rulings (obligation, recommendation, prohibition, dislike, permissibility). Second, it is the Shari' making something an indicator for one of those rulings. The word 'amara' means a sign or evidence. The definition emphasizes that wad'i rulings are not direct commands or prohibitions, but rather they set up conditions, causes, or preventers that indicate or lead to a taklifi ruling. The text begins to mention that this indicator can be something that is ... (the rest is cut off).

Definition of al-Hukm al-Wad'i and the Nature of Amara
Sanusi defines al-hukm al-wad'i and explains the nature of amara.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-hukm al-wad'i as the Lawgiver's appointment of an indicator (amara) upon one of the five rulings, and the key nuance that these indicators are not efficient causes but mere signs.

Core Idea
Allah's rulings are known through indicators, but nothing forces Him to legislate.

Sanusi begins his discussion of the declaratory judgment (al-hukm al-wad'i) by defining it as the Lawgiver's setting up of an indicator (amara) upon one of the five rulings. He immediately clarifies a crucial point: these indicators — whether from human actions or natural phenomena — are not efficient causes that compel Allah to act, but rather signs that allow us to know His rulings. This sets the stage for the later limitation of indicators to cause, condition, and obstacle, which the passage begins but does not complete.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic and translation for the definition. Note that it says 'expression of the Lawgiver's setting up an indicator upon a ruling.' This is the core definition of al-hukm al-wad'i.
The definition uses the word 'amara' meaning a sign or indicator.
2
Understand the nature of the amara
Notice the clarification: the amara is not a cause but a sign. The passage explicitly refutes those who claim that causes or conditions necessitate the ruling. Pay attention to the phrase 'none of them is a motivator for our Master'.
This refutes the Mu'tazila and others who believed in rational obligation upon Allah.
3
See the transition to limitation
The text then states that the amara is limited to cause, condition, and obstacle, but the explanation cuts off after the colon. Recognize that this is only the beginning of the discussion on limitation.
The next pages likely complete the enumeration and explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حد الحكم الوضعي
وَأَمَّا الْوَضْعُ لَهُمَا : فَهُوَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ نَصْبِ الشَّارِعِ أَمَارَةٌ عَلَى حُكْمِ مِنْ تِلْكَ الْأَحْكَامِ الْخَمْسَةِ يعني : أنَّ الحكم الوضعيّ : عبارةٌ عن جعل الشارع أمراً مِنَ الأمورِ أمارة على حكمٍ مِنْ تلك الأحكام الخمسة ، سواء كان ذلك المجعول أمارةً مِنْ أفعال المكلَّفينَ ؛ كجعل السرقة سبباً للقطع ، أو ليسَ مِنْ أفعالهم ؛ كجعل زوال الشمس سبباً لإيجاب صلاة الظهر مثلاً . وقولُه : ( نصبِ الشارع أمارةً ( أشار بلفظ ( أمارةً ) إلى أنَّ أحكامَ اللهِ تعالى ليسَتْ تابعةً للأسباب والشروط والموانع ، بل هذه الأمور أمارات على الأحكام ، نعرفها نحنُ منها لخفائها علينا ، وليس شيء منها باعثاً لمولانا جل وعزَّ على حكم مِنَ الأحكام كما زعم مَنْ ضَلَّ وابتدع .
انحصارُ الأمارة في السبب والشرط والمانع
وَهِيَ : السَّبَبُ ، وَالشَّرْطُ ، وَالْمَانِعُ
الضمير يعود على الأمارة ، ووجه انحصارِ الأمارة في هذهِ الثلاثةِ : أَنَّ ما يجعلُهُ الشرعُ أمارةً على حكم مِنَ الأحكام :
The definition of the declaratory judgment: As for the declaratory [judgment] for them, it is an expression of the Lawgiver's setting up an indicator upon a ruling from those five rulings. Meaning: that the declaratory judgment is an expression of the Lawgiver's making a thing from among things an indicator of a ruling from among those five rulings, whether that which is made an indicator is from the actions of the obligated parties—such as making theft a cause for amputation—or is not from their actions—such as making the sun's zenith a cause for obligation of the noon prayer, for example. And his statement: 'the Lawgiver's setting up an indicator' pointed by the word 'indicator' to the fact that Allah's rulings are not dependent on causes, conditions, and obstacles; rather these things are indicators for the rulings, we know them from them due to their hiddenness from us; and none of them is a motivator for our Master, mighty and sublime, regarding a ruling from among the rulings, as claimed by those who have strayed and innovated. The limitation of the indicator to cause, condition, and obstacle: And they are: cause, condition, and obstacle. The pronoun refers to the indicator; and the reason for the limitation of the indicator to these three is that what the Law makes an indicator of a ruling from among the rulings:
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. It defines al-hukm al-wad'i (the declaratory judgment) as the Lawgiver's appointment of an amara (sign/indicator) upon one of the five rulings (obligation, prohibition, recommendation, dislike, permission). The author first states the definition, then clarifies that the amara may be from the actions of the mukallaf (e.g., theft as a cause for amputation) or from external phenomena (e.g., the sun's zenith as a cause for zuhr prayer). He then stresses that these aamara are not efficient causes; they are mere signs that indicate the ruling to us. The ruling itself does not depend on them in a causal sense. This refutes the Mu'tazila and others who claimed that Allah must legislate according to rational goodness or badness. The passage then transitions to the limitation of the amara: it is confined to three categories: sabab (cause), shart (condition), and mani' (obstacle). The author begins to explain the reasoning but the text breaks off at the colon; the full enumeration and explanation likely continue on a subsequent page.

الأمارة
السبب
الشرط
المانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Causes and conditions are efficient causes that bring about the ruling.
Correction: They are merely indicators (amarat) set by the Lawgiver for our knowledge, not motivating factors for Allah.

Checkpoint

C) The limitation of the indicator to cause, condition, and obstacle.
D) The ruling being dependent on causes and conditions.

Key Takeaway
Al-hukm al-wad'i is the appointment of an indicator by the Lawgiver; these indicators are signs, not causes.

?

?
The author warns against those who claim Allah must legislate according to rational goodness. Are there areas in your life where you feel entitled to a certain outcome because you did 'enough'? Confess that entitlement to Allah now and ask Him to free you from it.

The Limitation of the Legal Sign to Cause, Condition, and Impediment
The author limits the legal sign to three types and explains the classification based on existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic legal theory classifies signs (amara) into cause, condition, and impediment based on the pattern of existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
The legal sign is confined to three categories determined by how existence and non-existence indicate a ruling.

This lesson introduces a foundational classification in Islamic legal theory: the limitation of the legal sign (amara) to three types: cause (sabab), condition (shart), and impediment (mani). The author explains that this classification is exhaustive and based on whether the sign's existence, non-existence, or both serve as indicators for a legal ruling. Understanding this classification is essential for analyzing how the Lawgiver links signs to rulings.

Guided Path
1

Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style of the author.

3

The author explains that the confinement is based on the Lawgiver's patterns of making something a sign. Grasp the logical reasoning behind the three possibilities.
The three patterns correspond to the three categories respectively.

انحصارُ الأمارة في السبب والشرط والمانع
(۲)
وَهِيَ : السَّبَبُ ، وَالشَّرْطُ ، وَالْمَانِعُ ()
الضمير يعود على الأمارة ، ووجه انحصارِ الأمارة في هذهِ الثلاثةِ : أَنَّ ما يجعلُهُ الشرعُ أمارةً على حكم مِنَ الأحكام :
- إمَّا أَنْ يجعلَ كلَّ واحدٍ مِنْ وجودِهِ وعدمِهِ أمارةً ودليلاً
أو يجعل عدمه فقط أمارةً .
أو يجعل وجوده فقط أمارة .
فالأوَّلُ : السببُ ، والثاني : الشرط ، والثالثُ : المانع
The confinement of the sign to cause, condition, and impediment. (2) And they are: the cause, the condition, and the impediment. The pronoun refers back to the sign, and the reason for confining the sign to these three is that what the Lawgiver makes a sign for a ruling: either He makes each one of its existence and non-existence a sign and indicator, or He makes its non-existence only a sign, or He makes its existence only a sign. So the first is the cause, the second is the condition, and the third is the impediment.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage classifies the legal sign (amara) into three categories and provides the rationale for this classification. The author first states that the sign is confined to three: cause (sabab), condition (shart), and impediment (mani). He then explains that the reason for this confinement is that when the Lawgiver makes something a sign for a ruling, He does so in one of three ways: either both the existence and non-existence of that thing are signs of the ruling, or only its non-existence is a sign, or only its existence is a sign. These three possibilities correspond respectively to the cause, condition, and impediment. Thus, the classification is exhaustive and mutually exclusive.

Misconception: One might think that a sign (amara) is itself the cause of the ruling.
Correction: The author clarifies that it is merely an indicator, not an efficient cause.

Checkpoint

A) Cause
B) Condition
C) Impediment
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The legal sign is confined to three exhaustive categories—cause, condition, and impediment—based on whether its existence, non-existence, or both indicate a ruling.

Definition of Cause and Its Explanation
The author defines the cause and explains each part of its definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a cause (sabab) in Islamic legal theory and how it differs from condition, impediment, and proof.

Core Idea
A cause entails both existence and non-existence of the ruling by itself.

This lesson presents the technical definition of a cause in Islamic legal theory. The author carefully distinguishes the cause from related concepts like condition, impediment, and proof by analyzing each part of the definition. Understanding this distinction is crucial for legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the definition has three parts: 'from whose existence the existence follows', 'from whose non-existence the non-existence follows', and 'by itself'.
2
Identify what each part excludes
The author explains that the first phrase excludes condition and impediment; the second excludes proof; the third includes causes that may not always produce the ruling due to external factors.
This is a classic logical definition technique.
3
Understand the summary
The footnote summarizes: for impediment only its existence matters; for condition only its non-existence; for cause both existence and non-existence are considered.
This reinforces the definition.

و(1)
فَالسَّبَبُ : مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ الْوُجُودُ وَمِنْ عَدَمِهِ الْعَدَمُ لِذَاتِهِ؛ كَزَوَالِ الشَّمْسِ لِوُجُوبِ الظُّهْرِ مَثَلاً

(1) وبهذا تعلم : أنَّ المعتبر من المانع وجوده ، ومن الشرط عدمه ، ومن السبب وجوده وعدمُهُ ، وقد وقع في (أ) خلاف هذا ، ولا التفات إليه ، وانظر
شرح تنقيح الفصول » ( ص ۸۲ )
And (1) The cause: that from whose existence the existence necessarily follows and from whose non-existence the non-existence follows, by itself; like the setting of the sun for the obligation of the noon prayer, for example. His statement: (ما) is like a genus. His statement: (يلزم من وجوده الوجود) is a differentia that excludes the condition and the impediment. His statement: (ومن عدمه العدم) excludes the proof for the ruling from the Book, Sunna, consensus, or analogy, because the proof requires its positive implication (i.e., from its existence the existence follows) but not its converse; i.e., from its non-existence the non-existence does not necessarily follow. As for the cause, both its positive and converse implication are required. His statement: (لذاته) includes the cause the existence of which does not necessarily imply [the ruling's] existence. (1) And by this you know that what is considered from the impediment is its existence, from the condition is its non-existence, and from the cause is both its existence and non-existence. And in (A) there is something contrary to this, but no attention is paid to it. See Sharh Tanqih al-Fusul, p. 82.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage provides the technical definition of the cause (sabab) in Islamic legal theory, along with a detailed breakdown of its logical components. The author first gives the definition: 'that from whose existence the existence [of the ruling] necessarily follows and from whose non-existence the non-existence follows, by itself.' He then explains each term: 'ما' is a generic noun; 'يلزم من وجوده الوجود' is the differentia that distinguishes cause from condition and impediment; 'ومن عدمه العدم' distinguishes cause from evidence (dalil), because evidence has positive implication but not converse implication; 'لذاته' includes cases where the existence of the cause does not necessarily imply the ruling's existence (i.e., external factors intervene). The footnote clarifies that for the impediment, only its existence matters; for the condition, only its non-existence; and for the cause, both its existence and non-existence are considered.

Correction: The author emphasizes that a proof only implies the ruling's existence (its positive implication), not its non-existence. A cause implies both.

Definition of 'Shart' and Its Three Types
Sanusi defines 'shart' and classifies it into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'shart' (condition) in Islamic logic and how it is classified into three types with examples.

Core Idea
A condition is necessary but not sufficient for its effect.

Sanusi begins by defining the term 'shart' (condition) both linguistically and technically. He then explains that a condition does not guarantee the occurrence of the conditioned; it is merely a prerequisite. This lesson will walk through the three categories of conditions and their logical relationship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the examples.
The passage uses a standard pattern: definition, then enumeration with examples.
2
Understand the linguistic and technical definition
Note that 'shart' in language means a sign; in technical usage, it is the same concept. The author quotes the Qur'anic phrase 'ashrat al-sa'ah' to illustrate.
This shows that the term has a consistent core meaning.
3
Analyze each type of condition
For each type (rational, customary, legal), identify the example and the logical relationship. Observe that the condition is necessary but not sufficient.
The condition's absence causes the absence of the conditioned; its presence does not guarantee the conditioned's presence due to other factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرطُ في اللغة : هو العلامة ، ومنه : أشراط الساعة ؛ أي : علاماتها وأما في الاصطلاح : فمعناه ما ذكرَ ، وهو ينقسم إلى : شرط عقليّ ، وشرط عادي ، وشرط شرعي مثال الشرطِ العقليّ : الحياة للإدراكِ ؛ فإنَّهُ يلزمُ مِنْ عدم الحياةِ عدم الإدراك ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِ الحياةِ وجود الإدراك ولا عدمه ؛ لأنَّه قد تُوجدُ الحياة ويكونُ معَها غَيبةٌ بنوم أو إغماء أو جنون ، حتى لا يدرك الحيُّ معَ هذه الآفاتِ شيئاً أصلاً ومثال الشرطِ العاديّ : النطفة في الرحم للولادة ؛ فإنَّهُ يلزمُ مِنْ نفي النطفة في الرحم نفي الولادة ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِ النطفة في الرحم وجود ولادةٍ ولا عدمُها ؛ لأنَّه بعد أنْ تُوجد في الرحم قد يكون الله تعالى منها ولادة وقد لا يكونُ . ومثال الشرطِ الشرعيّ : الطهارة لصحة الصلاة ، وتمام الحولِ لوجوب الزكاة في العين والماشية مثلاً ؛ فإنَّهُ يلزمُ مِنْ نفي الطهارة معَ القدرة على تحصيلها عدم صحة الصلاةِ ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ حصول الطهارة صحة الصلاة ولا عدمها ؛ لإمكان فسادِها بعد حصول الطهارة باختلالِ ركن من أركانها ونحو ذلك . وكذا يلزمُ مِنْ عدم تمام الحولِ عدم وجوب الزكاة في العين والماشية ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ حصولِ تمام الحول وجوب الزكاة فيهما ؛ لتوقفه على سبب ؛ وهو ملكُ النصاب ملكاً كاملاً ، وزيادة مجيء الساعي في الماشية إن جرَتِ العادة بمجيئه ، ونفي مانعِ الدَّيْنِ في العين دونَ الماشية ، ونفي مانع الرق والكفر فيهما
The condition in language is the sign, and from it is 'the signs of the Hour', meaning its signs. As for in technical terminology, its meaning is what was mentioned, and it divides into: rational condition, customary condition, and legal condition. An example of the rational condition is life for perception; for it necessarily follows from the absence of life the absence of perception, and it does not necessarily follow from the existence of life the existence of perception nor its absence; because life may exist and with it there is an absence due to sleep, fainting, or insanity, such that the living being does not perceive anything at all with these defects. And an example of the customary condition is the sperm in the womb for childbirth; for it necessarily follows from the negation of the sperm in the womb the negation of childbirth, and it does not necessarily follow from the existence of the sperm in the womb the existence of childbirth nor its absence; because after it exists in the womb, God Almighty may create from it a birth or not. And an example of the legal condition is purity for the validity of prayer, and the completion of the year for the obligation of zakat on gold/silver and livestock for instance; for it necessarily follows from the negation of purity while being able to obtain it the invalidity of prayer, and it does not necessarily follow from obtaining purity the validity of prayer nor its invalidity; because of the possibility of its corruption after obtaining purity due to a defect in one of its pillars or the like. Likewise, it necessarily follows from the non-completion of the year the non-obligation of zakat on gold/silver and livestock, and it does not necessarily follow from the completion of the year the obligation of zakat on them; because it is dependent upon a cause, which is complete ownership of the nisab, and additionally the coming of the collector in livestock if it is customary for him to come, and the negation of the preventer of debt in gold/silver but not in livestock, and the negation of the preventer of slavery and disbelief in both.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage defines 'shart' (condition) and classifies it into three types. The author begins by giving the linguistic meaning: 'shart' is a sign or mark, citing the Qur'anic phrase 'ashrat al-sa'ah' (signs of the Hour). Then he gives the technical meaning, which is the same as mentioned, and immediately divides it into three: rational ('aqli), customary ('adi), and legal (shar'i). For each, he provides an example and explains the logical relationship: the condition is necessary but not sufficient. For the rational condition, life is necessary for perception; without life there is no perception, but life alone does not guarantee perception because of interfering factors (sleep, fainting, insanity). For the customary condition, sperm in the womb is necessary for childbirth, but childbirth does not always follow because God may not create it. For the legal condition, purity is necessary for valid prayer, but purity does not guarantee validity because other conditions or pillars may be missing; similarly, the completion of the year is necessary for zakat obligation, but other factors (ownership of nisab, absence of debt, etc.) are also required. Thus the passage establishes that a condition is a sine qua non but not a sufficient cause.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a condition guarantees the conditioned; that is, if the condition is present, the conditioned must occur.
Correction: The author shows that the condition is merely necessary, not sufficient. The conditioned may not occur even if the condition is present because of other factors (preventers or missing causes).

Checkpoint

B) The condition is necessary but not sufficient for the conditioned.
C) The condition is sufficient but not necessary for the conditioned.
D) The condition has no logical relationship to the conditioned.

Key Takeaway
A condition is necessary for the conditioned, but the conditioned may not occur even if the condition is present.

How does this lesson's distinction between necessary and sufficient challenge that attitude? What will you change starting with your next prayer?

Why a Condition Does Not Necessitate Its Conditioned by Essence
Sanusi explains that the conditional relationship is not by essence but by concomitant factors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a condition (shart) does not by its essence necessitate the existence or non-existence of the conditioned (mashrut); rather, other factors—cause and preventer—determine the outcome.

Core Idea
A condition is not a cause; it only makes the effect possible.

This passage clarifies a subtle but critical point in kalam logic: the difference between a necessary condition and an efficient cause. Sanusi uses the example of zakat to show that the condition (completion of the year) does not by itself produce obligation; it requires a cause (ownership) and absence of preventers (debt). Understanding this distinction prevents confusion when analyzing theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two statements
Sanusi is clarifying two statements about conditions. Find where he discusses 'it does not necessarily follow from its existence existence nor non-existence' and 'what necessarily follows from its non-existence is non-existence'.
These two statements are the main focus of the passage.
2
Follow the zakat example
Notice how Sanusi breaks down the example: completion of the year is the condition, complete ownership of nisab is the cause, and debt is the preventer. Observe how each factor affects the obligation of zakat.
3
Understand why the condition alone does not produce the effect
Sanusi argues that the existence of the condition does not by itself necessitate the conditioned because causes and preventers can accompany it. The conditioned may exist when cause is present and preventer absent, or not exist when preventer is present.
This is the key insight: the condition is necessary but not sufficient.
4
Contrast with the first statement
Sanusi then reminds us that the non-existence of the condition always entails the non-existence of the conditioned. This is true by the essence of the condition itself, unlike the first statement which depends on external factors.
This asymmetry is important in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولنا : ( لذاتِهِ ( راجع للجملة الأخيرة ، وهي قولنا : ( ولا يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِهِ وجودٌ ولا عدمٌ ( لأنَّ وجود الشرط هو الذي قد يتفقُ أَنْ يصحبه وجود مانعِ ، فيلزمُ فيهِ عدمُ المشروط حينئذ ، لكن لا بالنظر إلى ذاتِ الشرط ، بل بالنظر إلى ذاتِ المانع وهو وقد يصحب وجودَهُ وجود السبب ونفي المانع ، فيلزم حينئذٍ مِنْ وجودِهِ وجود المشروط ؛ كما لو صاحب تمام الحول وجود السبب ؛ ملك النصابِ مِلْكاً كاملاً ، ونفي المانعِ الذي هو الدَّيْنُ ، فيلزمُ حينئذٍ وجوب الزكاة ، لكن لم تجب بالنظر إلى ذاتِ الشرط الذي هو تمام الحولِ ، وإِنَّما وجبَتْ بسبب ما قارنَهُ مِنْ وجودِ سببِ الزكاة ونفي مانعها ، ولو صاحبَ تمامَ الحول وجود المانع الذي هو الدَّيْنُ مثلاً . لزم معَهُ عدمُ الزكاةِ ، لكنْ ليسَ بالنظر إليهِ لزم عدمُها ، بل بالنظر إلى المانع الذي هو الدَّيْنُ . وأمَّا الجملة الأولى - وهي قولنا : ( ما يلزمُ مِنْ عدمِهِ العدم ) - فمعناها لازم للشرط على كل حال ، فلو قيدناه بذاتِ الشرط
And our saying: 'by its essence' refers to the last sentence, which is our saying: 'and it does not necessarily follow from its existence existence nor non-existence'; because the existence of the condition is something that may happen to be accompanied by the existence of a preventer, so the non-existence of the conditioned necessarily follows then, but not with respect to the essence of the condition, but with respect to the essence of the preventer. And it, and it may be accompanied by the existence of the cause and the negation of the preventer, so from its existence the existence of the conditioned necessarily follows then; as if the completion of the year is accompanied by the existence of the cause — complete ownership of the nisab — and the negation of the preventer which is debt, so then the obligation of zakat necessarily follows, but it is not obligated with respect to the essence of the condition which is the completion of the year; rather it is obligated due to what accompanied it of the existence of the cause of zakat and the negation of its preventer. And if the completion of the year is accompanied by the existence of the preventer such as debt, then the non-existence of zakat necessarily follows with it, but it is not with respect to it that its non-existence follows, but with respect to the preventer which is debt. And as for the first sentence — which is our saying: 'what necessarily follows from its non-existence is non-existence' — its meaning is necessary for the condition in every case, so if we restrict it by the essence of the condition...
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed logical clarification about the relationship between a condition and its conditioned. The author addresses two specific statements: (1) 'It does not necessarily follow from the existence of the condition existence nor non-existence' and (2) 'From the non-existence of the condition, non-existence follows'. He explains that the first statement is not by the essence of the condition (lidhatihi) but due to accompanying factors. For example, completion of the year (hawl) is a condition for zakat. By itself, it does not necessitate zakat obligation because a cause (complete ownership of nisab) and absence of preventers (debt) must also be present. If a preventer exists, the conditioned is absent, but that absence is attributed to the preventer, not to the condition's essence. The second statement, however, is always true of the condition by its essence: the non-existence of the condition always entails the non-existence of the conditioned. This distinction is crucial for understanding the role of conditions in kalam arguments—they are not efficient causes but necessary prerequisites.

سبب
مشروط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the condition is the cause of the conditioned.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the condition does not produce the effect by itself; other factors (cause and preventer) are decisive. The condition is necessary but not sufficient.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does the condition not necessitate the conditioned by its essence?
A) Because the condition is always accompanied by a preventer.
B) Because the existence of the conditioned depends on the presence of a cause and absence of a preventer, not on the condition alone.
C) Because the condition is defined by language, not logic.
D) Because the condition is a sign, not a cause.

Key Takeaway
A condition alone does not necessitate its conditioned; cause and preventer are the decisive factors.

Definition of al-Mani' and Its Two Types
Sanusi defines the preventer (al-mani') and divides it into two kinds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a preventer (al-mani') in kalam and legal theory and its division into two kinds based on what it conflicts with.

Core Idea
A preventer blocks existence, but its absence guarantees nothing.

In this lesson, we examine Sanusi's formal definition of the preventer (al-mani'), a key concept for understanding how conditions and obstacles interact in determining obligations. He not only defines it precisely but also classifies preventers into two distinct types, each with its own example. This foundation is essential for later discussions on causation and legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition of al-mani' and its translation. Note the logical structure: existence of the preventer implies non-existence of the thing, but its absence implies nothing.
The definition uses technical terms: وجود, عدم, يلزم.
2
Examine the example of menstruation
Sanusi gives menstruation as an example of a preventer for the obligation of prayer. Consider how this illustrates the definition.
The example shows that when menstruation exists, prayer is not obligatory; but when it is absent, prayer may still not be obligatory due to other conditions.
3
Identify the two types of preventer
The passage divides preventers into two types. Find the criterion for division (what the preventer conflicts with) and note the examples given for the first type.
The first type conflicts with the cause; the second type conflicts with the thing itself.
4
Analyze the example of debt in zakat
Debt prevents zakat because it undermines complete ownership, which is the cause. This illustrates the first type.
The second type's example (slavery) is incomplete in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمَانِعُ : مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ الْعَدَمُ ، وَلا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ عَدَمِهِ وُجُودُ وَلا عَدَمٌ لِذَاتِهِ ؛ كَالْحَيْضِ لِوُجُوبِ الصَّلاةِ .
المانعُ مِنَ الشيء على ضربين :
أحدهما : أنْ يمنع منه لمنافاتِهِ لسببِهِ .
الثاني : أن يمنع منه لمنافاتِهِ لهُ في نفسِهِ .
مثالُ الأَوَّلِ : الدَّيْنُ في زكاة العين ؛ فإنَّهُ يمنعُ مِنْ وجوبها ؛ لمنافاته لسببها الذي هو الملكُ الكامل للنصابِ ، ومثلُهُ الرقُ ; فإِنَّ
The preventer (al-mani'): that from whose existence non-existence necessarily follows, and from whose non-existence neither existence nor non-existence necessarily follows in itself (lidhatihi); like menstruation for the obligation of prayer. The preventer of a thing is of two types: One of them: that it prevents it due to its conflict with its cause. The second: that it prevents it due to its conflict with it in itself. The example of the first: debt in the zakat of wealth (al-'ayn); for it prevents its obligation due to its conflict with its cause, which is complete ownership of the nisab; and like it is slavery, for indeed...
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage provides a formal definition of the preventer (al-mani') in the context of legal theory (usul al-fiqh) and kalam reasoning. The definition states that a preventer is something whose existence necessarily entails the non-existence of the thing it prevents, but from whose non-existence neither the existence nor the non-existence of that thing necessarily follows in itself (lidhatihi). This means that the absence of the preventer does not guarantee the existence of the thing, because other factors like the absence of the cause or presence of another preventer could still block it. The example given is menstruation, which prevents the obligation of prayer: when menstruation exists, prayer is not obligatory, but when menstruation is absent, prayer may still not be obligatory if other conditions are not met. The passage then divides preventers into two types: (1) a preventer that blocks a thing because it conflicts with its cause, e.g., debt in the zakat on hidden wealth (al-'ayn) because it undermines complete ownership, which is the cause of zakat; (2) a preventer that blocks a thing because it conflicts with the thing itself in its essence. The example for the first type is given, but the second type's example (slavery) is only begun and cut off at the end of the page. The definition and classification are crucial for understanding how conditions (shurut) and preventers (manani') interact in determining legal obligations.

Misconception: That the absence of a preventer makes the thing obligatory or existent.
Correction: The definition clarifies that from the non-existence of the preventer, nothing necessarily follows regarding the existence or non-existence of the thing, because other factors like the presence of the cause or absence of other preventers still matter.

Checkpoint

A) The non-existence of the thing it prevents.
B) The existence of the thing it prevents.
C) Neither existence nor non-existence.
D) The existence of the cause.

Key Takeaway
A preventer blocks the existence of something, but its absence does not guarantee that thing's existence.

If the absence of a preventer never guarantees the presence of good, what will you actively pursue this week to ensure the cause of obedience is alive in your heart?

Definition of al-Mani': The Logical Preventer
Sanusi defines al-mani' with a logical formula and gives an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of al-mani' in usul al-fiqh and its logical relationship to cause, condition, and effect.

Core Idea
A preventer's absence does not directly cause existence; it only allows other factors to operate.

Sanusi begins by establishing a clear technical term: al-mani', the preventer. This is not a mere obstacle but a concept with a specific logical structure: its existence blocks something, but its absence does not automatically produce that thing. Understanding this definition is essential before we can classify its types.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition of al-mani' and its translation. Notice the three logical possibilities: existence leads to non-existence; non-existence does not lead to either existence or non-existence.
The phrase 'li-dhatihi' (for its own essence) will be explained later.
2
Analyze the logical formula
Break down the definition: (1) If the preventer exists, the thing does not exist. (2) If the preventer does not exist, nothing follows necessarily from that alone.
This contrasts with a cause (sabab), where absence necessarily leads to absence.
3
Consider the example
Menstruation prevents the obligation of prayer. If a woman is menstruating, prayer is not obligatory. But when menstruation ends, prayer does not automatically become obligatory—other conditions (like time, purification) are needed.
This example anchors the abstract definition in a concrete ruling.
4
Reflect on why this matters
Ask yourself: How does this preventer concept help us understand when rulings apply? It shows that absence of an obstacle is not enough; we need the presence of cause and condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حد المانع
وَالْمَانِعُ : مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ الْعَدَمُ ، وَلا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ عَدَمِهِ وُجُودُ وَلا عَدَمٌ لِذَاتِهِ ; كَالْحَيْضِ لِوُجُوبِ الصَّلاةِ .
المانعُ مِنَ الشيء على ضربين :
أحدهما : أنْ يمنع منه لمنافاتِهِ لسببِهِ
الثاني : أن يمنع منه لمنافاتِهِ لهُ في نفسِهِ
مثالُ الأَوَّلِ : الدَّيْنُ في زكاة العين ; فإنَّهُ يمنعُ مِنْ وجوبها ; لمنافاته لسببها الذي هو الملكُ الكامل للنصابِ ، ومثلُهُ الرقُ ; فإِنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ مِنَ الدَّيْنِ والرقّ مانعٌ مِنْ كمال التصرُّف في المالِ ، فلم يثبت معهما الغنى بذلك المال الذي هو حكمة وجوب الزكاة فيه ; كما قالَ النبيُّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : « خُذْهَا مِنْ أَغْنِيَائِهِمْ ، وَرُدَّهَا عَلَىٰ فُقَرَائِهِمْ »
ومثال الثاني : الكفرُ مثلاً بالنسبة إلى صحة الصلاةِ ; فإنَّهُ مانع مِنْ صحَتِها لا لمنافاتِهِ لسببها مِنْ دخول الوقتِ ، بل لمنافاته لها في نفسها ; إذْ لا يمكنُ معَ الكفرِ التقرُّبُ بها إلى المولى تبارك وتعالى ، وهذا معنى قولِ الأصوليين : ( المانعُ ينقسم : إلى مانع السبب وإلى مانع الحكم ) وقولنا أيضاً في حد المانع : ( لذاته ) راجع للجملة الأخيرة ; وهي قولنا : ( ولا يلزمُ مِنْ عدمِهِ وجودٌ ولا عدم ) لأن عدم المانع أيضاً هو الذي يتَّفقُ أن يصحبَهُ وجود السبب والشرطِ ، فيلزم حينئذٍ مِنْ عدمِهِ الوجود ، للكنْ ليسَ ذاتُ عدمِهِ هي التي اقتضت الوجود ، بل ذلك الذي اقتضاه اجتماع السبب مع الشرط عند عدم المانع ، وقد يصحب عدم المانعِ عدمُ السبب أو عدم الشرط ، فيلزم حينئذ العدمُ ، لكن ليس لذات عدم المانع ، بل لمصاحبة عدم السبب أو عدم الشرط
The Preventer: that which, from its existence, nonexistence necessarily follows, and from its nonexistence, neither existence nor nonexistence necessarily follows for its own essence; like menstruation for the obligation of prayer. The preventer of a thing is of two types: First: that it prevents due to its conflict with its cause. Second: that it prevents due to its conflict with it in itself. Example of the first: debt in the zakat of cash assets; for it prevents its obligation due to its conflict with its cause which is complete ownership of the nisab, and likewise is slavery; for each of debt and slavery prevents complete disposal of wealth, so wealth which is the wisdom of the obligation of zakat is not established with them; as the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Take it from their rich and give it to their poor.' Example of the second: disbelief, for example, regarding the validity of prayer; for it prevents its validity not due to its conflict with its cause of the entry of time, but due to its conflict with it in itself; since with disbelief it is impossible to draw near through it to the Lord Blessed and Exalted. And this is the meaning of the statement of the usuliyyun: 'The preventer is divided into preventer of the cause and preventer of the ruling.' And our statement also in the definition of the preventer: 'for its own essence' refers back to the last sentence, which is our statement: 'and from its nonexistence, neither existence nor nonexistence necessarily follows'; because the absence of the preventer is also that which happens to be accompanied by the existence of the cause and condition, so then from its nonexistence existence necessarily follows, but it is not the essence of its absence that necessitated existence, but rather that which is necessitated by the combination of the cause with the condition in the absence of the preventer; and the absence of the preventer may be accompanied by the absence of the cause or the absence of the condition, so then non-existence necessarily follows, but not due to the essence of the absence of the preventer, but due to the accompaniment of the absence of the cause or the absence of the condition.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of al-mani' (the preventer) in the context of Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh), but it is presented within a kalam work. It begins with a formal definition: al-mani' is that whose existence necessarily entails the non-existence of something, but whose non-existence does not necessarily entail either existence or non-existence of that thing for its own essence. This means the absence of a preventer does not by itself produce an effect; any resulting existence depends on other factors. The example given is menstruation preventing prayer: menstruation's existence prevents prayer, but its absence does not automatically obligate prayer (other conditions must be met). The passage then divides al-mani' into two types: (1) a preventer that conflicts with the cause (sabab) of the thing, and (2) a preventer that conflicts with the thing itself. For the first type, debt prevents the obligation of zakat because it conflicts with the cause (complete ownership of the nisab). Similarly, slavery prevents complete disposal of wealth. The hadith is quoted to support that zakat is taken from the rich. For the second type, disbelief prevents the validity of prayer because it conflicts with prayer itself, not its cause (time). The phrase 'for its own essence' (li-dhatihi) in the definition is then clarified: when the preventer is absent, existence may follow due to the conjunction of cause and condition, not due to the absence itself. Conversely, when the preventer is absent but cause or condition is absent, non-existence follows from that absence, not from the absence of the preventer. This underscores the neutral nature of the preventer's absence.

المانع
السبب
الشرط
الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that removing a preventer automatically causes the effect to happen.
Correction: The passage explains that the absence of a preventer does not necessitate existence; it only allows the cause and conditions to produce the effect.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's definition of al-mani', which of the following is true?
A) The existence of the preventer does not affect the thing.
B) The non-existence of the preventer necessarily leads to the existence of the thing.
C) The existence of the preventer necessarily leads to the non-existence of the thing.
D) The non-existence of the preventer necessarily leads to the non-existence of the thing.

Key Takeaway
The preventer is defined by its existence blocking an effect; its absence is neutral, not causative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own acts of worship, when an obstacle is removed, do you automatically assume you are now obligated and worthy? How does this lesson teach you to examine whether the true cause is present, not just the absence of a preventer?

When you face a spiritual failure, do you blame the absence of a condition or the presence of a preventer? Which of these is truly within your control? How can you actively seek the cause of obedience instead of merely removing obstacles?

Two Types of al-Mani': Preventer of Cause vs. Preventer of Ruling
Sanusi divides al-mani' into two types and clarifies the definition's nuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a preventer that conflicts with the cause of a ruling and a preventer that conflicts with the ruling itself, using the examples of debt in zakat and disbelief in prayer.

Core Idea
A preventer can block a ruling either by undermining its cause or by directly contradicting the ruling itself.

After defining al-mani' in general, Sanusi now divides it into two specific types. This classification is crucial for applying the concept correctly: sometimes the preventer attacks the cause (like debt conflicting with complete ownership for zakat), and sometimes it attacks the ruling directly (like disbelief conflicting with the very act of prayer). The clarification about 'li-dhatihi' ties it all together.

Guided Path
1
Read the division and examples
Read the text that introduces the two types: 'al-mani' min al-shay' 'ala darbayn...' and the two examples. Notice how each type is defined.
The first type is 'preventer of the cause' (mani' al-sabab), the second is 'preventer of the ruling' (mani' al-hukm).
2
Analyze the first type: debt and zakat
Debt prevents zakat because it conflicts with the cause of zakat: complete ownership of the nisab. Debt reduces ownership. Similarly, slavery prevents complete disposal of wealth.
The hadith about taking zakat from the rich is used to show that the wisdom (hikma) of zakat is wealth.
3
Analyze the second type: disbelief and prayer
Disbelief prevents the validity of prayer not because it conflicts with the cause (time entered), but because it conflicts with the act of prayer itself—a disbeliever cannot sincerely draw near to Allah.
This shows the preventer attacks the ruling (hukm) directly.
4
Understand the clarification about 'li-dhatihi'
Sanusi explains that the phrase 'li-dhatihi' in the definition refers to the last part: the absence of the preventer does not necessitate anything by itself. He uses an example: when the preventer is absent, if cause and condition are present, existence follows—but that is due to them, not the absence. If cause or condition is absent, non-existence follows from that, not the preventer's absence.
This reinforces the logical role of the preventer.

The Preventer: that which, from its existence, nonexistence necessarily follows, and from its nonexistence, neither existence nor nonexistence necessarily follows for its own essence; like menstruation for the obligation of prayer. Definition of al-Mani' and a Scholarly Disagreement
The author defines al-mani' and discusses a disagreement among usul scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-mani' (preventer) in usul al-fiqh, its two types, and the scholarly disagreement about whether it can cause non-existence when the cause is absent.

Core Idea
Al-mani' is defined as that whose existence necessitates non-existence, but its absence does not necessitate either existence or non-existence.

This lesson delves into the concept of al-mani' (the preventer) as defined by usul scholars. You will see how examples like debt and slavery prevent zakat, and disbelief prevents prayer, illustrate the preventer's role. Then you will explore a nuanced disagreement about the preventer's effect when both the preventer and the cause are absent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses two distinct examples and then moves to technical definition.
2
Identify the definition parts
Find the two clauses of the definition: 'that from whose existence non-existence necessarily follows' and 'from whose absence neither existence nor non-existence necessarily follows.' Understand what each means.
The first clause is always true; the second clause explains that absence of the preventer is not a direct cause.
3
Examine the scholarly disagreement
Look at the hypothetical situation where the preventer exists but the cause is also absent. Two views are presented: one attributes non-existence to the preventer only if the cause is present, the other attributes it regardless. See which view the author supports.
The author's definition supports the view that non-existence follows from existence of the preventer even without the cause.
4
Reflect on the practical implications
Consider why this matters in fiqh: when a preventer like menstruation coincides with absence of prayer time, should we say prayer is invalid due to the preventer? The answer affects how rulings are derived.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلَّ واحدٍ مِنَ الدَّيْنِ والرقّ مانعٌ مِنْ كمال التصرُّف في المالِ ، فلم يثبت معهما الغنى بذلك المال الذي هو حكمة وجوب الزكاة فيه ؛ كما قالَ النبيُّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : « خُذْهَا مِنْ أَغْنِيَائِهِمْ ، وَرُدَّهَا عَلَىٰ فُقَرَائِهِمْ » (١) ومثال الثاني : الكفرُ مثلاً بالنسبة إلى صحة الصلاةِ ؛ فإنَّهُ مانع مِنْ صحَتِها لا لمنافاتِهِ لسببها مِنْ دخول الوقتِ ، بل لمنافاته لها في نفسها ؛ إذْ لا يمكنُ معَ الكفرِ التقرُّبُ بها إلى المولى تبارك وتعالى ، وهذا معنى قولِ الأصوليين : ( المانعُ ينقسم : إلى مانع السبب وإلى مانع الحكم ) (٢) وقولنا أيضاً في حد المانع : ( لذاته ) راجع للجملة الأخيرة ؛ وهي قولنا : ( ولا يلزمُ مِنْ عدمِهِ وجودٌ ولا عدم ) لأن عدم المانع أيضاً هو الذي يتَّفقُ أن يصحبَهُ وجود السبب والشرطِ ، فيلزم حينئذٍ مِنْ عدمِهِ الوجود ، للكنْ ليسَ ذاتُ عدمِهِ هي التي اقتضت الوجود ، بل ذلك الذي اقتضاه اجتماع السبب مع الشرط عند عدم المانع ، وقد يصحب عدم المانعِ عدمُ السبب أو عدم الشرط ، فيلزم حينئذ العدمُ ، لكن ليس لذاتِ عدم المانع ، بل لمصاحبة عدم السبب أو عدم الشرط وأما الجملة الأولى - وهي قولنا : ( ما يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِهِ العدمُ ) - فمعناها لازم للمانع على كل حال واختلف الأصوليون : إذا قارن وجود المانع عدم السبب ؛ كان يقارن الحيض مثلاً عدم دخول الوقتِ .. هل يُعلَّلُ عدم الحكم بوجودِ المانع وإن انتفى أيضاً لعدم السببِ ؛ لأنَّ الأماراتِ أدلَّةٌ يصح تعدُّدُها ، أو لا يصح تعليلُ العدم به إلا حيثُ يُوجد السبب المقتضي للحكم ؛ إذ الذي يتبادرُ مِنْ معنى المانعِ أنَّ المقتضي للحكمِ موجود ، لكن انتفى الحكم لوجودِ المانع ؟ وهذا رأي الفخرِ (١) ، والأوَّلُ مختارُ ابن الحاجب وجماعة (٢) ، وهو الذي يُؤخذُ مِنْ حدِّنا للمانع ؛ لأنَّ :قولنا : ( يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِهِ العدمُ ( شامل لما إذا وُجِدَ المقتضي أو فُقدَ . وبالجملة : فقد جعلناه ملزوماً للعدمِ في كلا الحالين ، وهذا هو عينُ القولِ الأَوَّلِ ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق .
Each one of debt and slavery is a preventer from complete disposal of wealth, so wealth is not established with them by that wealth which is the wisdom of the obligation of zakat in it; as the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Take it from their rich and give it to their poor.' (1) And an example of the second: disbelief for example with respect to the validity of prayer; for it is a preventer of its validity not because it conflicts with its cause of the entry of time, but because it conflicts with it in itself; since it is not possible with disbelief to draw near to the Lord (blessed and exalted) through it. And this is the meaning of the statement of the usul scholars: 'The preventer is divided into a preventer of the cause and a preventer of the ruling.' (2) And our statement also in the definition of the preventer: 'due to its essence' refers to the last clause; namely our statement: 'And from its absence neither existence nor non-existence necessarily follows.' Because the absence of the preventer is also that which happens to be accompanied by the existence of the cause and condition, so then from its absence existence necessarily follows, but it is not the essence of its absence that necessitates existence, rather that which necessitates it is the combination of the cause with the condition when the preventer is absent. And sometimes the absence of the preventer is accompanied by the absence of the cause or the absence of the condition, so then non-existence necessarily follows, but not because of the essence of the absence of the preventer, but because of the accompaniment of the absence of the cause or the absence of the condition. As for the first clause – namely our statement: 'That from whose existence non-existence necessarily follows' – its meaning is necessary for the preventer in every case. And the usul scholars differed: when the existence of the preventer coincides with the absence of the cause – such as menstruation coinciding with the absence of the entry of time – is the non-existence of the ruling attributed to the existence of the preventer even if it also is negated due to the absence of the cause, because the indications are proofs whose multiplicity is valid; or is it not valid to attribute the non-existence to it except where the cause requiring the ruling exists, since what comes to mind from the meaning of the preventer is that the requisite of the ruling is present, but the ruling is negated due to the existence of the preventer? This is the view of al-Razi (1), and the first is the choice of Ibn al-Hajib and a group (2), and it is what is taken from our definition of the preventer; because our statement: 'That from whose existence non-existence necessarily follows' includes both when the requisite is present and when it is absent. In summary: we have made it a necessary condition for non-existence in both cases, and this is exactly the first opinion. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of al-mani' (the preventer) in usul al-fiqh and then discusses a scholarly disagreement about its application. The author begins by giving two examples of al-mani': debt and slavery prevent complete disposal of wealth, so zakat is not obligatory on such wealth because the person is not considered wealthy; this is an example of a preventer. The second example is disbelief with respect to the validity of prayer: disbelief prevents prayer from being valid not because it conflicts with the cause (entry of time), but because it conflicts with the act of prayer itself, since a disbeliever cannot draw near to Allah through it. The author then cites the usul scholars' division: al-mani' is divided into man' al-sabab (preventer of the cause) and man' al-hukm (preventer of the ruling). The author clarifies that the phrase 'due to its essence' (li-dhatihi) in the definition refers to the second part of the definition, which states: 'from its absence neither existence nor non-existence necessarily follows.' The author explains why: the absence of the preventer may coincide with the presence of the cause and condition, leading to existence, but this existence is due to the cause and condition, not to the absence of the preventer itself. Similarly, its absence may coincide with the absence of the cause or condition, leading to non-existence, but again not because of its absence per se. The first part of the definition—'that from whose existence non-existence necessarily follows'—is always true for the preventer. The author then presents a controversial case: when the preventer (e.g., menstruation) exists simultaneously with the absence of the cause (e.g., entry of time), can we attribute the non-existence of the ruling (prayer not being obligatory) to the preventer alone, even though the cause is also absent? Two opinions are given: al-Razi held that we cannot attribute it to the preventer unless the cause is present, because the preventer's meaning implies that its presence cancels the ruling only when the requisite is there. The other view, chosen by Ibn al-Hajib and others (and supported by the author's definition), holds that it is valid because the definition 'from whose existence non-existence necessarily follows' applies whether the requisite is present or absent. The author concludes that the definition supports the first view (that the preventer necessitates non-existence in both cases) and that this is exactly the chosen opinion.

Key Terms
المانع
المقتضي
السبب
الشرط
الحكم
الأصوليون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the absence of a preventer automatically causes the existence of the ruling.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the absence of a preventer does not necessitate existence; existence only follows when both the cause and condition are present.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the existence of a preventer coincides with the absence of the cause, which view does the author's definition of al-mani' support?
A) Non-existence of the ruling should not be attributed to the preventer because the cause is absent.
B) Non-existence of the ruling can be attributed to the preventer whether the cause is present or absent.
C) Non-existence of the ruling is attributed only to the absence of the cause.
D) The preventer is irrelevant when the cause is absent.

Key Takeaway
The definition of al-mani' is that its existence always leads to non-existence, but its absence does not guarantee existence; the scholarly disagreement concerns whether the preventer alone can explain non-existence when the cause is also absent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a spiritual failure—like a prayer that felt empty or a sin you keep committing—do you attribute it to an external obstacle (a 'man'') or do you examine whether the internal cause and conditions (like sincerity, presence of heart) were truly present? How does this lesson teach you to look deeper?
?
Is there an area of your life where you assumed that simply removing an 'obstacle' would automatically bring success? How does the author's point that 'absence of the preventer does not necessitate existence' force you to reconsider your reliance on removing obstacles without seeking the true cause? What will you do differently starting today?
?
The scholars debated whether the preventer alone explains non-existence when the cause is also absent. Reflect on your own excuses: do you blame circumstances or others for your shortcomings while ignoring the deeper absence of the necessary effort or condition within yourself? Will you take ownership and actively seek the cause and condition after removing obstacles?
