Examples of Habitual Condition and Obstacle
Sanusi gives examples of the habitual condition and obstacle and relates them to the habitual cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses concrete examples to clarify the concepts of habitual condition, habitual obstacle, and their relation to the habitual cause.

Core Idea
Habitual conditions, obstacles, and causes are interconnected through the example of eating and satiety.

Sanusi now moves from definitions to concrete examples. He illustrates the habitual condition and obstacle with a relatable case: achieving satiety through eating. This passage shows how these terms function together and ultimately refer back to the habitual cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the three distinct parts: example of condition, example of obstacle, and the explanation linking them to the cause.
The passage is concise; each sentence introduces a separate concept.
2
Identify the habitual condition
Find where Sanusi defines the habitual condition: safety from canine appetite (extreme hunger) is a condition for satiety. Reflect on why this is a 'habitual' condition—it is a normal prerequisite, not a logical necessity.
The condition is something that must be present for the cause to produce its effect in the usual course of things.
3
Identify the habitual obstacle
The habitual obstacle is the opposite: canine appetite itself prevents satiety. Understand that an obstacle is something that, if present, blocks the cause from producing the effect.
4
Connect to the habitual cause
Sanusi explains that all these examples relate to the habitual cause: eating staple food. The existence or non-existence of the cause (eating) determines the effect (satiety) or its opposite (hunger).
This shows that conditions and obstacles are relative to a specific cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال الشرط العاديّ : السلامةُ مِنَ الشهوةِ الكَلَبيَّةِ بالنسبة إلى الشبع أيضاً
ومثال المانع العادي له : الشهوة الكَلبية
والأمثلة التي ذكرناها في الأصل راجعة للسببِ العادي ؛ وهو أكل الطعام المقتاتِ باعتبارِ وجودِهِ أو نقيضه بالنسبة إلى وجودِ المسببِ
وهو الشبع أو ضده أو نقيضُهما، وبالله تعالى التوفيق
And an example of the habitual condition: safety from canine appetite with respect to satiety as well. And an example of the habitual obstacle to it: canine appetite. And the examples we mentioned in the original return to the habitual cause, which is eating the staple food, in terms of its existence or its opposite with respect to the existence of the effect, which is satiety or its opposite or their opposite. And with Allah Almighty is success.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage provides examples to clarify the concepts of habitual condition (sharṭ ʿādī) and habitual obstacle (māniʿ ʿādī) in relation to the habitual cause (sabab ʿādī). First, Sanusi gives an example of a habitual condition: being free from canine appetite (i.e., extreme hunger) is a condition for satiety. Then he gives an example of a habitual obstacle: canine appetite itself is an obstacle to satiety. He then states that the examples he mentioned previously in the original text all revert to the habitual cause, which is the eating of staple food. The habitual cause is considered in terms of its existence or non-existence relative to the existence of the effect (satiety) or its opposite (hunger) or their opposite (neither satiety nor hunger). The passage concludes with a phrase of reliance on Allah.

The Origin and Meaning of 'Jabriyya'
A footnote explaining the origin and meaning of 'Jabriyya'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'Jabriyya' is a later coinage, originally called 'al-Mujbira', and know its key figures.

Core Idea
'Jabriyya' is a coined word; the original name was 'al-Mujbira'.

In this footnote, Sanusi explains the lexical background of the term 'Jabriyya'. He notes that it is not an original term but a later development, and he traces it back to the verb 'ajbara' meaning 'to compel'. This clarification prevents confusion about the proper name of the determinist school in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic footnote once, then the English translation. Get a feel for the flow of information.
Notice the parenthetical references—they indicate the source is a commentary.
2
Identify the original term and its derivation
Look for the phrase 'al-Mujbira' and its root verb 'ajbara'. Understand that 'Jabriyya' is a secondary form.
The derivation shows the core meaning of compulsion.
3
Note key figures and the reason for the later coinage
Find the founder (al-Najjār) and followers (Jahm, Ḥafṣ). Observe that the form 'Jabriyya' was adopted for assonance with 'Qadariyya'.
This pairing is common in doxographic literature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) الجبرية : لفظة مولّدة ، والأصل لهذا المعنى أن يقال : المجبرة ؛ من ( أجبر ) ، يقال : أجبره على الفعل ؛ إذا قهره عليه ، وشيخ المجبرة : الحسين بن محمد النجار البصري ، ومنهم : جهم بن صفوان ، وحفص الفرد ، ثم الجبرية لفظاً : بفتح الجيم وسكون الباء ، وتحريك الباء للازدواج كلمة ( قدرية ( لكونها تذكر معها ، وانظر ) تاج العروس » ( ج ب ر ) .
(1) al-Jabriyya: a coined word (mawallada). The origin for this meaning is to say 'al-Mujbira'; from (ajbara), one says 'ajbarahu ‘alā al-fi‘l' meaning he compelled him to it. The chief of the Mujbira is al-Husayn ibn Muhammad al-Najjār al-Baṣrī, and among them are Jahm ibn Ṣafwān and Ḥafṣ al-Fard. Then 'al-Jabriyya' in wording: with fatha of the jīm and sukūn of the bā', and the movement of the bā' for assonance with the word 'al-Qadariyya' because it is mentioned with it. See Tāj al-‘Arūs (j-b-r).
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This footnote provides a lexical and historical note on the term 'Jabriyya' (the determinists). The author states that 'Jabriyya' is a post-classical coinage; originally the proper term was 'al-Mujbira', derived from the verb 'ajbara' meaning to compel. The founder of the Mujbira school is identified as al-Husayn ibn Muhammad al-Najjār al-Baṣrī, with followers including Jahm ibn Ṣafwān and Ḥafṣ al-Fard. The form 'Jabriyya' is said to have been adopted for assonance with 'Qadariyya' (the free-will school), as the two are often mentioned together. The author then gives the voweling: jīm with a fatha, bā' with sukūn, and notes that the bā' is sometimes moved (to a fatha?) for the sake of pairing. He references the lexicon Tāj al-‘Arūs for further detail.

Key Terms
الجبرية
المجبرة
أجبر
التولد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume 'Jabriyya' is the original name.
Correction: The footnote explains it is a later coinage; originally it was 'al-Mujbira'.

Checkpoint

B) Al-Mujbira
C) Al-Najjariyya
D) Al-Jahmiyya

Key Takeaway
The correct original term for the determinist school is 'al-Mujbira', derived from 'ajbara' (to compel), while 'Jabriyya' is a later coinage for assonance with 'Qadariyya'.

Defining Idtirār (Compulsion) in Kalam
An incomplete footnote defining idtirār as compulsion felt in action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam definition of idtirār (compulsion) as the feeling of being compelled to an action, and why this concept is central to the debate on human free will and divine predestination (qadar).

Core Idea
Idtirār is the felt compulsion to act, distinguishing coerced acts from voluntary ones.

This brief footnote from Sanusi's commentary introduces a crucial term in the qadar discussion: idtirār, or compulsion. Although the passage is incomplete, it provides a precise definition that sets the stage for distinguishing between compelled and voluntary actions. Understanding this term is essential for grasping the Ash'ari position on human acquisition (kasb).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice that idtirār is defined as that in which compulsion to the act is felt. The word yuḥass (يُحَس) indicates a subjective experience.
The text is incomplete; it breaks off at 'faman iddaʿā annahu mujbarun ʿalā...' (so whoever claims he is compelled to...).
2
Understand the key terms
Focus on the two technical terms: al-Idtirār (الاضطرار) and al-Iljā' (الإلجاء). Both relate to compulsion, but idtirār emphasizes the felt aspect.
3
Consider the context
This footnote likely refutes a claim of absolute compulsion in voluntary actions. The Ash'ari position holds that while God creates actions, humans acquire them voluntarily, not under compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳) وعليه فالاضطرار : ما يُحَس فيه الإلجاء إلى الفعل ، فمن ادعى أنه مجبر على =
(3) And accordingly, al-Idtirār (compulsion) is that in which compulsion to the act is felt. So whoever claims that he is compelled to...
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This incomplete footnote defines 'idtirār' (compulsion), which is the state of being forced to an action without choice. The author states that idtirār is what is felt (yuhass) of compulsion (iljā') to the act. The text then cuts off with 'so whoever claims that he is compelled to...' presumably continuing with a refutation of someone claiming they are compelled in voluntary actions. The footnote corresponds to the marker in the main text after 'اضطراراً'.

The Definition of Kasb – Neutral Relation Without Causation
Sanusi defines kasb as the neutral relation of human power to the act without effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of kasb in Ash'ari theology and how it distinguishes the human role from divine creation.

Core Idea
Kasb is the relation of human power to the act without causing it.

In this lesson, Sanusi introduces the concept of kasb, a central term in Ash'ari theology that describes the human role in actions. The definition carefully avoids attributing any causal effect to human power, setting the stage for the argument that only God creates. We'll examine the definition and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of kasb
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note that the definition is a single sentence stating what kasb is.
The text begins an argument but cuts off; focus on the definition.
2
Identify key terms
Look at the key terms: al-kasb, al-qudra al-haditha, al-maqdur, al-ta'alluq, al-ta'thir. Understand each term's role in the definition.
3
Understand the significance of 'without influence'
Consider why 'without influence' (ghayr ta'thir) is crucial. How does this differentiate the Ash'ari position from the Mu'tazila and Jabriyya?
The common misunderstanding is that kasb implies an actual effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْكَسْبُ : فَهُوَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ تَعَلَّقِ الْقُدْرَةِ الْحَادِثَةِ بِالْمَقْدُورِ فِي مَحَلِّهَا مِنْ غَيْرِ تَأْثِيرٍ . اعلمْ : أَنَّهُ لمَّا ثبت بالعقل والنقل وجوب انفراد المولى تبارك
And as for acquisition (al-kasb): it is an expression for the relation of the originated power to the thing-power-overed (al-maqdur) in its locus without influence. Know: that when it is established by reason and revelation the obligation of the uniqueness of the Master, Blessed...
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of kasb (acquisition) in Ash'ari theology. The author states that kasb is the relation (ta'allaq) of the human originated power (al-qudra al-haditha) to the act (al-maqdur) in its locus (i.e., the human capacity where the act occurs) without any causal influence (ghayr ta'thir). This definition distinguishes the Ash'ari position from the Mu'tazila, who affirm that human power directly creates the act, and from the Jabriyya, who deny any human role. The author then begins a proof: 'Know that when it is established by reason and revelation the necessity of God's sole creative power...' but the text breaks off. The intended argument is that since God alone is the creator, human power cannot have a causal role; rather, kasb is merely a concurrent relation.

Key Terms
الْكَسْبُ
الْقُدْرَةُ الْحَادِثَةُ
الْمَقْدُورُ
التَّعَلَّقُ
التَّأْثِيرُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That kasb implies an actual effect of human power on the act.
Correction: The definition explicitly states 'without influence' (ghayr ta'thir), so kasb is merely a relation, not a cause.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's definition, what does kasb (acquisition) involve?
A) The human power creating the act
B) The relation of the human power to the act without causing it
C) The human power causing the act independently
D) The human power limiting God's will

Key Takeaway
Kasb is the relation of human power to the act without any causal influence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own power has no effect in creating any action, then in what sense are you truly responsible for your deeds? Can you accept that your 'doing' is merely a relation without influence, and still strive with full effort? Think of one sin you have committed relying on your own strength to avoid it later. How does this lesson change your strategy for avoiding that sin tomorrow?
?
You believe that Allah alone creates your every action. But when you succeed, do you secretly credit your own skill? When you fail, do you blame your own weakness? Identify a moment today where you naturally assumed you caused something. How will you consciously replace that assumption with the realization that your action is only a kasb—a relation without influence—and that Allah is the true creator? Write a specific prayer you will say in that moment.

The Ruling on Acts of Ostentation (Riya')
Commentator defines sixth category and ruling on riyā'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of shirk al-aghrād, when performing righteous deeds for worldly goals constitutes forbidden riyā', and the exception for seeking worldly goals to aid obedience to Allah.

Core Idea
Not all worldly intentions in worship are forbidden—context matters.

This lesson unpacks a subtle distinction in Islamic theology regarding intention (niyya). The commentator explains the sixth category of shirk, which involves mixing worship with worldly aims. We'll see when such acts are forbidden and when they are permissible, and how to understand texts that promise worldly benefits for worship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the commentator's own words come through.
The passage is dense but clear.
2
Identify the definition of shirk al-aghrād
Find the phrase 'associating purposes' and note how it is defined: performing a righteous deed with intention to attain a worldly goal.
This is the sixth category mentioned by Sanusi.
3
Understand the general ruling
The commentator states that such an act is forbidden ostentation (riyā'), whether the goal is sought from creatures or from Allah directly.
This shows the strictness of the rule.
4
Note the exception
If the worldly goal is sought to aid obedience to Allah, then it is not riyā'. This is a key nuance.
Example: earning money to support worship.
5
Reflect on the spiritual interpretation
The commentator interprets texts about worship causing provision as referring to spiritual expansion (contentment, asceticism), not necessarily material wealth.
True enrichment is being rich in Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وحكمُ السادس : المعصية ) يعني بالسادس : شرك الأغراض ؛ وهو أن يعمل عملاً مِنَ الأعمال الصالحة بنيّة الوصولِ بهِ إلى غرض دنيوي ؛ وهو رياء محرَّم ، سواء طلبَ ذلكَ الغرضَ مِنَ الخلْقِ أو مِنْ مولانا جلَّ وعزَّ ، إلا أن يطلب ذلك الغرض الدنيوي ليستعين به على طاعتِهِ تبارك وتعالى ، فلا يكون ذلكَ حينَئِذٍ رياءً وعلى هذا يُحمَلُ ما ورد في بعض الطاعات أنَّها سبب للتوسع في الرِّزْقِ ، وقد يُحمَلُ ذلكَ على التوسعة المعنوية ؛ بخلْقِ القناعة ، والزهد ، والغَناءِ بالمولى تبارك وتعالى عن كلَّ ما سواه ، وهذا هو الغناء الأكبر والتوسعة الحقيقية
His saying: (And the ruling of the sixth is disobedience) means by the sixth: associating purposes (shirk al-aghrād); that is, that one performs a deed from among righteous deeds with the intention of attaining thereby a worldly goal; and it is forbidden ostentation (riyā'), whether he seeks that goal from creatures or from our Master, may He be glorified and exalted, except that he seeks that worldly goal in order to aid himself in obedience to Him, may He be blessed and exalted—then it is not ostentation at that time. And upon this is interpreted what has been reported concerning some acts of worship that they are a cause for expansion in provision; and that may be interpreted as an expansion in a spiritual sense, by creating contentment (qanā'ah), asceticism (zuhd), and being enriched by the Master, may He be blessed and exalted, from everything other than Him. And this is the greater enrichment and the true expansion.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on the sixth category from Sanusi's text, which the commentator identifies as 'associating purposes' (shirk al-aghrād). The commentator defines it as performing a virtuous deed with the intention of achieving a worldly goal—this is forbidden ostentation (riyā'), whether the goal is sought from creatures or from God Himself. However, there is an exception: if one seeks a worldly goal in order to aid one's obedience to God, then it is not considered riyā'. The commentator then applies this principle to interpret texts that mention acts of worship as a cause for expansion of provision. He explains that such expansion should be understood spiritually—as the creation of contentment, asceticism, and being enriched by God from all else. This, he says, is the true enrichment and genuine expansion.

Key Terms
رياء
شرك الأغراض
قناعة
زهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any worldly intention in worship is always forbidden riyā'.
Correction: The passage clearly states an exception: if the worldly goal is sought to aid obedience to Allah, then it is not riyā'.

Checkpoint
When is seeking a worldly goal through an act of worship not considered riyā'?
A) When the goal is sought from creatures.
B) When the goal is to aid obedience to God.
C) When the goal is to become wealthy.
D) When the act is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The true expansion of provision is spiritual contentment and closeness to Allah, not necessarily material wealth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you search your heart for any hidden desire for worldly reward, even from Allah? What would it take to purify your intention so that your sole aim is to strengthen your obedience to Him, not to gain something back?
?
Have you ever justified a wrong intention in worship by telling yourself you are seeking something from Allah, not from people? What does this lesson reveal about that justification, and how will you respond to this truth starting today?
?
Reflect on the 'true expansion' described here: contentment, asceticism, and finding fulfillment in Allah alone. Is that your reality, or are you still chasing material provision as the ultimate sign of blessing? What concrete step can you take this week to seek the greater enrichment?

Four Dangerous Beliefs That Undermine Faith
Sanusi enumerates four dangerous beliefs and states their potential outcomes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four dangerous beliefs that Sanusi warns about and why each can lead to kufr or bidʿa.

Core Idea
Four beliefs that seem harmless can destroy your faith.

Sanusi identifies four subtle intellectual traps that can undermine one's creed. Each involves a misunderstanding or misapplication of reason and revelation. In this lesson, you'll learn what these traps are and how they can lead to disastrous consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's enumeration come through.
Notice that each definition is succinct.
2
Identify the four problematic beliefs
List the four beliefs: habitual association, compound ignorance, clinging to apparent meanings of scripture, and ignorance of rational principles and Arabic.
Reflect on why each is considered dangerous.
3
Reflect on the consequences
The conclusion states that each may produce kufr or bidʿa. Ask yourself: why does Sanusi consider these so serious?
Think about how each belief could lead one astray.
4
Connect to broader Aqeedah
These warnings protect against common errors. How do they relate to the importance of sound reasoning and proper interpretation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
- وَالرَّبْطُ الْعَادِيُّ : وَهُوَ إِثْبَاتُ التَّلَازُمِ بَيْنَ أَمْرٍ وَأَمْرٍ وُجُوداً أَوْ عَدَماً بِوَاسِطَةِ التَّكَرُرِ .
- وَالْجَهْلُ الْمُرَكَّبُ : وَهُوَ أَنْ يَجْهَلَ الْحَقَّ ، وَيَجْهَلَ جَهْلَهُ بِهِ .
- وَالتَّمَسُّكُ في عَقَائِدِ الإِيمَانِ بِمُجَرَّدِ ظَوَاهِرِ الْكِتَابِ وَالسُّنَّةِ : مِنْ غَيْرِ تَفْصِيلِ بَيْنَ مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ ظَاهِرُهُ مِنْهَا وَمَا لا يَسْتَحِيلُ .
- وَالْجَهْلُ بِالْقَوَاعِدِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ : الَّتِي هِيَ الْعِلْمُ بِوُجُوبِ الْوَاجِبَاتِ ، وَجَوَازِ الْجَائِزَاتِ ، وَاسْتِحَالَةِ الْمُسْتَحِيلاتِ ، وَبِاللِّسَانِ الْعَرَبِيِّ : الَّذِي هُوَ عِلْمُ اللُّغَةِ وَالإِعْرَابِ وَالْبَيَانِ .
يعني : أنَّ اعتقاد واحدٍ مِنْ هذه الأمورِ : قد ينشأ عنه كفرٌ مُجمَعٌ عليه ، وقد ينشأ عنه بدعةٌ مُختلف في كفرِ صاحبها .
- Habitual association: it is the establishment of correlation between two things, in existence or non-existence, by means of repetition.
- Compound ignorance: it is to be ignorant of the truth, and to be ignorant of one's ignorance of it.
- Clinging in the creed of faith to mere apparent meanings of the Book and the Sunnah: without distinguishing between what is impossible from their apparent meanings and what is not impossible.
- Ignorance of the rational principles: which are the knowledge of the necessity of the necessary things, the possibility of the possible things, and the impossibility of the impossible things, and [ignorance] of the Arabic tongue: which is the science of lexicology, syntax, and rhetoric.
Meaning: that the belief in any one of these matters may produce from it unequivocal kufr, and may produce from it a bidʿa concerning which there is disagreement over whether its author is a kafir.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of four problematic stances that can affect one's faith. The author lists them with brief definitions, then concludes that each may lead to either unequivocal kufr or to a bidʿa whose perpetrator is disputed regarding kufr. The first is al-rabṭ al-ʿādī (habitual association), which is establishing a necessary connection between two things based on repeated occurrence. The second is al-jahl al-murakkab (compound ignorance), i.e., being ignorant of the truth and ignorant of that ignorance. The third is clinging to the mere apparent meanings of the Book and Sunnah in matters of faith without distinguishing between what may be interpreted figuratively and what may not. The fourth is ignorance of the rational principles (al-qawāʿid al-ʿaqliyya) – which are the knowledge of the necessary, possible, and impossible – and ignorance of the Arabic tongue (grammar, syntax, rhetoric). The concluding sentence ties these together as matters of creed.

Key Terms
الربط العادي
الجهل المركب
التمسك بظواهر الكتاب والسنة
القواعد العقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that these four issues are not equally serious and only some can affect one's faith.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that belief in any one of them may produce kufr or bidʿa, showing they are all potentially severe.

Checkpoint
What does al-rabṭ al-ʿādī (habitual association) mean according to Sanusi?
A) The belief that two things are always connected by rational necessity.
B) The establishment of correlation between two things by repetition.
C) Ignorance of the truth combined with ignorance of that ignorance.
D) Clinging to the literal meanings of scripture.

Key Takeaway
Four specific intellectual errors—habitual association, compound ignorance, literalism in scripture, and ignorance of rational and linguistic principles—can each lead to kufr or dangerous innovation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that something always causes another, like a habit, and forgotten that Allah is the ultimate doer? How can you break that unconscious association?
?
Could you be suffering from compound ignorance in some area of your faith—thinking you know the truth while actually being mistaken and unaware? What would it take to discover that blind spot?
?
When you read verses about Allah’s attributes, do you sometimes resist interpreting them even when the literal meaning would imply something impossible for Allah? Are you willing to accept ta'wil if it protects your faith?

Compound Ignorance and Its Danger
Sanusi defines compound ignorance and explains its role in persistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what compound ignorance is, why it is more dangerous than simple ignorance, and how it causes persistence in false beliefs.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance traps you because you don't know that you don't know.

Sanusi now turns to a critical concept for understanding why some people stubbornly cling to false beliefs: compound ignorance. Unlike simple ignorance—where a person is aware they lack knowledge—compound ignorance involves believing something false is true and being unaware of one's own error. This passage defines it, gives concrete examples from deviant sects, and explains why it makes correction so difficult.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Start by reading the Arabic definition of compound ignorance and its literal translation. Notice that it is called 'compound' because it combines ignorance of the truth with ignorance of one's own ignorance.
The footnote explains the 'compound' etymology.
2
Examine the examples
Sanusi gives two sets of examples: the philosophers (belief in eternal heavens and God acting by causation) and the Qadariyya (belief in independent creation and obligation of al-salāh wa-l-aslah). Identify what makes each a case of compound ignorance.
Both groups held false beliefs they considered correct.
3
Understand the root cause of persistence
Reflect on why compound ignorance leads to persistence: the person is unaware of their ignorance and believes they possess the truth. Therefore, they do not seek correction and resist anyone who challenges them.
This psychological mechanism is key to the concept.
4
Contrast with simple ignorance
Compound ignorance is worse than simple ignorance because the latter acknowledges lack of knowledge and thus allows for learning. Compound ignorance shuts the door to correction.
The text implies this contrast even if not explicitly stated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خطورة الجهل المركب
وأما الأمر الخامس؛ وهو الجهل المركب الذي هو اعتقاد أمر على خلاف ما هو عليه (1): فلا شك أنه سبب للتمادي على الكفر إن كان ذلك الكفر هو الذي وقع الجهل باعتقاده؛ كجهل الفلاسفة باعتقادهم قدم الأفلاك، واعتقادهم تأثير الإله بطريق التعليل، ونحو ذلك من كفرياتهم. وهو أيضاً سبب في التمادي على البدعة إن كانت تلك البدعة التي وقع الجهل باعتقادها؛ كجهل القدرية باعتقادهم استقلال الحيوانات بإيجادها أفعالها الاختيارية، واعتقادهم وجوب مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح في حق المولى تبارك وتعالى، ونحو ذلك من سائر البدع الاعتقادية.
تفاوت علاج المبتلى بالجهل المركب والمبتلى بالجهل البسيط
وإنما كان الجهل المركب سبباً وأصلاً للتمادي على الكفر والبدعة؛ لأجل عدم شعور صاحبه بجهله، واعتقاده الصواب والحق في جهله، ومن كان على هذه الصفة فإنه لا يطلب الخروج عن جهله؛ لأنه هو الصراط المستقيم عنده، وإذا اتفق أن يجيء من يشككه في معتقده، أو يرده إلى ما هو الحق في نفس الأمر.. يمتنع.
(1) إنما سمي مركبا: لتركبه من الجهل بالأمر، ومن الجهل بهذا الجهل
The danger of compound ignorance. As for the fifth matter – and it is compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab), which is believing something contrary to what it is (1) – there is no doubt that it is a cause for persistence (tamādī) upon kufr if that kufr is what one ignorantly believed: like the ignorance of the philosophers in their belief in the eternity of the heavens, and their belief in the effect of the God by way of necessary causation (taʿlīl), and similar things from their kufriyyāt. And it is also a cause for persistence upon bidʿa if that bidʿa is what one ignorantly believed: like the ignorance of the Qadariyya in their belief in the independence of animals in creating their voluntary actions, and their belief in the obligation of observing what is beneficial and most beneficial (al-ṣalāḥ wa-l-aṣlaḥ) with respect to the Lord, Blessed and Exalted, and similar things from the rest of the creedal innovations. The difference between treating one afflicted with compound ignorance and one afflicted with simple ignorance. And compound ignorance was only a root cause for persistence upon kufr and bidʿa because of the lack of awareness of its possessor of his ignorance, and his belief that the right and truth are in his ignorance. Whoever is of this description does not seek to exit his ignorance, because he considers it the straight path in his view. And if it so happens that someone comes who casts doubt in his belief or tries to return him to what is the truth in reality, he refuses. (1) It is only called compound because it is composed of ignorance of the matter and ignorance of this ignorance.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the danger of compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab). The author begins by defining it: it is believing something to be contrary to what it actually is. The footnote adds that it is called 'compound' because it consists of two ignorances: ignorance of the actual matter, and ignorance of one's own ignorance. Then the author states that compound ignorance is a cause for persistence (tamādī) in kufr if the ignorance pertains to a kufr belief, giving examples: the philosophers' belief in the eternity of the heavens and their belief that God acts by necessary causation (taʿlīl). Similarly, it is a cause for persistence in bidʿa if the ignorance pertains to a bidʿa, e.g., the Qadariyya's belief that animals independently create their own voluntary actions and that God must observe what is beneficial and most beneficial (al-ṣalāḥ wa-l-aṣlaḥ). The author then explains why compound ignorance is a root cause: the person is unaware of his ignorance and believes that his view is correct and the truth. Consequently, he does not seek to leave his ignorance because he thinks he is already on the straight path. Moreover, if someone comes to challenge his belief or try to guide him to the truth, he refuses and stubbornly persists. This highlights the dangerous effect of compound ignorance: it makes reform difficult because the person sees no need for change.

Key Terms
الجهل المركب
الجهل البسيط
التمادي
الفلاسفة
القدرية
الصلاح والأصلح
التعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that simple ignorance is just as dangerous as compound ignorance.
Correction: Compound ignorance is worse because the person believes he is correct and thus does not seek knowledge or accept correction, while a simply ignorant person knows he lacks knowledge and may seek it.

Checkpoint
Why does compound ignorance cause persistence in kufr or bid'a?
A) Because the person is unaware of his ignorance and thinks he is correct.
B) Because the person is too lazy to learn.
C) Because the person is actively evil.
D) Because the person lacks access to knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Compound ignorance is a dangerous state where a person holds a false belief while thinking it is true, making him resistant to correction and causing him to persist in error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a belief you hold with complete certainty that you have never questioned or even allowed yourself to doubt? What would it take for you to entertain the possibility that you might be in a state of compound ignorance right now, unaware of your own ignorance?
?
Think of a time someone tried to correct you on a deeply held view. Did you feel defensive, dismissive, or angry? According to this lesson, that resistance is a sign of compound ignorance. How can you train yourself to respond with humility and genuine inquiry instead of persistence?
?
Sanusi warns that compound ignorance makes a person 'firmly believe that his ignorance is the truth.' What is one area of your life—in ‘aqīdah or daily practice—where you might be operating on assumptions you've never tested? Will you seek out a knowledgeable person or text to examine that belief today, before it leads to spiritual persistence?

The Danger of Compound Ignorance and Its Cause
Sanusi explains the danger of compound ignorance, contrasts it with simple ignorance, and identifies its cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why compound ignorance is more dangerous than simple ignorance and what causes it.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance blocks the path to truth by making its possessor certain of falsehood.

Sanusi explains a critical concept in Islamic epistemology: the difference between simple ignorance (lack of knowledge) and compound ignorance (believing something false while thinking it is true). He warns that compound ignorance is especially dangerous because it makes a person immune to correction. He also identifies its root cause: intellectual arrogance and reliance on weak arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read Sanusi's definition of compound ignorance as believing something contrary to its reality. Notice that it involves two layers: being wrong and not knowing you are wrong.
The term 'compound' refers to this double ignorance.
2
See its consequences
Identify the two consequences Sanusi lists: persistence in disbelief (kufr) or persistence in innovation (bid'a). He gives examples like the philosophers and the Qadariyya.
3
Contrast with simple ignorance
Compare compound ignorance with simple ignorance. Note that the person with simple ignorance accepts correction, while the compound ignorant refuses it because he thinks he is right.
This contrast highlights why compound ignorance is more harmful.
4
Identify the cause
Read the cause: trust in non-demonstrative evidence and pride when one happens to be correct. This pride leads to deprivation of guidance in other matters.
Sanusi emphasizes humility and dependence on God in every intellectual pursuit.

وأما الأمر الخامس؛ وهو الجهلُ المركَبُ الذي هو اعتقاد أمر على خلافِ ما هو عليه (۱): فلا شكَ أَنَّهُ سببٌ للتمادي على الكفرِ إِنْ كَانَ ذلك الكفرُ هو الذي وقع الجهلُ باعتقادِهِ؛ كجهل الفلاسفة باعتقادهم قدم الأفلاكِ، واعتقادهم تأثير الإله بطريقِ التعليل، ونحو ذلكَ مِنْ كفرياتهم هي وهو أيضاً سبب في التمادي على البدعةِ إِنْ كانَتْ تلك البدعة التي وقع الجهلُ باعتقادِها؛ كجهل القدريَّة باعتقادِهم استقلال الحيوانات بإيجادها أفعالها الاختياريَّةَ، واعتقادهم وجوب مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح في حقٌّ المولى تبارك وتعالى، ونحو ذلكَ مِنْ سائرِ البدع الاعتقادية. وإنَّما كانَ الجهلُ المركَّبُ سبباً وأصلاً للتمادي على الكفر والبدعةِ؛ لأجل عدم شعورِ صاحبِهِ بجهلِهِ، واعتقادِهِ الصواب والحق في جهلِهِ، ومَنْ كانَ على هذهِ الصفة فإنَّه لا يطلب الخروج عن جهلِهِ؛ لأنَّه هو الصراط المستقيمُ عندَهُ، وإذا اتَّفَقَ أَنْ يجيءَ مَنْ يشكّكُهُ في مُعتقَدِهِ، أو يردُّهُ إلى ما هو الحق في نفس الأمرِ.. يمتنع بعدم الاستماع له، ومن قبولِ قولِهِ، بخلافِ الجهلِ البسيط؛ وهو عدم إدراك أمرٍ مِنَ الأمورِ؛ فإنَّ صاحبَهُ يطلبُ العلم بما جهلَهُ إِنْ شعرَ بـ إدراكه، وإنْ غفل عن ذلكَ وجاءَ مَنْ ينبِّهُهُ لطلب العلم بذلكَ، أو جاءَ مَنْ يعلّمُهُ ما جهلَهُ.. فإنَّهُ يجيبُ إلى ذلك ويقبلُه؛ لما جُبِلَتْ عليهِ النفوسُ مِنَ النَّفْرة عن الجهلِ البسيط، ومحبَّةِ تحصيل العلم بما ليسَ معلوماً لها. وسبب الجهل المركَبِ: وثوق النفس في العقليات بما ليس برهانياً مِنَ الأدلَّةِ (۱)، وتحسينُ الظَّنِّ بما يستبد بهِ مِنْ أنظارها و استنباطها لا سيما عندما تظهر لها الإصابة للحقِّ في بعض أنظارها، فتزهو وتعجب حينئذٍ، وتقيس سائر أنظارها على ذلك النظرِ الذي مَنَّ المولى الكريم تبارك وتعالى فيه بالتوفيق لدَرَكِ الحقِّ، فضلاً منه جل وعلا. فعُوقِبَ هـذا الناظرُ بالحرمانِ وعدم التسديد في سائر الأنظارِ؛ لتكبرِهِ، وإهمالِهِ شكر نعمةِ دَرَكِ الصوابِ التي انفرد بإسدائها المولى جل وعلا، وليس للعقل ولا للفكرة ولا للدليل الصحيح مادةً وتركيباً فيهِ تأثير ألبتةَ؛ لا بطريقِ التولُّدِ، ولا بطريق التعليل، وإهماله لزوم التواضع والفقر إلى المولى الكريم جلَّ وعلا في كل نظر يقف بباله.
(1) إنما سُمِّيَ مركباً: لتركَّبه من الجهل بالأمر، ومن الجهل بهذا الجهل.
(1) في (د) وحدها: (زهو النفوس ووثوق النفس) بدل (وثوق النفس).
As for the fifth matter, which is compound ignorance – that is, believing something contrary to what it actually is (1): there is no doubt that it is a cause for persisting in disbelief if that disbelief is what ignorance occurred about; like the ignorance of the philosophers in their belief in the eternity of the heavens, and their belief in the effect of God by way of necessitation, and similar of their disbeliefs. It is also a cause for persisting in innovation if that innovation is what ignorance occurred about; like the ignorance of the Qadariyya in their belief in the independence of animals in originating their voluntary actions, and their belief in the obligation of observing what is good and most beneficial with respect to the Lord, Blessed and Exalted, and similar of all other belief-innovations. For compound ignorance is a cause and root for persisting in disbelief and innovation only because its possessor is unaware of his ignorance, and believes correctness and truth in his ignorance. Whoever has this quality does not seek to exit from his ignorance, because to him it is the straight path. And if it happens that someone comes to cast doubt on his belief, or to bring him back to what is the truth in reality, he refuses to listen to him and to accept his statement, unlike simple ignorance – which is the lack of perception of something. For its possessor seeks knowledge of what he is ignorant of if he is aware of his lack of perception, and if he is heedless of that and someone comes who alerts him to seek knowledge of that, or someone comes who teaches him what he was ignorant of, he responds to that and accepts it, because souls are naturally disposed to aversion from simple ignorance and love of acquiring knowledge of what is not known to them. The cause of compound ignorance is the soul's trust in intellectual matters in what is not demonstrative among evidences (1), and its thinking well of what it independently derives from its reflections and deductions, especially when it appears to it that it has hit the truth in some of its reflections, so it becomes proud and amazed at that time, and it measures the rest of its reflections against that reflection in which the Noble Lord, Blessed and Exalted, granted success in attaining the truth, as a favor from Him, Mighty and Majestic. Thus this reflector was punished by deprivation and lack of guidance in the rest of the reflections, because of his arrogance and neglect of gratitude for the grace of attaining correctness, the bestowal of which the Lord, Mighty and Majestic, alone occupies. And the intellect, thought, and correct proof in terms of matter and composition have absolutely no effect in it – neither by way of generation nor by way of necessitation – and his neglect of the necessity of humility and neediness towards the Noble Lord, Mighty and Majestic, in every reflection that occurs to his mind.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage defines compound ignorance, explains its dangers, contrasts it with simple ignorance, and identifies its cause. The author first states that compound ignorance is the fifth matter, defining it as believing something contrary to its reality. He then asserts that it causes persistence in disbelief or innovation, giving examples: the philosophers' belief in the eternity of the heavens and God's effect by necessitation (which is disbelief), and the Qadariyya's belief in animals' independence in their voluntary actions and the necessity of God's doing what is best (which are innovations). The reason compound ignorance causes persistence is that the ignorant person is unaware of his ignorance and thinks he is correct, so he does not seek to leave it. If someone tries to correct him, he refuses to listen, unlike simple ignorance, which is mere lack of knowledge. The possessor of simple ignorance seeks knowledge if aware, or accepts teaching if someone alerts him, due to the natural aversion to ignorance and love of knowledge. The cause of compound ignorance is the soul's trust in non-demonstrative arguments in intellectual matters, and its pride when it seems to have hit the truth in some reflections, leading it to measure all other reflections by that one, forgetting God's favor and the helplessness of intellect and proof. This arrogance results in deprivation of guidance in other reflections. The author emphasizes that neither intellect nor thought nor correct proof have any effect in this state, because the person neglects humility and neediness to God.

الجهل البسيط
الفلاسفة
القدريَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think compound ignorance is less harmful because it involves belief, but Sanusi shows it is more dangerous because it prevents correction.
Correction: Compound ignorance is more dangerous because the person is certain of a falsehood and refuses to listen to truth, unlike simple ignorance where the person can be taught.

Checkpoint
Why does compound ignorance cause persistence in disbelief or innovation according to Sanusi?
A) Because the person is unaware of his ignorance and believes he is correct.
B) Because the person lacks any knowledge about the matter.
C) Because the person is forced to believe by external factors.
D) Because the person enjoys being ignorant.

Key Takeaway
Compound ignorance is a dangerous state where a person believes falsehood with certainty, preventing them from seeking truth, and its cause is intellectual pride and reliance on non-demonstrative arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly about your faith or life that you have never seriously questioned. Could it be compound ignorance? What would it take for you to genuinely consider that you might be wrong, even for a moment?
?
Sanusi says compound ignorance arises from trusting your own reasoning and feeling pride when you happen to be correct. When was the last time you felt proud of your understanding of a religious matter? Did you thank God for that success, or did you measure other matters by that one success?
?
When someone challenges a deeply held conviction of yours, do you listen openly or do you feel defensive? If you refuse to listen, you may be displaying compound ignorance. Ask yourself: In what area of your life are you most resistant to correction, and what would it cost you to humble yourself before God and seek the truth from Him alone?

The Metaphorical Lam: Refuting the Mu'tazila
Sanusi refutes two misinterpretations of Quranic verses due to ignorance of bayan and ma'ani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the science of rhetoric (bayan) to refute the Mu'tazila's claim that the lam in 'ليعبدون' indicates a literal final cause, and why that interpretation is a product of ignorance of Arabic rhetoric.

Core Idea
The lam in 'ليعبدون' is metaphorical, not literal.

The Mu'tazila misinterpreted the verse 'وما خلقت الجن والإنس إلا ليعبدون' by taking the lam as indicating a real purpose for Allah's action. Sanusi explains that this mistake stems from ignorance of the art of rhetoric (bayan). He shows that the verse uses a derivative metaphor where obligation is likened to a final cause, so the lam is used metaphorically. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to how Sanusi connects the Qadariyya and Mu'tazila errors, but focus on the Mu'tazila part.
The passage begins with a brief reference to the Qadariyya, but the main teaching is about the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's error
The Mu'tazila took the lam in 'ليعبدون' as literal purpose for Allah's action. Sanusi says this comes from ignorance of semantics and rhetoric.
3
Understand the rhetorical device: isti'ara taba'iyya
Learn how Sanusi explains that the lam is used metaphorically. Obligation is likened to a final cause, so the lam is not literal but figurative.
Derivative metaphor involves transferring a word from its literal meaning to a figurative one based on likeness.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this principle prevents misinterpreting other Quranic verses about Allah's attributes and actions. Reflect on the importance of rhetorical knowledge in 'aqida.
This lesson shows that ignorance of Arabic rhetoric can lead to theological errors.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاشتغال (1) ، فيُؤخَذُ حِينَئِذٍ مِنْ تعميم الخلْقِ لكلِّ شيءٍ بطلان مذهبِ القدرية ، ومن الجهل بفنّ علم المعاني والبيانِ : أخذُ المعتزلة تعليل أفعال المولى تبارك وتعالى بالأغراض مِنْ قولِهِ جلَّ وعلا : ﴿ وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ﴾ [الذاريات : ٥٦] ، فجعلوا اللام للتعليل حقيقة ، ولو خالطوا فنَّ البيانِ لعرفوا أنَّ الآيةَ مِنْ بابِ الاستعارة التبعيَّةِ ، وأَنَّهُ شُبِّهَ التكليف بالعبادة في ترتُّبِهِ على الخلْقِ بالعلَّةِ الغائيَّةِ التي تترتب على الفعلِ ويُقصد الفعل لأجلها ، فجُعِلَتِ العبادة ـ أي : التكليف بها - لأجل هذا الشَّبَهِ علَّةً غائيةً بطريق الاستعارة(۲) ، فتبع ذلك استعارة اللام الموضوعة للتعليل ، ودخلَتْ في العبادةِ للدَّلالة على العلَّةِ المجازية .
Engagement (1), then it is taken from the generalization of creation over everything the nullification of the doctrine of the Qadariyya. And from ignorance of the art of semantics and rhetoric: the Mu'tazila's taking the justification of the actions of the Exalted and Glorified Lord by purposes from His word: 'And I did not create jinn and humans except to worship Me' [Adh-Dhariyat: 56], so they made the lam for real purpose. Had they studied the art of rhetoric, they would have known that the verse is of the type of derivative metaphor (isti'ara taba'iyya), and that obligation (taklif) was likened to worship in its order of following creation like a final cause that follows the action and for which the action is intended. So worship—i.e., the obligation to it—was made, due to this likeness, a final cause by way of metaphor (isti'ara), and then followed the use of the lam that is placed for purpose, and it entered into worship to indicate the metaphorical cause.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two erroneous interpretations arising from ignorance of semantics (ma'ani) and rhetoric (bayan). The author first mentions the Qadariyya's misreading of the verse 'خلق كل شيء' (they interpreted it in a way that supports their doctrine of free will), then focuses on the Mu'tazila's mistake regarding the verse about creation for worship. The Mu'tazila took the lam (لِ) in 'ليعبدون' as indicating literal purpose, implying that Allah's act of creation is truly for the sake of worship. The author corrects this by explaining that the verse employs a derivative metaphor (isti'ara taba'iyya): the obligation to worship is likened to a final cause that follows action, but it is not a real final cause. Thus, the lam is metaphorical, not literal. The author emphasizes that familiarity with bayan would prevent such errors.

Key Terms
علم المعاني
علم البيان
الاستعارة التبعية
العلة الغائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The lam in 'ليعبدون' proves that Allah has a literal purpose for creating.
Correction: The lam is used metaphorically due to the likeness between obligation and a final cause; Allah's actions are not motivated by purposes.

Checkpoint

A) حقيقة عقلية
B) استعارة تبعية
C) مجاز عقلي
D) تشبيه تمثيلي

Key Takeaway
Understanding Arabic rhetoric is essential to avoid misinterpreting Quranic verses about Allah's actions and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you read the verse 'وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ', do you immediately assume it describes Allah's purpose? How does this lesson challenge you to be more careful with Quranic interpretation?

The Mu'tazila fell into error because they were ignorant of rhetoric. What areas of Islamic knowledge have you been neglecting that might lead you to misunderstand the Quran? Will you commit to studying them?
?
This lesson shows that a single letter (lam) can carry deep metaphorical meaning. Have you ever dismissed a subtle point of Arabic grammar as irrelevant? How will you change your approach to reading the Quran from now on?

Intellectual Metaphor (Majaz 'Aqli): Attributing Effects to Means
Sanusi explains and exemplifies intellectual metaphor with Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of intellectual metaphor (majaz 'aqli), its examples in the Quran, and how it differs from linguistic metaphor.

Core Idea
Intellectual metaphor attributes actions to means based on rational association, not linguistic convention.

Sanusi warns against mistakenly believing that created things directly cause events, as this stems from ignorance of rhetoric. He provides Quranic examples where effects are attributed to means, then explains that these are cases of intellectual metaphor—a rational attribution distinct from linguistic metaphor. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noting the examples and the definition. Observe the footnote explaining why it's called 'intellectual'.
The examples are from Quranic verses often misinterpreted.
2
Identify the examples
List the three examples given: faith increasing from hearing Quran, covering private parts from clothing, winds stirring clouds. Notice how each attributes an effect to a means.
The author calls believing in direct causation from these means 'ignorance'.
3
Understand the definition and distinction
Analyze the definition of intellectual metaphor as attributing action to something associated but not the true doer. Compare with linguistic metaphor.
Intellectual metaphor does not require a linguistic convention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا مِنَ الجهلِ بفنّ المعاني والبيانِ : اعتقاد صدورِ حوادثَ مِنْ غير المولى تبارك وتعالى ؛ كاعتقادِ زيادة الإيمانِ مِنْ سماع آياتِ القرآنِ ؛ أخذاً مِنْ قولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ وَإِذَا تُلِيَتْ عَلَيْهِمْ وَايَتُهُ زَادَتْهُمْ إِيمَانًا ﴾ [الأنفال : ٢] ، وستر العورةِ مِنَ اللباس ؛ أخذاً مِنْ قولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ يَنبَنِي ءَادَمَ قَدْ أَنزَلْنَا عَلَيْكُمْ لِبَاسًا يُوَرِى سَوْءَاتِكُمْ ﴾ [الأعراف : ٢٦] ، وإثارة الرياح للسحاب ونشرها ؛ أخذاً مِنْ قولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ اللَّهُ الَّذِي يُرْسِلُ الرِّيحَ فَنُشِيرُ سَحَابًا [الروم : ٤٨] ، ونحو ذلكَ ممَّا هو في القرآنِ والسنَّةِ كثير. ومَنْ خالطَ فنَّ البيانِ عرفَ أنَّ الإسناد في جميعِ ذلكَ مِنْ بابِ الإسنادِ المجازي العقلي (1) ؛ وهو إسنادُ الفعل أو ما في معناه إلى ملابس له غير ما هو له في الظاهرِ عند المتكلّم. (1) وإنما كان عقليّاً : لأنه لا يتوقف على وجود وضع لغوي ؛ إذ إسناد الفعل لما لا يتأتى منه ذاك الفعل لا يعقل ، أما المجاز اللغوي فيكون في لفظ لم يوضع أصالة لهذا المعنى ، وبهذا تعلم قوة المجاز العقلي
And likewise from ignorance of the art of semantics and rhetoric: the belief in the occurrence of events from other than the Exalted and Glorified Lord; like the belief that faith increases from hearing verses of the Quran, taking from His word: 'And when His verses are recited to them, they increase them in faith' [Al-Anfal: 2]; and covering the private parts from clothing, taking from His word: 'O children of Adam, We have sent down to you clothing to conceal your private parts' [Al-A'raf: 26]; and the stirring of winds for clouds and their spreading, taking from His word: 'It is Allah who sends the winds, and they stir up clouds' [Ar-Rum: 48]; and similar instances in the Quran and Sunnah are many. And whoever studies the art of rhetoric knows that the attribution in all of that is of the type of intellectual metaphorical attribution (al-isnad al-majazi al-'aqli) (1); and it is the attribution of an action or what is in its meaning to something associated with it that is not its true doer in appearance according to the speaker. (1) And it is only called intellectual because it does not depend on the existence of a linguistic convention; since attributing an action to that from which that action cannot proceed is not intellectually sound. As for linguistic metaphor (majaz lughawi), it occurs in a word that was not originally placed for that meaning. And thereby you learn the strength of intellectual metaphor.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of a type of figurative speech: intellectual metaphor (majaz 'aqli). The author gives examples where people mistakenly attribute effects to causes other than Allah, like the Quran increasing faith, or clothing covering private parts, or winds stirring clouds. He then states that these are instances of intellectual metaphor (al-isnad al-majazi al-'aqli), defined as attributing an action to something that is not its true agent but is associated with it. The footnote explains why it is 'intellectual': it does not rely on a linguistic shift but on a rational relation. This distinguishes it from linguistic metaphor (majaz lughawi).

Key Terms
الإسناد المجازي العقلي
المجاز العقلي
المجاز اللغوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quranic verses like 'the Quran increases faith' mean that the Quran itself directly causes the increase, independent of Allah.
Correction: Sanusi explains that such attributions are intellectual metaphors; the true agent is Allah, and the means are merely associated.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the metaphor in 'اللَّهُ الَّذِي يُرْسِلُ الرِّيحَ فَنُشِيرُ سَحَابًا' called 'intellectual'?
A) Because it involves a play on words
B) Because it does not depend on a linguistic convention but on rational attribution
C) Because it is used in intellectual arguments
D) Because it is understood by scholars only

Key Takeaway
Intellectual metaphor attributes actions to means based on rational association, and it differs from linguistic metaphor which relies on word usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear Quranic verses like 'the recitation increases faith,' do you ever pause to reflect that the increase in faith is directly from Allah, not from the verses as an independent cause? How can you train your heart to attribute every spiritual benefit ultimately to Allah?
?
Think of a material blessing you have — like your job providing income or your doctor healing you. In your daily thoughts, do you ever mentally attribute these to the means rather than to Allah? What specific step can you take today to consciously shift your attribution to Allah as the true agent?
?
The passage warns that believing events occur from other than Allah is ignorance of rhetoric. Honestly, where in your life do you find it hardest to see past the apparent causes? What would it mean for you to live with the constant awareness that Allah alone is the real doer of all actions?

The Fourfold Division of Existents
Sanusi enumerates the four categories of existents with their relation to substrate and specifier, then gives an alternative classification via spatial extension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of existents according to Sanusi based on their need for a substrate and a specifier.

Core Idea
Existents are either independent of God or dependent in three ways.

Sanusi begins the fifth introduction by classifying all existents from a Kalam perspective. He uses two criteria: whether the existent needs a substrate (a thing in which it inheres) and whether it needs a specifier (an agent that brings it into existence). This classification helps us understand the fundamental difference between the Creator and creation, and among created things themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the four categories
Start by reading Sanusi's listing of the four categories. Notice the pattern: each category is defined by its relation to a substrate (maḥall) and a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ).
The first and fourth categories involve God's essence and attributes, while the second and third involve created things.
2
Understand each category
For each of the four, ask: does it need a substrate? Does it need a specifier? Identify which existent falls into each category.
Accidents need both, bodies need only a specifier, God's essence needs neither, God's attributes need a substrate but not a specifier.
3
Read the alternative classification
The footnote provides an alternative scheme using spatial extension. Compare it with the main fourfold division. Note how it maps onto the same four existents.
This alternative comes from al-Ghazālī's 'al-Iqtiṣād' and shows the flexibility of Kalam classifications.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification is foundational. It establishes the ontological categories that all subsequent Kalam arguments will rely on.
Understanding these categories prevents confusion when discussing God, attributes, accidents, and bodies.

المقدمة الخامسة في الموجودات
والموجودات بالنسبة إلى المحل والمخصص أربعة أقسام:
- قسم غني عن المحل والمخصص: وهو ذات مولانا جل وعلا
- وقسم مفتقر إلى المحل والمخصص: وهو الأعراض.
- وقسم مفتقر إلى المخصص دون المحل: وهو الأجرام.
- وقسم موجود في المحل ولا يفتقر إلى مخصص: وهو صفات مولانا جل وعز (1).

(1) ويمكن أن نقول: كل موجود: إما أن يكون متحيزاً أو غير متحيز، والمتحيّر: إن لم يكن فيه ائتلاف فهو الجوهر الفرد، وإن كان فيه ائتلاف فهو الجسم، وغير المتحيز: إن استدعى وجوده جسماً فهو الأعراض، وإن لم يستدع وجوده جسماً فهو ذات الله تعالى، كذا ذكر حجة الإسلام في (الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد) (ص 129)، وحرَّر الإمام المصنف هنا فزاد الحديث عن صفاته تعالى؛ لأنه اعتبر المحل والمخصص.

مراده بالمحل: الذات التي تقوم بها الصفات، لا المكان الذي تجاوزه الأجسام.
ومعنى افتقار الشيء إلى المحل أو وجوده في المحل: قيامه به على سبيل الاتصاف (1)
ومعنى المخصّص: الفاعل المختار الذي يخصّص الممكن الحادث بجائز أراده دون جائز لم يرده (2).
ومعنى افتقار الشيء إلى المحل أو وجوده فيه: اتصاف ذلك المحل به (3).
ومعنى استغنائه عن المحل: أن يكون في نفسه ذاتاً موصوفاً بالصفات، لا صفة.
ومعنى افتقار الشيء إلى المخصّص: أن يكون حادثاً محتاجاً إلى فاعل يخصّصه بالوجود بدلاً عن العدم الذي كان عليه.

(1) يعني: اتصاف المحل بالشيء الذي قام به؛ كاتصاف الجرم الذي قام به البياض بكونه أبيض، وكاتصاف الذات العلية التي قام بها العلم بكونه عالماً، فقوله: (افتقار) خاص بالحوادث؛ إذ لا بياض إلا في جرم، وقوله: (وجوده) أراد به هنا: ذات القديم؛ إذ قيام الصفات القديمة بالقديم أفهمت الصفات المعنوية أو الأحوال أو الاعتبارات
(2) وهذا المخصص عند أهل الحق واحد؛ وهو الله سبحانه وتعالى، ولا مخصص سواه من حيث الإيجاد، وأفعال الحيوانات الاختيارية لها تخصيص مجازي يعبر عنه بالكسب، فالقديم مخصص لا يقبل التخصيص، والحدث مخصص فقط
(3) كالبيان والتأكيد لما سبق من هذا المعنى
The Fifth Introduction: On Existents
Existents with respect to the substrate (al-maḥall) and the specifier (al-mukhaṣṣiṣ) are four categories:
- A category independent of the substrate and the specifier: and it is the Essence of our Master, Majestic and High.
- And a category in need of the substrate and the specifier: and it is the accidents (al-aʿrāḍ).
- And a category in need of the specifier without the substrate: and it is the bodies (al-ajrām).
- And a category existing in the substrate but not in need of a specifier: and it is the attributes of our Master, Majestic and Mighty (1).

(1) It can be said: every existent either is spatially extended (mutaḥayyiz) or not spatially extended. The spatially extended: if there is no composition in it, it is the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard); and if there is composition in it, it is the body (al-jism). The non-spatially extended: if its existence requires a body, it is the accidents; and if its existence does not require a body, it is the Essence of Allah Most High. Thus mentioned the Proof of Islam (al-Ghazālī) in (al-Iqtiṣād fī al-Iʿtiqād) (p. 129). And the author (al-Imām al-muṣannif) formulated it here and added discussion of His attributes, may He be exalted, because he considered the substrate and the specifier.

His meaning of 'substrate' (al-maḥall) is: the essence (al-dhāt) in which attributes subsist, not the place (al-makān) which bodies occupy.
And the meaning of a thing's need for the substrate or its existence in the substrate: its subsisting in it (al-qiyām bihi) in the manner of description (ʿalā sabīl al-ittiṣāf) (1).
And the meaning of the specifier (al-mukhaṣṣiṣ): the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) who specifies the possible originated thing (al-mumkin al-ḥādith) by a permissible (jāʾiz) that He willed, to the exclusion of a permissible that He did not will (2).
And the meaning of a thing's need for the substrate or its existence in it: the description of that substrate by it (ittiṣāf dhālika al-maḥall bihi) (3).
And the meaning of its independence of the substrate: that it be in itself an essence described by attributes, not an attribute.
And the meaning of a thing's need for the specifier: that it be an originated thing (ḥādith) in need of an agent who specifies it with existence instead of the non-existence (al-ʿadam) which it had.

(1) Meaning: the description of the substrate by the thing that subsists in it; like the description of the body in which whiteness subsists as being white, and like the description of the exalted Essence in which knowledge subsists as being knowing. So his statement (need) is specific to originated things; for there is no whiteness except in a body, and his statement (its existence) intended here the Essence of the Eternal; for the subsistence of eternal attributes in the Eternal conveys the meaning of the attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) or states (al-aḥwāl) or considerations (al-iʿtibārāt).
(2) And this specifier according to the People of Truth (ahl al-ḥaqq) is One; and He is Allah, Glory be to Him, Most High; and no specifier other than Him in terms of bringing into existence (al-ījād); and the voluntary acts of animals have a metaphorical specification which is called acquisition (al-kasb). So the Eternal is a specifier that does not accept specification, and the originated is specified only.
(3) It is clarification and emphasis of what preceded of this meaning.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a classification of existents based on their relation to two concepts: a substrate (maḥall) and a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ). The author begins the fifth introduction of the book by presenting a fourfold division.

First, he lists the categories:
1. That which is independent of both substrate and specifier: the Essence of God (dhāt Allāh). 
2. That which needs both substrate and specifier: accidents (aʿrāḍ), like whiteness, which inhere in a body and require a cause.
3. That which needs a specifier but not a substrate: bodies (ajrām). Bodies are substances that do not inhere in anything but still need a creator.
4. That which exists in a substrate but does not need a specifier: the attributes of God (ṣifāt Allāh). They subsist in His Essence eternally and are not created.

The first footnote provides an alternative classification in terms of spatial extension (taḥayyuz): every existent is either spatially extended (mutaḥayyiz) or not. The spatially extended divides into the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) and the composite body (jism). The non-spatially extended divides into accidents (which need a body) and God's Essence (which does not). The author (Sanusi) chose to discuss attributes separately because he considered the substrate-specifier framework.

Then the author defines key terms:
- 'Substrate' means the essence in which attributes subsist, not physical space.
- 'Need for a substrate' means the thing's subsistence in it as a description—i.e., it is an attribute of that substrate.
- 'Specifier' is the free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) who selects which possible existent to bring into being.
- 'Need for a specifier' means the thing is originated (ḥādith) and requires a cause to bring it from non-existence to existence.
- 'Independence of substrate' means the thing is itself an essence described by attributes, not an attribute (so it is a substance, not an accident).

The second set of footnotes elaborates: (1) explains that 'need' in the definition of accidents is specific to contingent things, while 'existence in the substrate' when applied to God's attributes means the Essence is described by them. (2) affirms that the true specifier is God alone; human actions have a metaphorical specification called 'acquisition' (kasb). (3) states that the definition is a clarification and emphasis of the earlier point.

Throughout, the author maintains the Ash'ari ontological framework: God is self-sufficient, accidents are dependent on bodies, bodies are dependent on a creator, and God's attributes are co-eternal with His Essence.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's classification, which of the following is in need of both a substrate and a specifier?
A) The Essence of God
B) Bodies
C) Accidents
D) The attributes of God

Key Takeaway
God's essence is self-sufficient; everything else depends on Him either for existence, for a substrate, or both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything besides God depends on Him for either a substrate or a specifier, where in your daily life do you unconsciously act as though something or someone is independent of God? Will you today identify one such area and verbally affirm God's sole self-sufficiency before acting?
?
When you read that God's attributes exist in His essence without needing a specifier, do you truly believe His knowledge of you is eternal and uncreated? How would you live differently if you were convinced that every moment of your life is known by an attribute that has always been?
?
The passage distinguishes between accidents that need both a substrate and a specifier, and bodies that need only a specifier. Your own body is a body – it needs God to exist but has no eternal substrate. What does this fragility demand of you right now, before you finish this reflection?

Logic and Technical Terms
beginner
5 min
Defining Substrate and Specifier
Sanusi defines the key terms 'substrate' and 'specifier', explains the meanings of need and independence, and elaborates in footnotes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meanings of 'substrate' and 'specifier' in Kalam, and how they apply to God, attributes, accidents, and human actions.

Core Idea
Substrate means the essence bearing attributes, not space; specifier means the free agent who brings possibles into existence.

After presenting the classification, Sanusi provides detailed definitions of the key terms to avoid misunderstanding. He clarifies that 'substrate' means the essence in which attributes subsist, not physical location. He defines 'specifier' as the free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) who selects which possible things to create. The footnotes expand on these definitions, explaining the difference between need for a substrate in contingent things versus existence of attributes in the divine essence, and affirming that the true specifier is God alone, while human actions have a metaphorical specification called acquisition (kasb).

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of substrate
Sanusi defines 'substrate' (al-maḥall) as the essence that bears attributes, not the physical place occupied by bodies. Note the contrast: physical space is for bodies, but substrate is for attributes.
2
Understand 'need for the substrate'
He explains that a thing's need for a substrate means it subsists in it as a description. For example, whiteness subsists in a body and describes it. This applies to created accidents.
The footnote distinguishes between 'need' (iftiqār) for contingent things and 'existence' (wujūd) for eternal attributes.
3
Grasp the meaning of 'specifier'
The specifier is the free agent who brings a possible originated thing into existence, choosing one possibility over another. The footnote affirms that this is God, and human actions have a metaphorical specification (kasb).
This establishes the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb: humans acquire actions but God creates them.
4
Note the independence from substrate
A thing independent of substrate is itself an essence described by attributes, not an attribute. This applies to God's Essence and bodies.
Bodies are substances, not attributes, so they do not inhere in anything.
5
Review the footnotes' clarifications
The footnotes provide examples and theological refinements. They emphasize that God's attributes exist in His Essence eternally, and that specification belongs to God alone.
The third footnote simply restates the earlier definition for emphasis.

The Fifth Introduction: On Existents

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'substrate' (maḥall) refers to physical space or location.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does 'specifier' (al-mukhaṣṣiṣ) mean?
A) The material cause
B) The free agent who chooses possibilities
C) The substrate of accidents
D) The essence of God

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that everything originated needs a specifier who brings it into existence. Have you ever truly felt that your very existence is a gift constantly renewed by Allah's will? How would your day change if you remembered that every breath is a specification from the specifier?
?
If the substrate is the essence bearing attributes, then your attributes—your knowledge, your character—subsist in you. When you think about your faults or strengths, do you see them as mere descriptions of a fleeting self, or as qualities that point to the One who alone is independent of all substrates? What pride dissolves when you realise you are nothing but a 'substrate' sustained by Him?
?
Sanusi insists that the true specifier is Allah alone, and human actions are only metaphorical specification (kasb). This strips you of any claim to independent power over your actions. Does this humble you, or does it provoke you to argue for your own autonomy? Can you sincerely say, 'La ḥawla wa lā quwwata illā billāh' (There is no might nor power except with Allah) with full conviction after understanding this lesson?

The Meaning of al-Mahall (Locus) and Attributes
Sanusi defines al-mahall and explains the relation of attributes to it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-mahall is the essential substance that bears attributes, not a physical location, and the meaning of a thing's need for a locus or its self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
Al-mahall is the substrate of attributes, not a physical place.

Sanusi begins by clarifying a crucial term: 'al-mahall'. This term is often misunderstood as a physical location, but in kalam theology it refers to the essence (dhāt) that bears attributes. This lesson dissects the definitions and their implications for understanding how attributes relate to substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definitions carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own definitions come through before you interpret.
Notice the distinction between 'al-mahall' as substance vs. as place.
2
Identify the key distinction
Identify how Sanusi distinguishes between 'al-mahall' as a physical place and as the essential substance. Understand that attributes inhere in the substance, not in space.
This distinction is fundamental to kalam ontology.
3
Reflect on the two states
Reflect on the meaning of a thing's need for a locus (being an attribute) versus being self-sufficient (a substance qualified by attributes). Ask yourself: what does this imply about Allah's attributes?
The commentary explains that need for a locus applies only to temporal things; Allah is self-sufficient.

ومعنى افتقار الشيء إلى المحلّ أو وجودِهِ فيه : اتصاف ذلكَ المحل به (۳) .
ومعنى استغنائِهِ عن المحلَّ : أنْ يكونَ في نفسه ذاتاً موصوفاً بالصفات ، لا صفة .
ومعنى افتقار الشيء إلى المخصّص : أنْ يكونَ حادثاً محتاجاً إلى فاعل يُخصِّصُهُ بالوجودِ بدلاً عن العدمِ الذي كان عليه
(۱) يعني : اتصاف المحل بالشيء الذي قام به ؛ كاتصاف الجرم الذي قام به البياض بكونه أبيض ، وكاتصاف الذات العلية التي قام بها العلم بكونه عالماً ، فقوله : ( افتقار ( خاص بالحوادث ؛ إذ لا بياض إلا في جرم ، وقوله : ( وجوده ( أراد به هنا : ذات القديم ؛ إذ قيام الصفات القديمة بالقديم أفهمت الصفات المعنوية أو الأحوال أو الاعتبارات (۲) وهذا المخصص عند أهل الحق واحد ؛ وهو الله سبحانه وتعالى ، ولا مخصص سواه من حيث الإيجاد ، وأفعال الحيوانات الاختيارية لها تخصيص مجازي يعبر عنه بالكسب، فالقديم مخصص لا يقبل التخصيص ،
والحادث مخصص فقط (۳) كالبيان والتأكيد لما سبق من هذا المعنى
His intended meaning by 'al-mahall' (the locus) is the essential substance (adh-dhāt) that is characterized by attributes, not the place that bodies occupy. And the meaning of a thing's being in need of a locus or its existence in a locus is its subsisting in that locus in the manner of being an attribute (1). And the meaning of 'al-mukhaṣṣiṣ' (the specifier) is the freely acting agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) who specifies the contingent temporal thing (al-mumkin al-ḥādith) with what he wishes of the possible outcomes to the exclusion of what he does not wish (2). And the meaning of a thing's being in need of a locus or its existence in it is the locus's being qualified by it (3). And the meaning of its being free from need of a locus is its being in itself an essential substance qualified by attributes, not an attribute. And the meaning of a thing's being in need of the specifier is its being temporal (ḥādith), needing an agent to specify it with existence instead of the non-existence (al-ʿadam) that it had previously. (1) Meaning: the locus's being qualified by the thing that inheres in it; like the body's being qualified by whiteness when whiteness inheres in it, and like the exalted essence's being qualified by being knowing when knowledge inheres in it. So his statement 'being in need' is specific to temporal things, since there is no whiteness except in a body, and his statement 'its existence' is intended here to refer to the preeternal essence; since the subsistence of the preeternal attributes in the Preeternal imply the attribute-of-meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) or states (aḥwāl) or considerations (iʿtibārāt). (2) And this specifier, according to the people of truth, is one, and He is Allah, exalted be He. And there is no specifier other than Him in terms of bringing into existence. And the voluntary actions of animals have a metaphorical specification that is expressed as acquisition (al-kasb). The Preeternal is a specifier not subject to being specified, and the temporal is only something specified (3). This is clarification and emphasis of what preceded of this meaning.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a set of technical definitions. The author first defines 'al-mahall' (the locus). He specifies that it is the essential substance (adh-dhāt) that bears attributes, not a physical place. Then he defines a thing's need for a locus: it means the thing subsists in that locus as an attribute, i.e., it inheres in it. Next, he defines 'al-mukhaṣṣiṣ' (the specifier) as the freely acting agent who chooses among possibilities. The next definition restates the need for a locus as the locus being qualified by that thing. Then he defines self-sufficiency from a locus: the thing is a substance bearing attributes, not an attribute itself. Finally, the need for a specifier means the thing is temporal and requires an agent to bring it from non-existence to existence. The footnotes elaborate: footnote (1) gives examples and explains that 'being in need' applies only to temporal things, while 'existence in a locus' for the Preeternal refers to the subsistence of attributes. Footnote (2) states that the true specifier is only Allah, and creatures have only metaphorical specification called 'kasb'. The Preeternal is a specifier not subject to specification; the temporal is only specified.

Key Terms
المحل
الذات
الصفات
الاتصاف
الوجود
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-mahall means physical location
Correction: Sanusi clarifies it means the essential substance that bears attributes, not a place.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the meaning of 'al-mahall'?
A) Physical space
B) Essential substance bearing attributes
C) The attribute itself
D) Temporal thing

Key Takeaway
Al-mahall in kalam refers to the essence that subsists with attributes, not to spatial location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes like knowledge or power, do you ever imagine them as separate 'things' existing independently? Based on this lesson, how should you correct that mental picture?
?
The term 'need for a specifier' applies to temporal things. Have you ever considered your own existence as needing a specifier? How does that realization affect your daily reliance on Allah?

The Meaning of al-Mukhaṣṣiṣ (Specifier) and Need for a Specifier
Sanusi defines al-mukhaṣṣiṣ and explains a thing's need for it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-mukhaṣṣiṣ as the freely acting agent who specifies contingent things from nonexistence to existence, and the distinction between the true Specifier (Allah) and metaphorical specification (kasb).

Core Idea
Al-mukhaṣṣiṣ is the divine agent who chooses among possibilities; creatures have only acquired specification.

Sanusi then turns to another foundational term: al-mukhaṣṣiṣ. This concept is central to understanding divine causality and human acquisition. This lesson breaks down the definition and its implications for the nature of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of specifier
Read the Arabic definition and translation. Notice that the specifier is described as a freely acting agent who chooses among possibilities.
2
Understand the need for a specifier
Read the meaning of a thing's need for a specifier: it is temporal and requires an agent to bring it from non-existence to existence.
This reinforces the contingency of created things.
3
Reflect on true vs. metaphorical specification
Read footnote (2) which clarifies that only Allah is the true specifier; creatures have only metaphorical specification called 'kasb'. Consider the implications for human free will.
