ومعنى افتقار الشيء إلى المحلّ أو وجودِهِ في المحل : قيامُهُ بهِ على سبيل الاتصاف (۱)
ومعنى المخصّص : الفاعلُ المختارُ الذي يُخصِّص الممكن الحادث بجائز أراده دونَ جائز لم يرده (۲) ..

Bodies Are Independent of a Substrate but Need a Creator
Sanusi proves bodies are substrate-independent via three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why bodies (ajrām) are considered independent of a substrate (maḥall) and how kalam arguments establish this while maintaining their dependence on a Creator.

Core Idea
Bodies are substances, not accidents—they cannot subsist in another body.

This passage from Sanusi's theological primer tackles a foundational question: do physical bodies need a substrate to exist like accidents do? The author argues that bodies are independent of any substrate, but still require a specific Creator. He presents three rational proofs showing why the idea of a body subsisting in another body leads to absurd consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice that Sanusi begins with a dual claim: bodies need a creator but are substrate-independent.
2
Identify the three proofs
The passage offers three distinct arguments for independence from a substrate. Find each one and note its conclusion.
The first argument is based on spatial unity, the second on preference without a preferrer, the third on circularity/infinite regress.
3
Reflect on the logical consequences
Consider why each absurdity (unification, preference without preferrer, infinite regress) is impossible in rational thought.
These are standard kalam tools to establish that bodies are substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ الأجرام معَ افتقارها إلى المخصص غنيَّةٌ عنِ المحل ] وأما وجوبُ غَنائها عن المحل : فلأنَّها ليست صفاتٍ ، بل هي ذوات موصوفة بالصفاتِ ، فلو قام جرمٌ منها بجرم آخرَ لزمَ أَنْ يَتَّحدَ حيزهما ، وذلكَ يستلزمُ أنْ يكونَ الجرمان جرماً واحداً، وذلك لا يُعقَلُ . وأيضاً : لو افتقر الجرمُ إلى المحل كافتقار العرضِ إليه.. لزمَ الترجيح بلا مُرجّح ؛ إذْ ليسَ جَعْلُ أحدِ الجرمينِ محلاً للآخر بأولى مِنَ العكس . وأيضاً : يلزمُ في محلّهِ مِنَ الافتقار إلى محل ما لزم فيهِ ، فإِنْ كانَ الحال محلاً أيضاً لمحلّه .. لزم الدورُ ، وإِنْ كانَ غيرَهُ لزمَ التسلسل ، ودخولُ ما لا نهاية له في الوجودِ .
[Bodies, despite their need for a specific creator, are independent of a substrate.] As for the necessity of their independence from a substrate: because they are not attributes, but rather substances described by attributes. So if a body subsisted in another body, their spaces would necessarily unite, and that necessitates that the two bodies become one body, and that is inconceivable. Also: if the body needed a substrate like the need of an accident for it, then there would be a preference without a preferrer, since making one of the two bodies a substrate for the other is not more likely than the opposite. Also: what is necessary for its substrate from needing a substrate would be necessary for it [the substrate]; then if the state [the body] were also a substrate for its substrate, circularity would follow, and if it were other than that, infinite regress would follow, and the entry of what has no end into existence.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a proof that bodies (أجرام) are independent of a substrate (محل) while still needing a specific creator (مخصص). The author begins by stating the dual position: bodies depend on a creator but do not need a substrate. The proof for independence from a substrate rests on three arguments. First, bodies are not attributes but substances (ذوات) described by attributes. If a body subsisted in another body, their spaces would merge into one, making them a single body—which is absurd. Second, if bodies needed a substrate like accidents do, then there would be no reason for one body to be the substrate for another rather than the reverse: this would imply preference without a preferrer (ترجيح بلا مرجح). Third, the need for a substrate leads to infinite regress or circularity: if a body needs a substrate, that substrate itself would need another substrate, and so on infinitely or back to the original, both impossible. These arguments are standard in kalam to establish that bodies are substances (جواهر) not accidents (أعراض).

Key Terms
أجرام (bodies)
محل (substrate)
مخصص (specific creator)
غناء عن المحل (independence from substrate)
قيام (subsistence)
عرض (accident)
ترجيح بلا مرجح (preference without a preferrer)
دور (circularity)
تسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies, like accidents, need a substrate (mahall) to subsist.
Correction: Bodies are substances, not accidents; they do not subsist in anything else. They are independent of a substrate, as proven by the three arguments.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason bodies cannot subsist in another body?
A) They are accidents
B) They would become one body, which is inconceivable
C) They need a creator
D) They are eternal

Key Takeaway
Bodies are independent of any substrate because they are substances, not accidents, and requiring a substrate leads to logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi proves that bodies need no substrate yet require a Creator. If your own existence is similarly dependent only on Allah, what part of your life are you still treating as self-sufficient or independent? How will you dismantle that illusion today?
?
The argument against infinite regress shows that creation cannot be its own foundation. Where in your life are you relying on created things—people, possessions, status—as your ultimate support? What would it cost you to transfer that reliance entirely to the Creator?
?
Sanusi uses logic to defend a truth of faith. Do you ever feel embarrassed to use rational arguments for belief? Are you willing to study and master such proofs so that your faith is not merely inherited but deeply reasoned? Commit to one step you will take this week to strengthen your intellectual foundation.

The Four Categories of Substance and Their Proof
Sanusi enumerates the four categories of a substance and gives their proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories (akwan) of a substance and the logical proof that limits them to exactly these four.

Core Idea
A substance has only four possible states: rest, motion, separation, union.

This passage from Sanusi establishes the foundational categories of physical bodies in kalam. Before discussing change or accidents, we must know what states a substance can be in. Sanusi gives a simple but powerful proof by dichotomy, showing that these four categories are exhaustive and mutually exclusive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice the two dichotomies.
The passage includes a reference to Tarazi, which is a scholarly citation.
2
Understand the first dichotomy
The first dichotomy concerns the substance's relation to its own space: either it remains in that space (rest) or does not (motion). Ask yourself: can a substance be in any other state relative to its space?
Rest and motion are opposites with no middle state.
3
Understand the second dichotomy
The second dichotomy concerns the substance's relation to another substance: either there is a void between them (separation) or there is no void (union). Consider if any other relation is possible.
This covers all possibilities for spatial relations between two substances.
4
Reflect on exhaustiveness
Combine both dichotomies. The proof shows that all possible states of a substance are covered by these four categories. There is no room for a fifth category.
This is a foundational proof in kalam physics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالأكوان ، ودليل حصر الأكوان بهذه الأربعة : أن الجوهر إما أن يستقر في حيزه فذاك السكون ، أو لا فتلك الحركة ، وإذا نُسب الجوهر لآخر : فإما أن يكون بينهما فراغ فذاك الافتراق ، أو لا فذاك الاجتماع . مفاد «تارزي » ( ق ۳۱ ) .
Regarding the categories (أكوان), and the proof of limiting the categories to these four: The substance either rests in its space – that is rest – or does not – that is motion. And when the substance is related to another: either there is a void between them – that is separation – or not – that is union. The gist of Tarazi (page 31).
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof of the four categories (أكوان) that a substance (جوهر) can have. The author states that there are exactly four categories and proves this by two dichotomies. First, with respect to its own space (حيز), the substance either remains in it (rest) or moves away (motion). Second, with respect to another substance, either there is a void between them (separation) or not (union). These are the only possible states. The proof is exhaustive: rest/motion and separation/union cover all logical possibilities. The reference to Tarazi is likely a citation from a scholar. This classification is foundational in kalam physics.

Key Terms
أكوان
جوهر
سكون
حركة
افتراق
اجتماع
حيز
فراغ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think there are more than four categories, such as spinning or vibrating.
Correction: The dichotomies show that any state of a substance must be either rest/motion regarding its own space and either separation/union regarding another substance. These four are exhaustive.

Checkpoint
How many categories (أكوان) does Sanusi list for a substance?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The substance has exactly four possible categories: rest, motion, separation, and union, proven by two exhaustive dichotomies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that every substance—including the atoms of your own body—can only be in one of four states at any given moment. When you lay down to sleep tonight, your body is in a state of rest and your limbs are in union. Will you take a moment to reflect that this order is maintained by Allah alone? Will you thank Him for keeping your body intact and commit to using it in obedience tomorrow?
?
If these four categories are exhaustive, then every change you experience—every movement, every separation, every coming together—is a sign of Allah's creation and control. When you feel separated from a loved one or united in joy, do you recognize these as manifestations of the same divine order? Will you let this awareness turn your heart to the Creator who governs all atoms?

The Definition of Knowledge and Its Exclusions
Commentary on the definition of knowledge and its exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of knowledge, its key components (the known, disclosure), and what it excludes (conjecture, doubt, illusion, firm belief, compound ignorance).

Core Idea
Knowledge is the clear, continuous disclosure of reality, leaving no room for doubt.

This lesson unpacks the technical definition of knowledge (al-ilm) in Islamic theology. Before we can discuss what it means to know Allah or His attributes, we need a precise understanding of 'knowledge' itself. The Ash'ari scholars defined it as an attribute that discloses the known with full clarity, and this definition carefully excludes all states that fall short of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three categories of the known
The passage states that 'the known' includes everything that can be known: the necessary, the impossible, and the possible. These are the three rational categories that form the objects of knowledge.
2
Learn the core meaning of 'disclosure'
Knowledge is defined as an attribute by which the known is disclosed with absolute clarity, leaving no obscurity. This disclosure must be continuous, based on necessity or demonstration.
3
Identify what the definition excludes
The definition explicitly excludes conjecture, doubt, illusion, firm belief (even if true) because they involve probability or susceptibility to challenge. It also excludes compound ignorance, which is believing something contrary to reality.
The use of the present tense implies continuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني بالمعلومِ : كلَّ ما يصحُ أنْ يُعلَمَ ؛ وهو : كلُّ واجب ، وكلُّ مستحيل ، وكل جائز .
ومعنى ( ينكشفُ ) : أنَّهُ يَتَّضحُ ذلكَ المعلومُ لمَنْ قامَتْ بهِ تلكَ الصفةُ ويتميز عن غيرِهِ اتضاحاً لا خفاءَ مَعَهُ
وهذا مُخرِجٌ للظنِّ والشكَّ والوهم ؛ فإنَّ الاحتمال القائم فيها يمنعُ مِنِ انكشاف ذلك المظنون والمشكوك والموهوم ، ويوجب له خفاء .
ويُخرِجُ أيضاً : الاعتقاد الجازم، مطابقاً كان أو غير مطابق ؛ لأنَّهُ يحتمل النقيض بتشكيكِ مُشكّكٍ ، فلا يستمر معه الانكشاف
والتعبير بالمضارع في الانكشاف يقتضي دوام الانكشاف واستمرارَهُ ؛ بحيثُ لا يحتملُ النقيض بوجه ، وذلك لاستناد هذه الصفة إلى ضرورة أو برهان(۱)
وقوله : ( على ما هو بهِ ) زيادةٌ في البيانِ ، وتصريح على سبيل التوكيد بإخراج الجهل المركَبِ ؛ وهو اعتقاد أمرٍ على خلاف ما هو به
والمقصود من هذا التعريف : التقريبُ على سبيل الاختصار ؛ لعشرِ تعريف العلم بما يسلمُ مِنْ كلَّ مناقشة (۲)
(۱) وإنكار المعلوم بعد الضرورة أو البرهان يُسمَّى عِناداً، لا جهلاً أو ظنّاً ونحوهما. (۲) قد عرَّف إمام الهدى أبو منصور الماتريدي العلم تعريفاً مطرداً منعكساً لا يُرَدُّ = [؟]
ويدخل في العلم على مقتضى هذا التعريف : إدراك السمع والبصر وسائر الإدراكاتِ ، فهي إذا أنواعٌ مِنَ العلم ، وهذا مذهب الشيخ الأشعري رضيَ الله تعالى عنه
و (۱) عليه كما قال العلامة أبو المعين النسفي في ( تبصرة الأدلة ) ( ۱۱/۱ ) وذاك قوله : ( العلم : صفةٌ يتجلّى بها المذكور لمَنْ قامت هي به ) . هذا : لا بد من إثبات السمع والبصر كصفتين مستقلتين له سبحانه وتعالى، ولا يغني إثبات صفة العلم عنهما كما فعل بعض المعتزلة في معنويتهما .
He means by 'the known': everything that can be known; and it is: every necessary, every impossible, and every possible. And the meaning of 'discloses' (yankashifu): that the known becomes clear for the one in whom that attribute subsists, and he is distinguished from others by a clarity with which there is no obscurity. And this excludes conjecture (al-zann), doubt (al-shakk), and illusion (al-wahm); for the probability present in them prevents the disclosure of that conjectured, doubted, and illusory thing, and necessitates for it obscurity. And it also excludes firm belief (al-i'tiqad al-jazim), whether corresponding or not; because it admits the opposite by the doubt of a doubter, so disclosure does not continue with it. And the expression with the present tense in 'discloses' requires continuity of disclosure and its permanence, such that it does not admit the opposite at all, and that is because this attribute is based on necessity (darurah) or demonstration (burhan). (1) And denial of the known after necessity or demonstration is called obstinacy (inad), not ignorance or conjecture and the like. And His saying: 'as it is' (ala ma huwa bihi) is an increase in clarification and an explicit statement by way of emphasis, excluding compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab); which is believing something contrary to what it is. And the purpose of this definition is approximation for the sake of brevity, for a definition of knowledge that is safe from all objection. (2) The leader of guidance Abu Mansur al-Maturidi defined knowledge with a definition that is comprehensive and exclusive, not refuted = [؟]. And entering into knowledge according to this definition is the perception of hearing, sight, and all other perceptions; they are therefore types of knowledge, and this is the madhhab of Shaykh al-Ash'ari, may Allah be pleased with him. And (1) upon it as said by the scholar Abu al-Ma'in al-Nasafi in (Tabsirat al-Adillah) (11/1): and that is his statement: 'Knowledge is an attribute by which the mentioned thing is manifested for the one in whom it subsists.' This: it is necessary to affirm hearing and sight as two independent attributes for Him, exalted be He, and the affirmation of the attribute of knowledge does not suffice for them, as some Mu'tazila did regarding their [i.e. the attributes'] being intelligible states (ma'nawiyya).
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the definition of knowledge (al-`ilm). It begins by clarifying what is meant by 'the known' (al-ma`lum): it is everything that can be known, which comprises three rational categories: the necessary (al-wajib), the impossible (al-mustahil), and the possible (al-ja'iz). Then it explains the key term 'disclosure' (inkishaf): it means that the known becomes clear to the one who possesses the attribute, distinguished from other things with a clarity that leaves no obscurity. This part serves to exclude conjecture (al-zann), doubt (al-shakk), and illusion (al-wahm), because these involve probability that prevents full disclosure. It also excludes firm belief (al-i'tiqad al-jazim), even if it matches reality, because a skeptic can challenge it, so it does not maintain continuous disclosure. The use of the present tense for 'discloses' indicates that disclosure must be continuous and permanent, without any possibility of its opposite; this is because such knowledge is based either on necessity (darurah) — like self-evident truths — or on demonstration (burhan) — like reasoned proofs. A footnote adds that denying a known after necessity or proof is called obstinacy (inad), not ignorance. The phrase 'as it is' (ala ma huwa bihi) serves to exclude compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab), which is believing something contrary to its true nature. The commentator notes that this definition is meant to be a concise approximation that avoids common objections. He then extends the definition: sensory perceptions (like hearing and sight) enter into knowledge according to this definition, and this is the position of Imam al-Ash'ari. A final note cites Abu al-Ma'in al-Nasafi's definition of knowledge and emphasizes that hearing and sight must be affirmed as independent attributes of God, not merely reduced to knowledge as some Mu'tazila do.

Key Terms
العلم
المعلوم
الانكشاف
الظن
الشك
الوهم
الاعتقاد الجازم
الجهل المركب
ضرورة
برهان
عناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that firm conviction (i'tiqad jazim) is knowledge even if it can be challenged.
Correction: The passage shows that true knowledge must be immune to challenge; firm belief is excluded because it can be questioned by a skeptic.

Checkpoint
What does the phrase 'على ما هو به' (as it is) in the definition exclude?
A) Doubt (al-shakk)
B) Conjecture (al-zann)
C) Compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab)
D) Firm belief (al-i'tiqad al-jazim)

Key Takeaway
Knowledge in Ash'ari theology is a continuous, certain disclosure of reality, based on necessity or proof, and excludes all forms of uncertainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a core Islamic belief you hold (e.g., Allah's existence, prophecy). Have you ever felt your certainty waver when confronted with skeptical doubts? According to this lesson, that means your belief was not yet true knowledge. What would it take for you to transform that belief into knowledge based on necessity or demonstration? What step will you take this week to find that unshakeable proof?
?
The passage says firm belief (i'tiqad jazim) is not knowledge because it can admit the opposite when challenged. Are there any beliefs about your faith that you have simply inherited without personal examination, and that you avoid questioning for fear of losing them? What would it mean to confront those beliefs with honest inquiry, seeking the clarity that this lesson defines as knowledge?
?
The footnote declares that denying the truth after clear proof is obstinacy (inad), not ignorance. Is there any truth about your own soul—a sin you commit or a duty you neglect—that you have recognized clearly but continue to reject? How can you move from obstinacy to acceptance and action today?

Knowledge Includes Sensory Perception: The Ash'ari View
Extension of knowledge to sensory perceptions and divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school extends the definition of knowledge to include sensory perceptions and why hearing and sight must be affirmed as independent attributes of God.

Core Idea
Sensory perceptions are types of knowledge, and God's hearing and sight are distinct attributes, not reducible to knowledge.

The definition of knowledge as a clear disclosure of the known might seem to apply only to intellectual understanding. But the Ash'ari scholars argued that sensory perceptions—like hearing and sight—also satisfy that definition. This lesson explores that extension and its implications for understanding God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Recognize that sensory perceptions are knowledge
The passage states that according to the given definition, perceptions of hearing, sight, etc. enter into the category of knowledge, as per Imam al-Ash'ari's position.
2
Understand the distinctness of divine attributes
The passage concludes by affirming that hearing and sight must be considered independent attributes of God, not mere aspects of His knowledge, countering the Mu'tazilite view.

Whether Accidents Are Perceived by Touch
Sanusi presents the debate on whether akwan are perceived by touch and endorses the affirmative view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether the accidents (akwan) are directly perceived by touch and why the affirmed view is considered correct.

Core Idea
Touch directly perceives motion, rest, conjunction, and separation.

In Islamic theological discussions, there is a question about what our senses actually perceive. One specific issue is whether the accidents known as 'akwan' (motion, rest, conjunction, separation) are directly perceived by the sense of touch. This passage presents two opposing views on this matter and concludes with the verified position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Note the structure: a question, two opinions, and a conclusion.
The passage begins with 'اتفاقا' indicating prior consensus on something.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the first view (that touch perceives akwan) and its proof about moving and separating objects. Then find the second view (denial) and its claim.
Notice how each view is supported.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage ends with a quote from al-Muqitir stating the verified view is the first one. Reflect on why this might be the stronger argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتفاقا(۱) : هل هي مُتعلَّق لإدراكِ اللمس أم لا ؟ فذهبَ بعضُهم إلى أنَّ إدراك اللمس يتعلَّق بها ، واحتج على ذلكَ : بأَنَّ مَنْ لمس شيئاً واضطرب تحت يده .. أدرك حركته ، وإذا تفرَّقَتْ أجزاؤُهُ في يَدِهِ أدركَ تفرقتها ، ومِنَ الأصحابِ مَنْ أنكر ذلكَ ، وزعم أَنَّهُ يعلم ذلكَ عند اللمس ، ولم يتعلَّق إدراك اللمس بهِ ، قال المقترح : ( والتحقيق : الأوَّلُ ((٢)
By unanimous agreement [there is a footnote]: Are they an object of the perception of touch or not? So some held that the perception of touch relates to them, and argued for that: because one who touches something and it moves under his hand perceives its motion, and if its parts separate in his hand he perceives their separation. And among the [Shafi'i?] companions, some denied that, and claimed that he knows that at the time of touch, but the perception of touch is not related to it. Al-Muqitir said: 'The verified view is the first.'
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a brief discussion on whether the accidents (akwan: motion, rest, conjunction, separation) are perceived by the sense of touch. It begins with the phrase 'by agreement' indicating that there is consensus on something prior. Then it presents the question: are these accidents objects of touch perception? Some argue that they are, giving the example that when you touch something that moves, you perceive its motion, and if its parts separate, you perceive that separation. Others deny this, claiming that although you know these things at the time of touch, the perception of touch itself does not relate to them. The passage concludes by quoting 'al-Muqitir' (likely a compiler or commentator) saying the verified view is the first opinion, i.e., that touch does perceive these accidents.

Key Terms
الأكوان (akwan)
إدراك اللمس (touch perception)
الحركة (motion)
السكون (rest)
الاجتماع (conjunction)
الافتراق (separation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the discussion is about whether accidents themselves have a nature, rather than about their perceptibility by touch.
Correction: The passage specifically addresses whether the accidents (akwan) are directly perceived by the sense of touch, not their ontological status.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct view regarding whether accidents are perceived by touch?
A) They are not perceived by touch.
B) They are perceived by touch.
C) Only motion is perceived.
D) It is impossible to know.

Key Takeaway
The verified position is that touch directly perceives the accidents (akwan) of motion, rest, conjunction, and separation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you place your hand on a moving object, do you ever pause to recognize that you are directly perceiving an accident created by Allah in that moment? How could this awareness change your sense of Allah's constant presence and power in the most ordinary actions of your daily life? What one ordinary movement will you pay attention to today to remind yourself that even motion is a direct creation of Allah that He sustains at every instant?
?
If touch directly perceives akwan, then every time you feel something move or rest, you are experiencing a direct sign of Allah's creative act. Does your heart register this as a sign, or do you simply pass over it with heedlessness? What would it take for you to transform your next handshake or step into a moment of dhikr and recognition of the Creator?
?
The verified view is that the sense of touch directly apprehends accidents. This means that when you bump into a chair and feel its resistance (rest), you are not just sensing a substance but an accident that Allah sustains. How does this truth confront the modern assumption that the physical world is self-sufficient and independent of Allah? Are you willing to let this lesson change how you perceive every physical interaction today?

Division of Speech into Khabar and Insha'
Sanusi defines the two main categories of speech: khabar and insha'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental division of speech into statement (khabar) and construction (insha'), and how each is defined by its relation to truth and falsehood.

Core Idea
Speech divides into two: that which can be true or false, and that which cannot.

Sanusi begins by establishing the basic categories of speech from a logical perspective. Before we can discuss theological statements, we need to know which speech acts are truth-apt. This passage lays the foundational split between khabar and insha'.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening definition
Read the first line: 'Speech divides into statement and construction.' Then read the definitions that follow.
Notice that khabar is defined by its capacity for truth/falsehood 'by its essence'.
2
Identify examples of insha'
The passage lists examples of insha': command, prohibition, etc. Think of your own examples to internalize.
These speech acts are not evaluated as true/false.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this division matters for theology. Kalam is built on statements that can be true or false.
This will connect to later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْكَلامُ : يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى خَبَرٍ ، وَإِنْشَاءِ . فَالْخَبَرُ : مَا يَحْتَمِلُ الصَّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ لِذَاتِهِ وَالإِنْشَاءُ : مَا لا يَحْتَمِلُ صِدْقاً وَلا كَذِباً لِذَاتِهِ . أنَّ كل كلام - وهو ما أفاد نسبةً مقصودة لذاتها ـ فهو منحصر في قسمين ؛ وهما : الخبرُ ، والإنشاء . [ تعريف الخبر ] • فالخبرُ : هو الكلام الذي يقبلُ الصدق والكذبَ لأجل ذاتِهِ ؛ أي : لأجل حقيقتِهِ ، مِنْ غير نظر إلى المخبر والمادة التي تعلّق بها الكلام ; كأن تكونَ مِنَ الأمور الضروريَّةِ التي لا يقبلُ إثباتها إلا الصدق ولا يقبل نفيها إلا الكذب . فخرج بالقيدِ الأَوَّلِ - وهو احتمالُ الصدق والكذب - الإنشاءاتُ ; كالأمر ، والنهي ، والاستفهام، والتمنِّي ، والعَرْضِ ، والتحضيض، والنداء . ودخل في الخبرِ بسبب تقييدِ احتمالِ الصدق والكذب بالذاتِ ثلاثة أقسام : الأول : ما يحتمل الصدق والكذب مطلقاً ؛ أي : يقبلُهُما بالنظر إلى حقيقة ذلكَ الكلام ، وبالنظر إلى زائد عليه ؛ وهو المخبِرُ بهِ ، والمعنى المخبر به . ومثاله : قول قائل غيرِ معصوم مِنَ الكذب : فلانٌ مِنْ أَهْلِ الجنَّةِ ، وفلانٌ مِنْ أَهلِ النارِ ، ونحو ذلكَ ; فإنَّ هذا الكلام يحتمل الصدق والكذب مطلقاً ، سواءٌ نظرنا إلى صورة نسبتِهِ ، أو إلى مادَّتِهِ ومعناه . فقط . القسم الثاني : ما يحتمل الصدق والكذبَ بالنظر إلى صورة نسبتِهِ مع قطع النظرِ إلى زائد على ذلكَ ، أمَّا إذا نظرنا إلى زائد على صورةِ نسبتِهِ فإنَّهُ ينتفي عنه الاحتمال ، ويتحتّم لهُ الصدقُ بلا ارتياب . ومثال ذلك : إخبارُ مولانا جل وعزّ ، وإخبارُ رُسُلِهِ عليهم الصلاةُ والسلام ; كقولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ إِنَّ الْمُتَّقِينَ فِي جَنَّتِ وَنَهَرٍ ﴾ [القمر : ٥٤] ، وقولِهِ جل وعلا: وَالسَبِقُونَ السَّبِقُونَ * أُوْلَيْكَ الْمُقَرَّبُونَ ﴾ [الواقعة : ١٠-١١] ، وقوله سبحانه : وَنَادَى أَصْحَبُ الْجَنَّةِ أَصْحَابَ النَّارِ ... [الأعراف : ٤٤] الآية ، ونحو ذلكَ مِنْ سائر أخباره تبارك وتعالى ، ومثل قوله عليه .
And speech (al-kalam) divides into statement (khabar) and construction (insha'). So the statement (khabar) is that which admits truth (sidq) and falsehood (kidhb) by its essence (li-dhatihi), and the construction (insha') is that which admits neither truth nor falsehood by its essence. That every speech – and it is what conveys a relation intended for itself – is confined to two divisions: the statement (khabar) and the construction (insha'). [Definition of the statement] So the statement (khabar) is speech that accepts truth and falsehood for its own sake (li-ajli dhatihi); i.e., for the sake of its reality (haqiqatihi), without regard to the speaker (al-mukhbit) or the material matter (al-madda) to which the speech is attached; such as being among necessary matters whose affirmation accepts only truth and whose negation accepts only falsehood. So by the first condition – which is the probability (ihtimal) of truth and falsehood – the constructions (insha'at) are excluded: such as command, prohibition, interrogation, wish, offer, urging, and call. And included in the statement (khabar), because of restricting the probability of truth and falsehood to the essence (dhat), are three divisions: The first: that which admits truth and falsehood absolutely (mutlaqan); i.e., it accepts them with regard to the reality of that speech and with regard to something additional to it: that which is informed of (al-mukhbar bihi) and the meaning informed of (al-ma'na al-mukhbar bihi). Its example: the saying of one not infallible from lying: 'So-and-so is from the people of paradise, and so-and-so is from the people of the fire,' and the like; for this speech admits truth and falsehood absolutely, whether we look at the form of its relation (surat nisbatihi) or at its matter (maddatihi) and its meaning. Only. The second division: that which admits truth and falsehood with regard to the form of its relation while disregarding something additional to that; but if we look at something additional to the form of its relation, then probability is removed from it, and truth is necessarily established for it without doubt. Its example: the informing of our Lord, Glorious and Exalted, and the informing of His messengers, peace be upon them; such as His saying, the Exalted: 'Indeed the righteous will be in gardens and rivers' [al-Qamar 54], and His saying, Glorious and Exalted: 'And the foremost, the foremost, those are the ones brought near' [al-Waqi'a 10-11], and His saying, the Exalted: 'And the companions of Paradise call to the companions of the Fire...' [al-A'raf 44] the verse, and the like from the rest of His reports, Blessed and Exalted, and like his saying, peace be upon him.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of speech (al-kalam) into two main types: statement (khabar) and construction (insha'), followed by a detailed breakdown of khabar into three divisions. The author begins by stating that speech divides into khabar and insha'. Khabar is defined as that which by its very essence admits the possibility of being true or false, whereas insha' does not admit such a possibility essentially. This is the core logical distinction. The author then reiterates that every speech that conveys a relation intended for itself is confined to these two divisions. Next, a more precise definition of khabar is given: it is speech that accepts truth and falsehood for the sake of its own essence (haqiqa), without considering external factors like the speaker or the material subject matter. The author gives an example: necessary propositions that can only be true in affirmation or false in negation. The first condition—probability of truth and falsehood—excludes insha' statements, which include commands, prohibitions, questions, wishes, offers, urging, and calls. These are not truth-apt inherently. Then, by restricting the probability to the essence, khabar is divided into three types. The first type is that which admits truth and falsehood absolutely, considering both the form of the proposition and additional factors (the subject matter and the meaning). An example is a statement by a non-infallible person about someone's fate in the afterlife; this statement can be true or false from all perspectives. The second type admits truth and falsehood when considering only the form of the relation, but when additional factors (like the speaker's infallibility) are considered, probability is removed and truth is necessitated. An example is reports from Allah and His messengers, which, by virtue of the speaker's veracity, are necessarily true. The passage cuts off before the third type is given, but the pattern suggests a third type that admits falsehood necessarily due to external factors.

Key Terms
كلام (kalam)
خبر (khabar)
إنشاء (insha')
صدق (sidq)
كذب (kidhb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any speech can be true or false regardless of type.
Correction: The passage clarifies that constructions like commands are not truth-apt; they are evaluated differently.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of insha' according to the passage?
A) 'The sky is blue.'
B) 'Do not steal.'
C) 'Water boils at 100°C.'
D) 'The earth orbits the sun.'

Key Takeaway
Speech is divided into two categories: khabar, which can be true or false, and insha', which cannot; this distinction is essential for theological reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a claim about your faith—like 'Allah is merciful'—do you treat it as a khabar that must be true in reality, or just a personal expression? How does this lesson challenge you to be more careful with such statements?
?
Think of a command from the Quran you often recite, like 'Establish prayer.' According to this lesson, it is insha', not khabar. How does that shift your understanding of your responsibility toward it from merely 'knowing' to 'doing'?
?
Have you ever doubted a creedal statement because you treated it as if it were just an insha' expression of tradition rather than a khabar that must be objectively true? What would change if you fully embraced its truth-aptness?

Logic and Technical Terms

The Three Types of Khabar
Sanusi enumerates three types of khabar based on how they admit truth and falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three divisions of khabar based on how they admit truth and falsehood, focusing on the first two types exemplified in the passage.

Core Idea
Khabar can be true/false absolutely, or only conditionally depending on additional factors.

Having defined khabar, Sanusi now refines the classification by considering different perspectives on how a statement can be truth-apt. Some statements are always open to truth and falsehood, while others are true necessarily due to external factors like the speaker's infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the threefold division
Read from 'ودخل في الخبر' to the end of the passage. Identify the three types.
The third type is not fully given but can be inferred.
2
Analyze the first type
The first type admits truth/falsehood absolutely. Example: human statements about unseen matters.
This is the common case.
3
Analyze the second type
The second type admits truth/falsehood in form but becomes necessarily true when considering the speaker's infallibility.
Example: divine reports.
4
Reflect on the third type
Though not fully stated, the pattern suggests a third type that is necessarily false due to external factors.
This completes the logical classification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a statement about the unseen from a human source, do you instinctively treat it as absolutely probable (Type 1), or do you demand evidence to elevate it to conditional certainty? How does this lesson expose your reliance on Allah's word as the only source of necessary truth about the afterlife?
?
You recite verses like 'Indeed, the righteous will be in gardens and rivers' with your tongue, but do you truly internalize them as necessarily true? When a fear of death or doubt creeps in, do your actions reflect that you believe these words are more than just probable? What would it take for you to live as if every divine report is absolutely certain?
?
Consider a report you heard recently about a religious matter from a person you trust. Did you accept it as true without verifying its source? This lesson teaches that human statements are only absolutely probable unless backed by infallible testimony. Will you commit to checking every religious claim against the Quran and authentic sunnah before acting on it?

Logic and Technical Terms

Refining the Definition of Insha' with 'Bi-Dhatihi'
Sanusi refines the definition of insha' by adding the qualification 'bi-dhatihi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the qualification 'bi-dhatihi' is necessary to properly define insha' and exclude certain types of khabar.

Core Idea
A precise definition must capture both inclusiveness and exclusiveness.

In this passage, Sanusi refines the definition of insha' (construct speech) by adding the crucial qualification 'by its essence'. He shows that a simple definition would mistakenly include two types of khabar and exclude insincere commands. Understanding this refinement is key to mastering the logical distinction between insha' and khabar.

Guided Path
1
Examine the examples of insha'
Read the Arabic passage and notice the examples: commands, prohibitions, interrogatives, wishes, and calls. Observe that none of these admit truth or falsity in external reality.
These examples illustrate the core property of insha'.
2
Identify the problem with the simple definition
Sanusi points out that defining insha' simply as 'what does not admit truth or falsehood' would include the necessarily true and necessarily false types of khabar. Understand why these types appear to fit the simple definition.
3
Learn how 'bi-dhatihi' solves inclusiveness
The addition 'by its essence' excludes those two khabar types because they do admit truth/falsity when considered in themselves. See how this rectifies the definition's inclusiveness (al-ṭard).
4
See how 'bi-dhatihi' solves exclusiveness
Sanusi gives the example of an insincere command: it can be called true/false based on implication, but in its own essence it is insha'. Without the qualification, such a command would be excluded, making the definition defective in exclusiveness (al-ʿaks).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثاله : الأوامر ؛ نحو : قُمْ ، واقعد والنواهي ؛ نحو : لا تقم ، ولا تقعد ، وكقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَلَا نَقْرَبُوا الزِّنَى ﴾ [الإسراء : ٣٢] ، وقولِهِ عزَّ وجلَّ : ﴿ وَلَا نَقْرَبُوا الْفَوَاحِشَ ﴾ [الأنعام : ١٥١]، ﴿ وَلَا نَقْرَبُواْ مَالَ الْيَتِيمِ ﴾ [الأنعام : ١٥٢] ، ﴿ وَلَا تَقْتُلُوا النَّفْسَ الَّتِي حَرَّمَ اللهُ إِلَّا بِالْحَقِّ ﴾ [الأنعام : ١٥١] ، ونحو ذلك مما هو كثير . والاستفهام ؛ كقولِكَ : هل قام زيدٌ ؟ وقوله تعالى : ﴿ مَاذَا قَالَ رَبُّكُمْ قَالُوا الْحَقَّ ﴾ [سبا : ٢٣] . والتمنّي ؛ كقولك : ليت زيداً قائمٌ ، وقوله تعالى إخباراً عن المنافقين : ﴿ يَلَيْتَنِي كُنتُ مَعَهُمْ فَأَفُوزَ فَوْزًا عَظِيمًا ﴾ [النساء : ٧٣] . والنداء ؛ كقولِكَ : يا زيد ، وقولِهِ تعالى إخباراً عن أهل النارِ : ﴿ يَمَلِكُ ﴾ [الزخرف : ٧٧] ونحوه . فإنَّ هذه الأمثلة كلَّها لا تحتمل صدقاً ولا كذباً ؛ لأنَّها لم تحكم بوقوع شيء في الخارج ولا بعدمِ وقوعِهِ ، ولهذا لا يحسنُ أَنْ يُقالَ للمتكلّم بها : صدقت ، ولا : كذبت وإنَّما زدنا أيضاً في تعريف الإنشاء التقييد بقولنا : ( لذاتِهِ ) ليخرج منه القسمان الآخرانِ مِنْ أقسام الخبرِ الثلاثة التي ذكرناها في تعريف الخبر ؛ فإنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ منهما لا يحتمل الصدق والكذبَ ، بل يتحتّم في الأوَّلِ منهما الصدقُ لا غيرُ ، وفي الثاني الكذب لا غير ، فلوِ اقتصرنا في تعريف الإنشاء على قولنا : ( وهو ما لا يحتمل صدقاً ولا كذباً ) .. لدخلَ فيهِ ذانِكَ القسمانِ مِنْ أقسام الخبرِ ، ويكونُ التعريفُ حينئذٍ فاسد الطردِ (١) ، فلما زدنا في تعريف الإنشاء تقييد نفي احتمالِ الصدق والكذب بالذاتِ .. خرجَ منه ذانِكَ القسمانِ ؛ لأنَّهما يحتملان الصدق والكذبَ بالنظر إلى ذاتِهما ، فهما إذا خبر لا إنشاء . ويدخلُ أيضاً في الإنشاء بسبب هذا التقييد : الأمر لشخص بأكل الطعام مثلاً إذا كانَ الآمرُ يحتمل ألا يريدَ مِنَ المأمور أكُلاً ، أو ليسَ عندَهُ ما يُؤكَلُ أصلاً ، وإِنَّما صدرَ منهُ الأمرُ بالأكل بمجرَّدِ رياءِ ونحوِه ؛ فإنَّ هذا الأمر يحتمل الصدق والكذبَ باعتبارِ ما دلّ عليهِ عُرْفاً ؛ مِنَ الإخبار بإرادة أكل المأمورِ بهِ والحب فيه والتمكن منه ، ولهذا كثيراً ما يُقالُ لَمَنْ فُهِمَ منهُ مجرَّدُ الرياء في هذا الأمرِ : كذبْتَ ، ويُقالُ لَمَنْ فُهِمَ منه خلوص المودَّةِ والمحبَّةِ فيما أمرَ بهِ : صدقت ، ولا يحتمل هذا الأمر صدقاً ولا كذباً مِنْ حيثُ ذاتُهُ وحقيقتُهُ الطلبية فلولا زيادة التقييد بالذاتِ في تعريف الإنشاء لخرجَ هذا الأمرُ ونحوهُ مِنَ الإنشاءاتِ المحتملة للصدق والكذب باعتبار لوازمها الخبرية ، ويكون التعريفُ حينئذٍ فاسد العكس ، فقد أصلحت هذهِ الزيادة طرْدَ التعريف وعكسه في الإنشاء والخبر ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق . (١) يعني : لا يكون مانعاً من دخول غير أفراد المحدود
And its example: commands, like 'Stand' and 'Sit'; and prohibitions, like 'Do not stand' and 'Do not sit', and like His statement, the Most High: 'And do not approach zina' [al-Isra 32], and His statement, Mighty and Majestic: 'And do not approach immoralities' [al-An'am 151], 'And do not approach the property of the orphan' [al-An'am 152], 'And do not kill the soul which God has forbidden except by right' [al-An'am 151], and the like of that which is many. And interrogation, like your saying: 'Did Zaid stand?' and His statement, the Most High: 'What did your Lord say? They said the truth' [Saba 23]. And wishing, like your saying: 'Would that Zaid were standing', and His statement, the Most High, informing about the hypocrites: 'Oh, would that I had been with them, so I would have achieved a great success' [al-Nisa 73]. And calling, like your saying: 'O Zaid', and His statement, the Most High, informing about the people of the Fire: 'O Malik' [al-Zukhruf 77] and the like. For all these examples do not admit truth nor falsehood; because they did not judge the occurrence of something in external reality nor its non-occurrence, and for this reason it is not appropriate to say to the speaker of them: 'You have told the truth', nor 'You have lied'. And we have also added in the definition of insha' the restriction by our saying: 'by its essence' in order to exclude the other two sections from the three sections of khabar which we mentioned in the definition of khabar; for each of them does not admit truth and falsehood, rather in the first of them truth is necessitated and nothing else, and in the second falsehood is necessitated and nothing else. So if we confined ourselves in the definition of insha' to our saying: 'It is what does not admit truth nor falsehood'... then those two sections from the sections of khabar would enter into it, and the definition would then be invalid in its inclusiveness (fāsid al-ṭard). So when we added in the definition of insha' the restriction of negating the possibility of truth and falsehood by essence... those two sections exited from it; because they admit truth and falsehood with respect to their own essence, so they are khabar, not insha'. And there also enters into insha' due to this restriction: the command to a person to eat food, for example, if the commander possibly does not intend from the commanded an eating, or he does not have what can be eaten at all, but his command to eat issued merely out of hypocrisy and the like; for this command admits truth and falsehood by virtue of what it conventionally indicates—of informing about the intention of eating the commanded thing, desire for it, and ability to do it. For this reason, it is often said to one from whom mere hypocrisy in this command is understood: 'You have lied', and it is said to one from whom sincerity of affection and love in what he commanded is understood: 'You have told the truth', yet this command does not admit truth nor falsehood in terms of its own essence and its requestive reality. So were it not for the addition of the restriction by essence in the definition of insha', this command and its like would exit from insha' while being capable of truth and falsehood by virtue of their informative implications, and the definition would then be invalid in its exclusiveness (fāsid al-ʿaks). So this addition has rectified the inclusiveness and exclusiveness of the definition for both insha' and khabar. And with God, the Most High, is success. (1) Meaning: it does not prevent the entry of non-individuals of the defined.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a logical refinement of the definition of insha' (construct speech), distinguishing it from khabar (informative speech). The author first provides examples of insha': commands, prohibitions, interrogatives, wishes, and calls, showing that none of these bear truth or falsity because they do not assert something about external reality. Then he identifies a problem: a simple definition of insha' as 'what does not admit truth or falsehood' would inadvertently include two special types of khabar (the necessarily true and the necessarily false) that also appear not to admit truth/falsity in their external judgment. To exclude these, he adds the qualification 'bi-dhatihi' (by its own essence), meaning that insha' does not admit truth/falsity by its very nature, whereas those other types do admit them when considered in themselves. He further illustrates with a command issued insincerely (out of hypocrisy); such a command can be called 'truthful' or 'false' based on the speaker's inner state, which is an informative implication, but in its own essence as a request, it does not bear truth/falsity. Without the 'by essence' restriction, this command would be excluded from insha' even though it is truly insha'. Thus the qualification ensures both inclusiveness (all insha' are covered) and exclusiveness (no non-insha' enters). The author concludes that this addition perfects the definition.

Key Terms
إنشاء
خبر
الصدق والكذب
بالذات
طرد
عكس
فاسد الطرد
فاسد العكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All commands are pure insha' with no implication of truth or falsity.
Correction: Commands can imply an informative element (e.g., sincerity) and thus may be evaluated as true or false in that sense, yet they remain insha' in their essence as requests.

Checkpoint
Why did Sanusi add the qualification 'bi-dhatihi' to the definition of insha'?
A) To include commands that are insincere.
B) To exclude the necessarily true and necessarily false types of khabar from the definition.
C) To make the definition include interrogatives.
D) To emphasize that insha' can be true or false.

Key Takeaway
The qualification 'bi-dhatihi' ensures that insha' is defined by its own essence, not by external implications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you give a command—whether to a child, a student, or a colleague—do you consider the truthfulness of your own intention? Are there times your command is like the hypocritical example, issued without genuine care? How can the 'bi-dhatihi' qualifier remind you to examine the essence of your speech?
?
Your words carry both a literal meaning and an inner reality. Are there areas in your life where your speech (like a command or a wish) seems righteous on the outside but lacks sincerity at its core? What would it mean for you to bring your words into alignment with their essence before Allah?

Mu'tazilite Definitions of Truth and Falsehood
Sanusi presents two Mu'tazilite definitions of sidq and kidhb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite definitions of truth and falsehood as proposed by al-Nazzam and al-Jahiz, and why al-Nazzam's definition is considered corrupt.

Core Idea
Truth is not merely subjective belief; it must correspond to reality.

The previous discussion on the verse about hypocrites led Sanusi to expose a flaw in the Mu'tazilite al-Nazzam's argument. Here, he presents al-Nazzam's definition of truth and falsehood, which severs truth from objective reality, and contrasts it with al-Jahiz's more balanced definition. This sets the stage for the orthodox position that truth requires correspondence to reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that Sanusi is presenting two Mu'tazilite definitions, not his own view.
The phrase 'wa bihādhā ta'rifu fasād...' signals that the author is using the previous analysis to critique al-Nazzam.
2
Compare the two definitions
Al-Nazzam defines truth as correspondence to belief only, ignoring reality. Al-Jahiz requires correspondence to both reality and belief. Identify the key difference.
Notice how al-Jahiz includes 'ma'a al-i'tiqād li-dhālika' (along with belief in that).
3
Understand the critique
Sanusi states that al-Nazzam's reliance on the verse is corrupt because his definition ignores objective reality. Reflect on why this is problematic for a coherent understanding of truth.
This critique is implied by the phrase 'fasād i'timād al-Nazzām'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا تعرف فساد اعتماد النظام المعتزلي على هذه الآية؛ فيما ذهب إليه: أن الصدق: عبارة عن مطابقة الخبر لاعتقاد المخبر وافق ما في نفس الأمر أم لا، والكذب: عدم مطابقة الخبر لاعتقاد المخبر، خالف ما في نفس الأمر أم لا. وذهب الجاحظ من المعتزلة: إلى أن الصدق: مطابقة الخبر في نفس الأمر مع الاعتقاد لذلك، والكذب: عدم مطابقة الخبر لما في نفس الأمر مع الاعتقاد لذلك.
And by this you know the corruption of the reliance of al-Nazzam the Mu'tazilite on this verse; in what he went to: that truth (sidq) is an expression of correspondence of the report to the belief of the informer, whether it agrees with what is in reality or not, and falsehood (kidhb) is the lack of correspondence of the report to the belief of the informer, whether it contradicts what is in reality or not. And al-Jahiz from the Mu'tazilites went to: that truth is correspondence of the report to what is in reality along with belief in that, and falsehood is the lack of correspondence of the report to what is in reality along with belief in that.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents the definitions of truth and falsehood according to two prominent Mu'tazilite scholars, al-Nazzam and al-Jahiz, after the author uses the previous analysis of the hypocrites' verse to show the corruption of al-Nazzam's reliance on it. Al-Nazzam defines truth (sidq) as the report's correspondence to the speaker's belief (i'tiqād), regardless of whether it matches external reality. Similarly, falsehood (kidhb) is simply non-correspondence to belief, regardless of reality. This definition severs truth from objective reality, making it purely subjective. Al-Jahiz offers a more balanced definition: truth requires correspondence to both external reality (nafs al-amr) and the speaker's belief in that reality. Falsehood is the failure to correspond to reality while also requiring belief in that reality (i.e., the speaker believes something false). The author implies that al-Jahiz's definition is closer to the orthodox view, which maintains that truth involves correspondence to reality, and that falsehood is a mismatch with reality regardless of belief. The passage thus contrasts two Mu'tazilite positions and sets up the orthodox critique that al-Nazzam's definition is corrupt.

Key Terms
صدق
كذب
مطابقة
اعتقاد
نفس الأمر
النظام
الجاحظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Nazzam's definition that truth is only correspondence to belief is acceptable.
Correction: Al-Nazzam's definition is corrupt because it ignores objective reality; truth must ultimately correspond to reality, not just belief.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jahiz, what is required for a statement to be true (sidq)?
A) The report must match the speaker's belief only.
B) The report must match reality only.
C) The report must match both reality and the speaker's belief.
D) The report must match the speaker's intention.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nazzam's definition of truth as mere correspondence to belief is corrupt because it severs truth from objective reality; a sound definition requires correspondence to reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever justify a lie by telling yourself that you 'meant well' or that your intention was good? How does al-Nazzam's subjective definition of truth expose the danger of such thinking, and what would it mean for you to commit to speaking only what corresponds to reality, even when it is difficult?
?
Think of a time when you believed something false but spoke as if it were true because you had convinced yourself of it. In light of al-Jahiz's definition, what does that reveal about your integrity? What step will you take this week to align your words with reality, not just your feelings?
?
Al-Nazzam's definition severs truth from reality. Are there areas of your life—such as politics, relationships, or self-talk—where you prefer a 'truth' that matches your beliefs rather than reality? What would it cost you to surrender that comfort and seek the truth of the matter?

Al-Nazzam's Definition of Truth and Falsehood
Sanusi presents al-Nazzam's definition of truth and falsehood and criticizes his use of a Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's Mu'tazili definition of truth as correspondence to belief, and why Sanusi considers it corrupt.

Core Idea
Truth is what the speaker believes, regardless of reality.

In this passage, Sanusi critiques al-Nazzam's reliance on a Quranic verse to support his definition of truth. Al-Nazzam defines truth as correspondence to the speaker's belief, ignoring external reality. This radically subjective view has significant theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-Nazzam
Read the Arabic text and translation that present al-Nazzam's definition and Sanusi's critique.
Note that Sanusi calls his reliance 'corrupt'.
2
Identify the key components of his definition
Look for the terms 'mutabaqa' and 'i'tiqad'. Understand that truth is only about belief, not reality.
He does not consider nafs al-amr.
3
Understand Sanusi's objection
Sanusi argues that al-Nazzam's use of the verse is invalid because the verse uses 'lie' figuratively.
This shows the importance of proper scriptural interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحتمل أن يكونَ تُجوِّزَ بالكذب ؛ فأُطلق على غلطهم باستعمالهم كلمة الشهادةِ التي وُضِعَتْ لغةً للمعلوم المحقق في غير موضوعها(١) ؛ (٢) وهو ما ليس بمعلوم ولا محقق في قلوبهم (٢) . ١ أقوال للمعتزلة في حد الصدق والكذب وبهذا تعرفُ فساد اعتمادِ النظام المعتزلي على هذه الآية ؛ فيما ذهَبَ إِليهِ : أَنَّ الصدق : عبارةٌ عن مطابقة الخبر لاعتقادِ المُخبِرِ وافق ما في نفس الأمرِ أم لا ، والكذبَ : عدم مطابقة الخبر لاعتقادِ المُخبر ، خالف ما في نفس الأمر أم لا(٣) لما ٣٣ وذهب الجاحظُ مِنَ المعتزلة : إلى أنَّ الصدق : مطابقةُ الخبر في نفس الأمرِ معَ الاعتقادِ لذلكَ ، والكذبَ : عدم مطابقة الخبر لما في نفس الأمر مع الاعتقادِ لذلك (١) ، فشرطَ في كلٌّ مِنَ الصدقِ والكذب شرطين ، ومهما انتفيا أو أحدهما . . كان الخبر واسطة ؛ لا يوصفُ بالصدقِ ولا بالكذب ، فأقسام الخبر عنده سنةٌ : واحدٌ صدق : وهو المطابق للاعتقادِ وما في نفس الأمرِ وواحد كذبٌ : وهو المخالف لما في نفس الأمر وللاعتقادِ . وأربعةٌ واسطةٌ : وهو المطابق لما في نفس الأمر مع اعتقادِ خلافِ ذلك ، والمطابق لما في نفس الأمرِ معَ الشكٍّ في ذلك ، والمخالفُ لما في نفس الأمر مع اعتقادِ مطابقتِهِ لهُ ، والمخالف لما في نفس الأمر مع الشك في ذلك . وشبهته في ذلك وردُّها معلوم في فنّ الأصول والبيان (۳) . (١) انظر ( البحر المحيط » للزركشي ( ٤ / ۲۲۲ ) ، وقال : ( وكأنه أجرى الصدق مجرى العلم ، فكما أن العلم يتعلّق بالشيء على ما هو به من جهة صحته . أنه فكذلك الخبر ، ويجوز راعی أصله الفاسد في التحسين والتقبيح ؛ فراعى في كونه صدقاً وقوعه حسناً ؛ لمفارقة الصدق والكذب في حسن أحدهما وقبح الآخر ، ولا يكون الخبر حسناً إلا مع المخبر بحال المخبر عنه ؛ لأن تجويزه على خلاف ما أخبر يقتضي قبحه ، ونحن قد بيَّنا أن الصدق قد يقبح ، فلا يجب أن يكون كونه صدقاً علةً لحُسْنه ، ككونه كذباً علة لقبحه ، بل لو كان كونه صدقاً علة تقتضي الحسن .. لكان الحسن إنما ثبت إذا انتفت وجوه القبح ) واعلم : أن الجاحظ ينظر إلى النِّسَبِ الثلاث : ( اللفظ ، والاعتقاد ، والخارج ) ، أما النظام فإنما ينظر فقط إلى نسبتين : ( اللفظ ، والاعتقاد ) . (٢) انظر ( البحر المحيط ) ( ٤ / ٢١٨ ) ، و ( الإيضاح » ( ٥٩/١ )
And it is probable that [the word] 'lie' was used figuratively; so it was applied to their error in using the word 'shahada' which was linguistically placed for certain known [truth] in other than its proper context (1); (2) and that is what is not known nor certain in their hearts (2). [Section heading: Statements of the Mu'tazila on the definition of truth and falsehood] And by this you know the corruption of al-Nazzam al-Mu'tazili's reliance on this verse, in what he held: that truth (sidq) is an expression of correspondence (mutabaqa) of the report to the belief of the informant, whether it agrees with reality or not, and falsehood (kidhb) is lack of correspondence of the report to the belief of the informant, whether it contradicts reality or not (3). And al-Jahiz from the Mu'tazila held: that truth is correspondence of the report to reality together with belief in that, and falsehood is lack of correspondence of the report to reality together with belief in that (1). So he required in each of truth and falsehood two conditions, and whenever both or one of them is absent, the report is intermediate (wasita); it is not described as true nor as false. So the divisions of the report according to him are six: one true: it is that which corresponds to belief and to reality; and one false: it is that which contradicts reality and belief. And four intermediate: that which corresponds to reality while believing the opposite; that which corresponds to reality while doubting that; that which contradicts reality while believing it corresponds; and that which contradicts reality while doubting that. And his argument and its refutation are known in the science of usul and bayan (3). (1) See al-Bahr al-Muhit by al-Zarkashi (4/222): 'And it is as if he made truth analogous to knowledge, for just as knowledge pertains to a thing as it is from the perspective of its correctness, so too the report; and he considered his corrupt principle in tahsin and taqbih; so he considered in its being true that it occurs as good; due to the difference between truth and falsehood in the goodness of one and the ugliness of the other; and a report is not good except with the informant understanding the state of the informed-about; because permitting its opposite to what he reported necessitates its ugliness. And we have shown that truth may be ugly, so it is not necessary that its being truth be a cause for its goodness, just as its being falsehood is a cause for its ugliness. Rather, if its being truth were a cause requiring goodness, then goodness would only be established when the aspects of ugliness are absent.' And know that al-Jahiz considers the three relations: (utterance, belief, and external reality), while al-Nazzam only considers two relations: (utterance and belief). (2) See al-Bahr al-Muhit (4/218) and al-Idah (1/59).
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage discusses two Mu'tazili positions on the definition of truth (sidq) and falsehood (kidhb) of reports (akhbar). It first notes a possible figurative interpretation of the word 'lie' in a Quranic verse. The main content begins with al-Nazzam's definition: truth is correspondence of the report to the speaker's belief, regardless of external reality; falsehood is lack of that correspondence. Then it presents al-Jahiz's view: truth requires both correspondence to reality and correspondence to belief; falsehood requires both lack of correspondence to reality and correspondence to belief (i.e., the speaker believes his false report). Al-Jahiz thus adds a condition from reality, making his definition more complex. Consequently, he divides reports into six types: one true (matches both), one false (matches neither), and four intermediate cases (where there is mismatch between reality and belief). The text notes that al-Jahiz's argument and its refutation are known in uṣūl al-fiqh and rhetoric. The footnote clarifies that al-Jahiz considers three relations (utterance, belief, external reality) while al-Nazzam considers only two. This illustrates a key theological debate about the nature of truth and the role of objective reality.

Key Terms
صدق (sidq)
كذب (kidhb)
مطابقة (mutabaqa)
اعتقاد (i'tiqad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think truth is simply correspondence to reality; al-Nazzam defines it as correspondence to belief.
Correction: He defines truth relative to the speaker's belief, not reality.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nazzam, what makes a report true?
A) Correspondence to reality
B) Correspondence to the speaker's belief
C) Correspondence to both belief and reality
D) Correspondence to the listener's belief

Key Takeaway
Al-Nazzam defines truth as correspondence to the speaker's belief alone, ignoring objective reality, which Sanusi finds corrupt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because you sincerely believe something, it must be true? How does al-Nazzam's definition challenge your own tendency to equate sincerity with truth, and what might be the danger of that assumption in matters of faith?
?
When you read the Quran, do you sometimes interpret it according to what you already believe, rather than letting it challenge your beliefs? How does this lesson expose the corruption of making your own belief the measure of truth?
?
If truth were only about what you believe, then falsehoods could never be corrected. Is there a belief you hold that you have never examined against objective reality (the Quran and Sunnah)? What would it cost you to honestly test it?

Al-Jahiz's Definition and the Six Types of Reports
Sanusi presents al-Jahiz's definition of truth and falsehood and his sixfold classification of reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jahiz's definition of truth and falsehood, his two conditions, and his classification of reports into six types.

Core Idea
Truth requires both correspondence to reality and to belief; otherwise, the report is intermediate.

Al-Jahiz offers a more complex definition than al-Nazzam. He requires that a true report correspond to both external reality and the speaker's belief, while a false report contradicts both. This leads to four intermediate cases where only one condition is met or there is doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Jahiz's definition
Read the Arabic and translation for al-Jahiz's position. Note the two conditions for truth and falsehood.
He uses the term 'wasita' for intermediate.
2
Understand the six types
Al-Jahiz divides reports into six: one true, one false, and four intermediate. Identify each case.
The intermediate cases arise when there is mismatch between belief and reality.
3
Consider the significance
Think about how this definition impacts the evaluation of riwayah and shahada. Al-Jahiz's view is more objective but also more complex.
Sanusi notes that his argument and refutation are known in usul and bayan.

ويحتمل أن يكونَ تُجوِّزَ بالكذب ؛ فأُطلق على غلطهم باستعمالهم كلمة الشهادةِ التي وُضِعَتْ لغةً للمعلوم المحقق في غير موضوعها(١) ؛ (٢) وهو ما ليس بمعلوم ولا محقق في قلوبهم (٢) . ١ أقوال للمعتزلة في حد الصدق والكذب وبهذا تعرفُ فساد اعتمادِ النظام المعتزلي على هذه الآية ؛ فيما ذهَبَ إِليهِ : أَنَّ الصدق : عبارةٌ عن مطابقة الخبر لاعتقادِ المُخبِرِ وافق ما في نفس الأمرِ أم لا ، والكذبَ : عدم مطابقة الخبر لاعتقادِ المُخبر ، خالف ما في نفس الأمر أم لا(٣) لما ٣٣ وذهب الجاحظُ مِنَ المعتزلة : إلى أنَّ الصدق : مطابقةُ الخبر في نفس الأمرِ معَ الاعتقادِ لذلكَ ، والكذبَ : عدم مطابقة الخبر لما في نفس الأمر مع الاعتقادِ لذلك (١) ، فشرطَ في كلٌّ مِنَ الصدقِ والكذب شرطين ، ومهما انتفيا أو أحدهما . . كان الخبر واسطة ؛ لا يوصفُ بالصدقِ ولا بالكذب ، فأقسام الخبر عنده سنةٌ : واحدٌ صدق : وهو المطابق للاعتقادِ وما في نفس الأمرِ وواحد كذبٌ : وهو المخالف لما في نفس الأمر وللاعتقادِ . وأربعةٌ واسطةٌ : وهو المطابق لما في نفس الأمر مع اعتقادِ خلافِ ذلك ، والمطابق لما في نفس الأمرِ معَ الشكٍّ في ذلك ، والمخالفُ لما في نفس الأمر مع اعتقادِ مطابقتِهِ لهُ ، والمخالف لما في نفس الأمر مع الشك في ذلك . وشبهته في ذلك وردُّها معلوم في فنّ الأصول والبيان (۳) . (١) انظر ( البحر المحيط » للزركشي ( ٤ / ۲۲۲ ) ، وقال : ( وكأنه أجرى الصدق مجرى العلم ، فكما أن العلم يتعلّق بالشيء على ما هو به من جهة صحته . أنه فكذلك الخبر ، ويجوز راعی أصله الفاسد في التحسين والتقبيح ؛ فراعى في كونه صدقاً وقوعه حسناً ؛ لمفارقة الصدق والكذب في حسن أحدهما وقبح الآخر ، ولا يكون الخبر حسناً إلا مع المخبر بحال المخبر عنه ؛ لأن تجويزه على خلاف ما أخبر يقتضي قبحه ، ونحن قد بيَّنا أن الصدق قد يقبح ، فلا يجب أن يكون كونه صدقاً علةً لحُسْنه ، ككونه كذباً علة لقبحه ، بل لو كان كونه صدقاً علة تقتضي الحسن .. لكان الحسن إنما ثبت إذا انتفت وجوه القبح ) واعلم : أن الجاحظ ينظر إلى النِّسَبِ الثلاث : ( اللفظ ، والاعتقاد ، والخارج ) ، أما النظام فإنما ينظر فقط إلى نسبتين : ( اللفظ ، والاعتقاد ) . (٢) انظر ( البحر المحيط ) ( ٤ / ٢١٨ ) ، و ( الإيضاح » ( ٥٩/١ )
And it is probable that [the word] 'lie' was used figuratively; so it was applied to their error in using the word 'shahada' which was linguistically placed for certain known [truth] in other than its proper context (1); (2) and that is what is not known nor certain in their hearts (2). [Section heading: Statements of the Mu'tazila on the definition of truth and falsehood] And by this you know the corruption of al-Nazzam al-Mu'tazili's reliance on this verse, in what he held: that truth (sidq) is an expression of correspondence (mutabaqa) of the report to the belief of the informant, whether it agrees with reality or not, and falsehood (kidhb) is lack of correspondence of the report to the belief of the informant, whether it contradicts reality or not (3). And al-Jahiz from the Mu'tazila held: that truth is correspondence of the report to reality together with belief in that, and falsehood is lack of correspondence of the report to reality together with belief in that (1). So he required in each of truth and falsehood two conditions, and whenever both or one of them is absent, the report is intermediate (wasita); it is not described as true nor as false. So the divisions of the report according to him are six: one true: it is that which corresponds to belief and to reality; and one false: it is that which contradicts reality and belief. And four intermediate: that which corresponds to reality while believing the opposite; that which corresponds to reality while doubting that; that which contradicts reality while believing it corresponds; and that which contradicts reality while doubting that. And his argument and its refutation are known in the science of usul and bayan (3). (1) See al-Bahr al-Muhit by al-Zarkashi (4/222): 'And it is as if he made truth analogous to knowledge, for just as knowledge pertains to a thing as it is from the perspective of its correctness, so too the report; and he considered his corrupt principle in tahsin and taqbih; so he considered in its being true that it occurs as good; due to the difference between truth and falsehood in the goodness of one and the ugliness of the other; and a report is not good except with the informant understanding the state of the informed-about; because permitting its opposite to what he reported necessitates its ugliness. And we have shown that truth may be ugly, so it is not necessary that its being truth be a cause for its goodness, just as its being falsehood is a cause for its ugliness. Rather, if its being truth were a cause requiring goodness, then goodness would only be established when the aspects of ugliness are absent.' And know that al-Jahiz considers the three relations: (utterance, belief, and external reality), while al-Nazzam only considers two relations: (utterance and belief). (2) See al-Bahr al-Muhit (4/218) and al-Idah (1/59).
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage discusses two Mu'tazili positions on the definition of truth (sidq) and falsehood (kidhb) of reports (akhbar). It first notes a possible figurative interpretation of the word 'lie' in a Quranic verse. The main content begins with al-Nazzam's definition: truth is correspondence of the report to the speaker's belief, regardless of external reality; falsehood is lack of that correspondence. Then it presents al-Jahiz's view: truth requires both correspondence to reality and correspondence to belief; falsehood requires both lack of correspondence to reality and correspondence to belief (i.e., the speaker believes his false report). Al-Jahiz thus adds a condition from reality, making his definition more complex. Consequently, he divides reports into six types: one true (matches both), one false (matches neither), and four intermediate cases (where there is mismatch between reality and belief). The text notes that al-Jahiz's argument and its refutation are known in uṣūl al-fiqh and rhetoric. The footnote clarifies that al-Jahiz considers three relations (utterance, belief, external reality) while al-Nazzam considers only two. This illustrates a key theological debate about the nature of truth and the role of objective reality.

مطابقة (mutabaqa)
واسطة (wasita)
اعتقاد (i'tiqad)
نفس الأمر (nafs al-amr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think truth only requires correspondence to reality; al-Jahiz also requires belief.
Correction: Al-Jahiz requires both correspondence to reality and belief in that correspondence for truth.

Checkpoint
How many intermediate types of report does al-Jahiz recognize?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Al-Jahiz defines truth as correspondence to both reality and belief, leading to six report types: one true, one false, and four intermediate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak, do you always believe what you say is true? Recall a recent conversation where you stated something as fact but were uncertain. According to al-Jahiz, you did not speak truth—you were in an intermediate state. How does that sit with your conscience?
?
The passage teaches that truth requires both correspondence to reality and belief. Have you ever shared a correct fact (true to reality) but without personal conviction, perhaps to fit in? That is not considered truth. What would it take for you to align your speech with your inner belief?
?
Al-Jahiz lists four types of intermediaries—corresponding to reality while believing the opposite, or while doubting, or contradicting reality while believing it corresponds, or while doubting. Which of these do you find yourself falling into most often when discussing your faith? How can you move toward speaking only from a place of full truth and belief?
